THE RED PAGE.
THE TWISTING OF THE HOPE
SINCE the Dun Emer press is devoted to
Ireland, there will be Irish in Aus-
tralia to devote a part of their time to
the publications of the Dun Emer
press. It is placed at Dundrum, and directed
by that Miss Yeats whose name has come to
ring like an Irish pledge. Her workers
are girls and women : they print their
books upon home-made paper and bind
them, all in a comradeship of pleasant in-
dustry. One of these books is Stones of
Red Hanrahan by William Butler Yeats,
who is looked upon to-day as the head
and front of Irish literature’s defending.
All the stories have been printed before,
but the form of them left their author un-
easy, and ‘‘ a friend has helped me to re-
make’’them “nearer to the mind of the
country places.” The remaking is in large
part re-creation that leaves nothing of the
original stuff, and the change is certainly for
better reality. As a proof of the general
iniquity of poets, and the care that must
needs be exercised in dealing with the Irish
ones, we cite some passages telling how a
serviceable trick was played upon the wan-
dering poet Hanrahan.
Hanrahan was walking the roads one time near
Kinvara at the fall of day, and he heard the sound
of a fiddle from a house a little way off the road-
side- He turned up the path to it, for he never
had the habit of passing by any place where there
was music or dancing or good company, without
going in. The man of the house was standing at
the door, and when Hanrahan came near he knew
him, and he said :
“A welcome before you, Hanrahan, you have
been lost to us this long time.” But the woman
of the house came to the door and she said to her
husband: “I would be as well pleased for
Hanrahan not to come in to-night, for he has no
good name now among the priests, or with women
that mind themselves; and I wouldn’t wonder
from his walk if he has a drop of drink taken.”
But the man said: “I will never turn away
Hanrahan of the poets from my doorand with
that he bade him enter...
The fiddler was tuning his fiddle for another
dance, and the man of the house said to the young
men, they would all know what dancing was
when they saw Hanrahan dance, for the like of it
had never been since he was there before.
Hanrahan said he would not dance, he had better
use for his feet now, travelling as he was through
the five provinces of Ireland. Just as he said that
there came m at the half door Oona, the daughter
of the house, having a few bits of bog deal from
Connemara in her arms for the fire. She threw
them on the hearth and the flame rose up, and
showed her to be very comely and smiling, and
two or three of the young men rose up and asked
for a dance. But Hanrahan crossed the floor and
brushed the others away, and said it was with him
she must dance, after the long road he had
travelled before he came to her. And it is likely
he said some soft word in her ear, for she said no-
thing against it, and stood out with him, and
there were little blushes in her cheeks. The other
couples stood up, but when the dance was going
to begin, Hanrahan chanced to look down, and he
took notice of his boots that were worn and
broken, and the ragged grey socks showing
through them ; and he said angrilv it was a bad
floor, and the music no great things, and he sat
down in the dark place beside the hearth. But if
he did, tne girl sat down there with him.
The dancing went on, and when that dance was
over another was called for, and no one took much
notice of Oona and Red Hanrahan for a while, in
the corner where they were. But the mother
grew to be uneasy, and she called to Oona to come
and help her to set the table in the inner room.
But Oona, that had never refused her before, said
she would come soon, but not yet, for she was
listening to whatever he was saying in her ear.
The mother grew yet more uneasy then, and she
would come nearer them, and let on to be stirring
the fire or sweeping the hearth, and she would
listen for a minute to hear what the poet was say-
ing to her child. And one time she heard him
telling about white-handed Deirdre, and how she
brought the sons of Usnach to their death ; and
how the blush in her cheeks was not so red as the
blood of kings’ sons that was shed for her, and her
sorrows had never gone out of mind ; and he
said it was maybe the memory of her that made
the cry of the plover on the bog as sorrowful in
the ear of the poets as the keening of young men
for a comrade. And there would never have been
that memory of her, he said, if it was not for the
poets that had put her lieauty in their songs. And
the next time she did not well understand what he
was saying, but, as far as she could hear, it had
the sound of poetry though it was not rhymed,
and this is what she heard him say: “ The
sun and the moon are the man and the
girl, they are my life and your life, they
are travelling and ever travelling through
the skies as if under the one hood. It was God
made them for one another. He made your life
and my life before the beginning of the world, he
made them that they might go through the world,
up and down, like the two best dancers that go on
with the dance up and down the long floor of the
barn, fresh and laughing, when all the rest are
tired out and leaning against the wall.”
The old woman went then to where her husband
was playing cards, but he took no notice of her,
and then she went to a-woman of the neighbours
and said : “Is there no way we can get them from
one another ? ” and without waiting for an answer
she said to some young men that were talking
together : “ What good are you when you cannot
make the best girl in the house come out and
dance with you ? And go now the whole of you,”
she said, “ and see can you bring her away from
the poet’s talk.” But Oona would not listen to
any of them, but only moved her hand as if to
send-them away. Then they called to Hanrahan
and said he had best dance with the girl himself,
or let her dance with one of them. When Han-
rahan heard what they were saying he said :
“ That is so, I will dance with her, there is no
man in the house must dance with her but myself.”
He stood up with her then, and led her out by
the hand, and some of the young men were vexed,
and some began mocking at his ragged coat, and
his broken boots. But he took no notice, and
Oona took no notice, but they looked at one an-
other as if all the world belonged to themselves
alone. But another couple that had been sitting
together like lovers stood out on the floor at
the same time, holding one another’s hands and
moving their feet to keep time with the music.
But Hanrahan turned nis back on them as if
angry, and in place of dancing he began to sing,
and as he sang he held her hand, and his voice
grew louder, and the mocking of the young men
stopped, and the fiddle stopped, and there was
nothing heard but his voice that had in it the
sound of the wind. And what he sang was a
song lie had heard or had made one time in his
wanderings on Slieve Echtge, and the words of it
as they can be put into English were like this :
I heard under a ragged hollow wood,
A queen-woman dressed out in silver, cry
When the sun looked out of his golden hood,
O that none ever loved but you and 11
O hurry to the water amid the trees,
For there the tall deer and his leman cry
When they have but looked upon their images,
O that none ever loved but you and 11
O hurry to the ragged wood, for there
I will drive out the deer and moon and cry—
O my share of the world, O yellow hair,
No one has ever loved but you and I!
And while he was singing it Oona moved nearer
to him, and the colour had gone from her cheek,
and her eyes were not blue now, but grey with
the tears that were in them, and anyone that
saw her would have thought that she was ready to
follow him there and then from the west to the
east of the world.
But one of the young men called out: “Where
is that country he is singing about ? Mind your-
self, Oona, it is a long way off, you might be a
long time on the road before you would reach to
it.” And another said : “It is not to the Country
of the Young you will be going if you go with
him, but to Mayo of the bogs.” Oona looked at
him then as if she would question him, but he
raised her hand in his hand, and called out
between singing and shouting: “It is very near
us that country is, it is on every side; it may be
on the bare hill behind it is, or it may be in the
heart of the wood.” And he said out very loud
and clear : “In the heart of the wood ; oh, death
will never find us in the heart of the wood. And
will you come with me there, Oona ? ” he, said.
But while he was saying this the two old
women had gone outside the door, and Oona’s
mother was crying, and she said : “He has put
an enchantment on Oona. Can we not get the
men to put him out of the house ? ”
“ That is a thing you cannot do,” said the other
woman, “for he is a poet of the Gael, and you
know well that if you would put a poet of the
Gael out of the house, he would put a curse on
you that would wither the corn in the fields and
dry up the milk of the cows, if it had to hang in
the air seven years.”
“ God help us,” said the mother, “ and why did
I ever let him into the house at all, and the wild
name he has ! ”
It would have been no harm at all to have kept
him outside, but there would great harm come
upon you if you put him out by force. But listen
to the plan I have to get him out of the house by
his own doing, without anyone putting him from
it at all.”
It was not long after that the two women came
in again, each of them having a bundle of hay in
her apron. Hanrahan was not singing now, but
he was talking to Oona very fast and soft, and he
was saying : “ The house is narrow but the world
is wide, and there is no true lover that need be
afraid of night Or morning or sun or stars or
shadows of evening, or any earthly thing.”
“ Hanrahan,” said the mother then, striking him
on the shoulder, “will you give me a hand here
for a minute ?” “Do that, Hanrahan,” said the
woman of the neighbours, “and help us to make
this hay into a rope, for you are ready with your
hands, and a blast of wind has loosened the
thatch on the haystack.”
“ I will do that for you,” said he, and he took
the little stick in his hands, and the mother began
giving out the hay, and he twisting it, but he was
hurrying to have done with it, and to be free
again. The women went on talking and giving
out the hay, and encouraging him, and saying
what a good twister of a rope he was, better than
their own neighbours or than anyone they had
ever seen. And Hanrahan saw that Oona was
watching him, and he began to twist very quick,
and with his head high, and to boast of the readi-
ness of his hands, and the learning he had in his
head, and the strength in his arms. And as he
was boasting, he went backward, twisting the
rope always till he came to the door that was open
behind him, and without thinking he passed the
threshold and was out on the road. And no
sooner was he there than the mother made a sud-
den rush, and threw out the rope after him, and
she shut the door and the half door and put a bolt
upon them.
She was well pleased when she had done that,
and laughed out loud, and the neighbors laughed
and praised her. But they heard him beating at
the door, and saying words of cursing outside it,
and the mother had but time to stop Oona that
had her hand upon the bolt to open it. She made
a sign to the fiddler then, and he began a reel,
and one of the young men asked no leave but
caught hold of Oona and brought her into the
thick of the dance. And when it was over and
the fiddle had stopped, there was no sound at all
of anything outside, but the road was as quiet as
before.
As to Hanrahan, when he knew he was shut Put
and that there was neither shelter nor drink nor a
girl’s ear for him that night, the anger and the
courage went out of him, and he went to where
the waves were beating on the strand.
He sat down on a big stone, and he began
swinging his right arm and singing slowly to
himself, the way he did always to hearten him-
self when every other thing failed him. And
whether it was that time or another time, he
made the song that is called to this day “ The
Twisting of the Rope,” and that begins, “What
was the dead cat that put me in this place is not
known...”
It must be admitted that all this, besides
being finely written, is very like a poet of
the Gaels.
WHETILM BUTLER YEATS
HOUGH his prose be subtile, it is in his
I poetry that Teats may be known best,
and the poetry is of a kind which
will not admit of valuation by odd
snatches, however beautiful. In all Celtic art
one must be steeped if one would profit: there
is little virtue in little sips. It is a thin fluid
that yields intoxication only when you com-
bine Hood’s unfortunate woman and disso-
lute man, and go “over the brink of it,”
“ bathe in it, drink of it”- —wallow in it, in
fact. Then, with every pore a mouth, it is
possible to be drunken on a dream, like him
who slept in Fairyland.
Yeats is not as Celtic or even as Irish as
his work appears. Where he is most mysteri-
ous, the mystery looks suspiciously adventi-
tious or deliberate.
Do you not hear me calling, white deer with no
horns!
I have been changed to a hound with one red ear...
A man with a hazel wand came without sound;
He changed me suddenly; I was looking another
way;...
—and so on. There is for this kind of thing
an excellent descriptive word that rhymes
with the name of a friend of Fitz Gerald’s.
One supposes that it represents the natural
ambition of a Celtic poet to drive his Gael a
little further than it will go.
Yeats’s work is scattered in several tinker-
ings over a good many little volumes : people
who desire his acquaintance may as well
begin with the biggest of Poems (Lond.,
Unwin ; 7s. 6d .) Three parts of Yeats are
here, including that middle part which
one may hold poetically the best of
him. In The Shadowy Waters he can be
judged to embrace beauty more closely, but
throughout the Poems there are parallel
passages with a dropping music like the
plucking of harpstrings :
Red Rose, proud Rose, sad Rose of all my days !
Come near me, while I sing the ancient ways :
Cuhoolin, battling with the bitter tide ;
The Druid, gray, wood-nurtured, quiet-eyed.
Who cast round Fergus dreams, and ruin untold ;
And thine own sadness, whereof stars, grown old
In dancing silver sandalled on the sea,
Sing in their high and lonely melody...
“ Wood-nurtured ” is harsh in that r line :
Yeats’s use of words is not flawless, and some-
times his taste in alliteration errs :
In laughter rose the litter of the waves...
But usually he weaves his lines artfully into
a honeyed harmony that brings an echo
of Shelley, and sometimes of Keats, and
sometimes of Coleridge, and of Poe and
Ingelow and Emerson and others, yet is
always his own.
In the early Wanderings of Oisin there is
a great deal of flat and decorated narrative
that may represent the primary Yeats, rely-
ing on regular form. There is also much
with the cadence of Shelley’s ‘ 1 Sensitive"
Plant,” but less airy, less exuberant.
On a survey it seems fair to suggest that
Yeats is at his best when he is
neither formal nor formless ; when his
verbal outline is broken sufficiently to
give his melody play, yet not sufficiently
to let his substance escape. In his earlier
writing he is comparatively lucid, in his
later writing sometimes positively obscure :
one thinks that he is happiest with a middle
style that is neither in shadow nor in shine,
but in twilight moon-chequered.
The music that Verlaine craved beyond
all is found in Yeats, and he has ideas
withal, a strength of thought and fancy that
(midway through the Poems) moves one to
admiration again and again. It is a strength
that is never the strongest, for Yeats is
perpetually refining and refining ; yet it
exists, and exists with a power of com-
pression that is rare in the Celt. In The
Land of Heart’s Desire , perhaps the finest of
his dramatic essays, he fills ear and mind
with a grave sweetness :
To sit beside the board and drink good wine
And watch the turf smoke coiling from the fire,
And feel content and wisdom in your heart,
This is the best of life,...
Then I would mould a world of fire and dew
With no one bitter, grave, or over wise.
And nothing marred or old to do you wrong.
And crowd the emaptured quiet of the sky
With candles burning to your lonely face...
Finvarra, and their Land of Heart’s Desire,
Where beauty has no ebb, decay no flood,
But joy is wisdom, Time an endless song...
Yeats creates his atmosphere ; he trans-'
fers his mood; he follows, singing, an
exalted quest: he is a true poet and a fine.
And if his inspiration remain aspiration,
if his deed be but a dream, we are never
sure that our best moments are not the most
ineffectual, of that our happiest moments
are not those spent in musing with a
beautiful regret.
Paul Wenz the Austral-French pas-
toralist who so triumphantly has pose un
lapin—devotes an occasional calm Australian
Sunday to literature ; and A VAutre Bout
du Monde , published by the Librairie Uni-
verselle, Paris, collects sixteen sketches
of “aventures et moeurs australiennes.”
Several are reprinted from L’ Illustration,
where they were eagerly read by stay-
at-home Frenchmen, yearning tor fireside
adventure as in the days when Mayne
Peid and Fenimore Cooper were hailed
romanciers du premier ordre. Mr. Wen« has
travelled far and with a fresh eye; his
aventures australiennes are not limited to
the N.S.W. corner, but go north and east to
the islands. He recounts his impressions
dramatically simply, much in Becke’s manner,
in a quaint English-French that shows his
new country is supplanting the old :
D’oh venez-vous? dit l’etranger d’un ton de
commandement.
Jimmy hesita, balbutia, puis repondit :
—De la creek la-bas; j’ai perdu mon chemin,
je ne sais oh je suis.
—Oh avez-vous campe hier soir ?
—lci, dit le “swaggy”; j’efcais si fatigud que
je n’ai pu lever le camp ce matin...
One admires the skill with which Mr. Wenz
has taken the familiar motives of Australian
story—coin so worn that it is refused circu-
lation nowadays—and given them fresh
weight and currency in another language.
The work is literal and faithful, and
one could not have facets of the fact
cut more dexterously for the foreigner.
That the foreigner will receive a false
impression is undeniable: nearly every
book about Australia should begin by
stating that this country is a giant’s
garment, and an author can only pull
half-a-dozen threads from the sleeve;
but ! Elsewhere Mr. Wenz follows
untrodden paths, and turns his themes with
the literary grace that seems a Frenchman’s
birthright. Yet it is to the sketches showing
a keen appreciation of bush character that
one turns a second time.

The Press Illustration Co., Sydney, issues the
“Tophet Series” of post-cards by Fred Brown,
with subjects chosen from Bulletin caricatures
showing how human injustice is righted...L’s-
where. The designer’s inventive talent is
seconded by an ingenious pen; and his work
deserves better printing, with “ black as is black.”
A grateful scribe records that A. E. W. Mason,
semi-historical novelist and M.P., devoted part of
his first House of Commons speech to quotation
from a young lady at a high school. This young
lady described platonic love as “a rapprochement
in which She did n’t know what He meant and
He did n’t mean it.”
Soul-Twilight, by Lucas Cleeve (Lond., John
Long ; 2s. 6 d.) describes a mirage of sex-relation-
ships that astonished a married lady. Its distant
basis of reality seems to lie in passages describing
the lady’s emotions when an eccentrio Venus
swims into her ken and wobbles celestially.
These passages contain a profitable analysis
(naturally wordy) of unreasons at the root of the
eternal Yes-No. In contrast, other answers to
the problem are displayed too imaginatively.
The rest is for femmes incomprises at the lib-
raries.
M. C. Fraser recites anew the marvel of Japan.
One lingers on her picture of the Emperor during
the war, working “ from dawn and frequently till
midnight,” with no Sunday intermission; and
proving sympathy with his frozen soldiers by an
order to discontinue heating his palace till the
war should be over. “The only recreation the
Emperor permitted himself during those eighteen
months of stress was the composing of an occa-
sional short poem in the severely condensed
Chinese style of which he is master.”—The
picture, we submit, is ALschylean.
In Folly, by Edith Bickert (Lond., Arnold;
2s. 6 d.), a new turn is given to F. Morton’s
“twisted idyl.” By the time the woman has
decided that it is imperative for her to bolt, the
man is dying of a disease unspecified (it looks like
cancer of minor poetry), and is in such pain that
he has nothing to...say to her (which smoothes
the path to reconciliation later on). A large
black gulf with a broken fence and a
danger-post yawns through several chapters
where the man has nothing to say ; and the deft
manner in which the author plucks daisies of sen-
timent on the very edge of the gulf is a matter for
surprised plaudits. Folly is a study of the woman
with a tangled temperament, who, in her own
phrase, has to swing like a pendulum before she
settles to rest on the bosom of a husband fortun-
ately steadfast. Reality is gained with accurate
finesse, and the story is worth scrutiny in a pass-
ing show.
A.G.S.
C 1 ED DOF, I count thee happy in thine end,
Far out. upon the ■main at set of sun,
The free wind blowing ; when the wur.c is done
Death conietli only as releasing friend.
The mighty beings wind and ocean blen t
About thy dying eyes, thy soul teas one
With their intensity, thy fate was sinus
With thread that Titan anguish could but rend.
The sea that trill be murmuring evermore,
The vext wind of eternity, they seek
A slumber never gathered—thou hast made
Through toil a path to silence and a shade,
That all our marvelling gaze can not explore,
Sanctified as a dawn-enthroned peak.
HUBERT CHURCH.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cypress Shadows.
The shadows deepen,
The sun sets red
Upon the tombstones
Of the dead.
The sun has set in
A sea of cloud,
A blazing pyre,
A crimson shroud.
On marble cold falls
A ruddy light,
An angry glow or
A promise bright ?
I ask the question
Of Earth and Sky ;
The black trees murmur,
The night winds sigh.
The moon is shining,
The wind is cold,
The Earth is grey, and
The stars are old.
The moonlight steals on
Thy lonely grave,
Through cypress shadows
That stir and wave.
Your eyes are closed and
Your heart is stone ;
The light of Life and
Of Love has flown.
I ask the question ;
The night winds sigh j
There is no answer
In Earth or Sky.
N.S.W.
TTHE BUD.
So-Long!
He used to ride to the fence and say :
“ And how are you getting on to-day ? ”
And then we would talk for an hour or more
Till mother came to the wash-house door,
Calling, “Please, young fellow, just ride away
Or there won’t be any work done to-day ! ”
He would slip the bridle from off the gate,
And grumble, “ Surely the work can wait! ”
The wind would whisper across the plain
All fresh and green from the recent rain ;
Down in the willows beside the swamp
Where wild duck hide in the rushes damp
A bird would break into joyous song ;
And he’d say to me “ My girl, so-long !
We meet to-morrow, you and I.
So-long, sweetheart, but not good-bye ! ”
Ihe days of that spring were happy days,
The ways of my lover were merry ways.
When I fed the fowls or baited the bread
I was always turning my silly head,
To look across by the culvert bridge.
To see him riding over the ridge;
At first the crown of his cabbage straw,
And then the laugh in his eyes I saw,
And then his shoulders so strong and square l —
how can any girl help but care
When the best man in the country side,
For her sake alone, long miles will ride?
My heart was mad with its springtime song,
I called back to him, “So-long ! so-long !
We are bound for ever, you and I;
So-long, sweetheart, but not good-bye.”
What did he see, I wonder, in her,
A sleek white kitten to scratch and purr—
Why did she come, like a blight, to his life ?
And fancy her as a cookie’s wife !
She’ll want a maid to fasten her lace,
And smear the paint on her smirking face—
But what does it matter now to me ?
He is free to love her, and I am free
To mind my work and to bake my bread ;
Free from a love that was quickly dead.
And I leave my sweeping on no pretence;
Nobody looks for me over the fence,
Nobody rides by the culvert bridge,
There’s nothing stirring along the ridge—
Nothing but grass and trees and sky.
Not “So-long, sweetheart!” but a long good-bye.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
After Years.
And here you are in the street again,
So pleased with the world as to leave no doubt
That your life, with its little trouble of rain,
To a broad, fair noon has opened out!
Married these ten years since, you say—
And loved not less as the years go by;
Well, there in your eyes of the dawn’s own grey
The truth looks out, and you cannot lie.
I think that a man might beggar his heart
And pay the coin of his love for less
Than one, just one, of the lights that start
And ripple and play on your every tress.
Well, yes, if you like, it is foolish talk;
“ All false to the last fair word,'’ you say;
Yet, some are ever too prone to walk
In paths where the gods have no feet of clay.
And you—what poison has soured your breast ?
All frank you were in the days of old,
Took phrase and praise, and desired no test
To prove their truth or to try their gold.
Ten years, you say, and it seems so strange !
But yesterday, or the day before,
Time changed a heart, but it did not change
A girl’s red mouth and the smile it wore.
There Time wrought ill, wrought lasting harm,
To spoil a heart that was frank and pure,
And keep rose-red in their early charm,
The mouth and the smile to tempt and lure.
Good-bye, good-bye—there is just a touch
Of light left still in the western sky—
Do you remember an evening such—
When we ... ? Well then—again—Good-bye.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
“ Punter ” : Ever heard of a musical dog?
I possess such an animal which, at the least, can
howl in two notes, if not more. When the Sydney
express passes our place, it gives a loud, long
whistle at a certain crossing: This dog howls
directly after in exactly the same note as the
whistle. The morning goods has a different
whistle, somewhat lower than the express, and
the same tripe-hound imitates this also to a “ T.”
I suppose some monster with a faint resemblance
to human shape will say that he has a dog that
can play the harp ; but I can vouch for the above
case.
“1I.J.L.” : Marine turtle eggs are food
for the gods, provided one does not get frightened
at the fact that the white, or albumen, will not
set, no matter how much it is boiled. Best way
is to pray hard, shut one’s eyes, grab an egg, tear
a small hole in the shell, place to lips, and mutter
“Eureka !” as you squeeze and it slides down.
Then proceed as with oysters. Marine turtle
nests contain anything from 80 to 150 eggs. Also
I can highly advise fresh pelican eggs. Two’s a
meal. In case, however, they should be stale, it
is best to pre-arrange one’s funeral. When a
pelican’s nest is being robbed, the female acts
ludicrously. The largest bird seemingly mar-
shals the colony ; they form in line, with solemn
head-wags, and waddle in single file a short dis-
tance. There they left-about face, stand in line,
and solemnly watch the robbing of their nests,
with an expression of anathema. Then I have
always felt like the jackdaw of Rheims after the
cursing.
“ Gr. Rooney ” : I have just been viewing
the circumstantial evidence of some remarkable
tree-climbing. About Muttama (N.S.W.), where
a lot of the eucalypti extract comes from, great
tall trees are to be seen with all their smaller
branches lopped off. Some of the trunks are
wholly innocent of any foothold, and are as smooth
and slippery as glass. A few years ago, when
the extract business was booming, great rivalry
existed among the climbers. The acknowledged
champion was a man named Hudson, who, with a
light axe, used to cut small notches through the
bark and into the sapwood, just big enough to
accommodate his big toe, and progress upward
with amazing rapidity. Directly in front of the
Muttama Hotel is to be seen one of his master-
pieces. It is a big yellow box-tree as straight
as a gun-barrel, and this writer wouldn’t
undertake to climb it with a step-ladder. Hud-
son eventually came to grief (all climbers do,
sooner or later); he had his nose broken through
falling from the top of a forest sky-scraper. Even
then his presence of mind didn’t forsake him, for
he grasped the limb he fell on and recovered his
balance, otherwise he must have been smashed on
the landscape beneath. But the accident injured
his nerve, and his reputation deteriorated after-
wards. There is a sort of superstition about this
country’s premises to the effect that the aborigines
are the best climbers. Undoubtedly they can
climb—their activity and scaling capacities are all
right, but they lack the white man’s pluck and
judgment. The accomplished eucalypti-getter
will chop away quite fearlessly and unconcernedly
where a blackfellow wouldn't venture for a beer
or the happiest hunting ground ever dreamed of.
By the way, most of the eucalypti extract comes
from the leaves of the yellow-box—or “yellow-
jacket,” as it is commonly called—and not, as is
generally supposed, from those of the forest and
river gum.

Leichhardt Testimony
Every Locality sends its Tribute.
The Case of MRS. A. YOUNG.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
Here is a narrative which will assuredly put many a reader on the right track—supplied to us by
Mrs. Annie Young, of Myrtle-street, Leichhardt. It was at Mrs. Young’s present address, where
she has been living for the past eleven years, that our special correspondent was granted the inter-
view which appears hereunder: —
“It is thirteen years since I had the illness you refer to. Truly the time does fly. It doesn’t
seem half so long ago, for everything is as fresh in my memory as though it was only yesterday.”
“ Were you experiencing any physical pain ? ”
“Oh! don’t talk about it. Have you ever had neuralgia in the head? I hope not, for it was
something dreadful. Right across the forehead, through from one temple to the other, and up to the
top of my head, the most acute pain imaginable harassed me. The agony was so great that often I
imagined something was pressing on my brain, and I really used to think I would go mad with it.
It had such an effect upon me, indeed, that even my sight went bad, and as for the feeling in my
ey ea —well, they felt as if they were double the size they should have been. Ah ! what desolate,
melancholy days they were ! I’m sure I felt myself to be about the most unhappy woman in the
world, and I think I had occasion to be in view of the fact that the outlook was so black.”
“Do you mean that your prospects of getting strong again seemed remote ?”
“They did, indeed, for I had been attended to by several doctors, and the most they could do for
me was to”give just a little relief now and then ; but there was nothing lasting about it, and if I felt a
trifle better one day, I was sure to feel ten times worse the next, and the medicine, besides being so
ineffective, was always disagreeable to take. A strange thing was that I felt always so worn-out,
drowsy and exhausted—as if I was only fit to go somewhere and lie down and die—yet, when I made
an effort to sleep, do you think I could ? Not a bit of it. I’d twist and turn nearly the whole night
through, and when at last I dozed off the dreams I had were fearful.”
“And you couldn’t get permanent relief from these symptoms through the medicine prescribed
for you?”
“No, unfortunately. But finally I got something to do me good. Clement’s Tonic it was,
and the best remedy I ever had in all my life, too. Goodness, what a show it made of every-
thing else I had taken, for instead of only easing me a little occasionally, it went right to the
base of my infirmities, and lifted them out by the roots! In addition to what I have already told
you, I must state that I was highly nervous—nobody could be more so, for I was actually
afra’id to stay in my own house by myself, and was continually worrying myself about
things that I “imagined were going to happen. Trouble was always hanging over me, and I
couldn’t get the idea out of my head—not until I had been taking Clement’s Tonic for a space
of time, when, quite mysteriously, I ceased brooding over such silly things, and my mind became
as happy as the day. Oh !it was wonderful what an altered woman Clement’s Tonic made me.
Instead of being anxious and timid all day, I got as happy and cheerful as possible—nothing
frightened me, and my nerves were steady. It is because I know so many nervous women that I deem
it my duty to say what a splendid remedy Clement’s Tonic is for that complaint, though, for that matter,
it was equally beneficial to me in other ways. I used to get pains down the small of my back
and about my shoulders, also palpitation and pains in the region of my heart, which made me think
that something very serious was wrong with me there; but Clement’s Tonic showed itself to be such a
pain-removing medium that I can faithfully recommend it to all sufferers. Its regulating influences
on the heart were extraordinary, and for making one’s sleep sound and peaceful, the same remedy was
the only one to meet with my requirements. I wish I had used it months before, for plenty of people
told me what a grand medicine Clement’s Tonic was. The least I can do is to assist my neighbours
to throw off their afflictions, so you may publish my experiences in any manner you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I ANNIE YOUNG, of Myrtle-street, Leichhardt, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of
Australia do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of mv illness and cure by Clement’s Tonic, and also contains my full permission to
nublish in any way my statements—which I give voluntarily without receiving any payment; and I make this
bilemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act,
1000 (Sgd.) ANNIE YOUNG.
Subscribed and declared at
before me,
Petersham this fifth day of September, one thousand nine hundred and four,
H. EVANS, J.P.

Hoiv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regal’d to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, JUNE 21, 1906.
THE IMPORTED OFFICIAL.
The Governor-General’s speech at the opening of
Parliament gave some indications that the time
comes at length when the Australian will be a
man of some honor in his own country. Hitherto—
somewhat like the Irishman, who cuts a notable
figure in the public life of almost every land in the
world, except Ireland—the Australian has found
least encouragement in this, his country by
birth or adoption. “ Australian ” as an adjective,
applied to men or goods in Australia, has long
been almost synonymous with “second-class.”
Australian wine, “colonial” beer, local furniture,
home-made jam are used as terms of inferiority,
and give indications of a wide current of anti-
Australian thought. The Australian youth of the
richer classes cannot be taught on absolutely right
lines in Australia. He has to go to Eton and
learn to leave the last button of his vest loose, as
if he had dressed in a hurry and really couldn’t
bother ; or to Harrow and find the art of poising a
pill-box straw-hat on his head; and then on to
Oxford to acquire the Piccadilly drawl and the
languishing affectation of an accent of boredom.
The Australian girl isn’t quite the thing until she
has been to London and worn a shockingly cold
dress on a shockingly cold day before a royal per-
sonage who hasn’t gained enough common-sense
to allow his or her guests to be decently and
warmlv clad at a “Drawing-Room.” When the
Australian youth of the English-educated type re-
turns it is often with the conviction that good clothes
are only made in London, and his sister or sweet-
heart, the Australian girl who has been
“finished” in London, has the same faith in the
impossibility of Australian clothes. I he Aus-
tralian politician has long looked for his
highest earthly reward in London, and sang his
nunc dimitte when a tired clerkling in Downing-
street posted to him one of a batch of decorations
intended for nigger chiefs, “colonial” politicians,
and small English mayors. The Australian daily
newspapers have long set themselves to be pallid
shadows of the London journals, putting British
ideas into inferior English for the consumption of
the Australian intellect. Finally Australian en-
terprises have been generally discouraged and
treated with contempt, it being preached assidu-
ously that there isn’t enough energy and brain-
power in the continent to do more than grow
wheat and wool to feed and clothe the superior
race inhabiting the British isles.
* * * «
The phenomenon of this general self-abasement
in Australia is more than difficult to explain, for,
from the beginning, the Australian population, in
the mass, was extraordinarily virile. The first
Australians were to a slight extent criminal con-
victs, to a much greater extent they were convicts
representing the cream of the British populace—
sturdy English rebels against game laws worthy
of the worst days of the French Bourbons ; Scotch
revolters against tyranny ; Irishmen guilty of no
other crime than patriotism; Chartists, discon-
tenteds, men who refused to doff caps and bend
knees—the daring spirits who clashed with
brutal laws and tyrannical customs. To these
early colonists, translated into a country
where they breathed an ampler air than ever
Europe could offer, came in time the hardy
adventurers of all the world, drawn by the gold
rush and the later rush for “free selection ” land.
Thus the Australian population from the first
seethed with energy ; and it still has great capacity
for energy. (There is nothing that is done on earth
to-day that an Australian cannot do as well. There
is no race with more resource and more industry.)
Having sucked in so much of the adventurous
genius of other lands, Australia, with its infinite
possibilities, seemed destined to a quick leap to
greatness. But something was in the way. Aus-
tralian development is to-day, though not frus-
trated, blocked. It is not possible to find any
reason for this except it is to be found in Aus-
tralian politics—in the fact that the various Aus-
tralian Parliaments in the past did not repre-
sent the vigor of the race. The people were too
busy subduing the wilderness to trouble much
about politics, and fell under the dominance of
small peddling men, whose ideas were foreign to
the new aspirations of a new people, and who
elected to limp after English examples in tardy
apish imitation. No one can deny Australian
energy. It shows itself locally in every domain
of effort from athletics to art. It shows itself
even more conspicuously abroad. The Australian
who can find no honor in the Commonwealth you
will find in Europe, Asia, Africa and America
doing for other lands what he is not per-
mitted to do for his own. Only in the
government of Australia has the Australian
so far shown sloth, negligence and incapa-
city. That exception sadly enough spoils most
of the value of effort in other directions. Without
guiding political leadership, the Australian energy
largely runs to waste. Physical strenuousness,
denied other outlet, loses itself in athletics. In-
ventive and administrative ability seek rewards
abroad, and in every branch of art the Australian
exile advertises at once the capacity of his country
to produce great men, and its incapacity to put
them to good use.
* * * *
Now there is some indication of a change.
Federation has given a new meaning to politics.
A Prime Minister speaks openly of “ Australia for
the Australians’’—Australians by biith or adop-
tion, and for all reputable, healthy white immi-
grants who are willing to become Australian, and
to think Australian; instead of Australia for
the foreigner, whose thoughts and ambi-
tions are foreign. The last Governor-General’s
speech to Parliament declares that there will
be no more appointments of military officers
from abroad to teach the Australian soldier the
warfare that he will have to carry on, not under
the conditions of abroad,_ but the conditions of-
here. The same speech hinted at a change in the
administration of Papua from the present British
colony government, which has failed, to an
Australian system of rule which, in all proba-
bility, would succeed better, and which, in any
case, is fitting, since Papua is now an Australian
possession, for which Australia must take the
financial and other responsibility. But the hint
was misleading, if it is true, as is just now freely
rumored, that a new Governor is to be imported
for Papua—a sexagenarian outsider, when plenty
of young capable Australians are available. If
this proves true, then clearly Deakin’s patriotic cry
is more for the platform than the Cabinet. And it
certainly is true that the present Deakin Govern-
ment with its policy of “Australia for the Aus-
tralians ” is talking of importing a meteorologist
merely because the habit of importing people and
things has become so deep-seated as to be almost
instinctive, and has to be unlearned with painful
effort. Prime Minister Deakin has learned that it
is not necessary to import boots and clothes and
machinery ; nor military officers, nor judges ; but,
knowing little probably of meteorology, when a
professor of that science is wanted, he instinctively
thinks of bringing from abroad a man with no
knowledge of Australian climate or conditions.
And what is wanted in our public men is a deep-
seated conviction that Australians can make any-
thing and do anything—a conviction only to be
abandoned in any particular regard on the clearest
of proof to the contrary. Only when that con-
viction is formed will the Australian energy get a
chance of justifying itself.
• * * *
The extent to which the disease of importing
officials has infected Australia can be seen by a
glance at the news items and articles in the daily
press for the last month. Proposals, or arguments,
or congratulations will be there found for importing
a Governor for Papua, on the ground that no mere
Australian is capable of ruling over a few thousand
blacks; a military commandant and staff, because
the Australian can do only the rough work of
soldiering, and mustn’taspiremuchabovethegoose-
step stage ; a railway commissioner for N.S.W.,
because the local Chief Commissioner finds it hard
to do good work, hampered by cat-fighting col-
leagues : a meteorologist, because Australian
weather must be better understood by an Ameri-
can or Englishman than by any mere Australian,
without the stamp of Boston or London;
a Judge for Westralia, because Australian
lawyers haven’t the necessary knowledge
or character ; an engineer for the pro-
posed new Brisbane waterworks—presumably
because the present works, designed by an im-
ported engineer, are a ghastly failure; and
parsons for various parts of the country, because
Heaven is more gracious when addressed in an
Oxford accent. All these proposed or actual
appointments are canvassed with wide approval,
in spite of the fact that, in every department of
human effort embraced in those callings, Austra-
lians have done, and are doing now, either in
Australia or elsewhere, conspicuously good work.
It is simply that the importing idea has become an
actual obsession ; public men, from a long habit of
looking to London for their ideas, have got an over-
mastering delusion that Australia’s destiny is to ex-
port raw material and import shoddy manufactures;
to export raw genius and import shoddy officials.
Tap Bulletin, in fighting that mania, wishes to
set up no narrow and bigoted test of Australian
birth as a condition of Australian official employ-
ment. Every white man, who is here and who
shows his sympathy with Australian life by de-
termining to stay here and throw in his lot with
ours, may be ranked as Australian. And when
Australian public men really begin to act and
think Australian, and the Commonwealth pro-
gresses as it should, there will be such demand for
men of all grades that a big influx of officials, as
well as workers, from abroad will be inevitable.
What has to be fought against is the idea that,
when a man of capacity is wanted, importation is
the first thing to be thought of. Let Australia be
closely scanned first. In short, let the policy
of the Immigration Restriction Act be applied
to bar any imported person —even a general
or a bishop—coming here under contract unless
there is clear proof that no Australian is fit for the
post. •
THE N.S.W. LANDS SCANDALS IN 1885.
Report of the N.S.W. Lands Inquiry Com-
mission, 1906, recalls the land scandals ventilated
by Dr. Ross, M.L.A,, and the late John
M'Elhone, M.L.A., as far back as 1885. (It was
Dr. Ross’s sturdy honesty in bringing these
scandals to light that inspired the celebrated
Phil May picture of Ross as Diogenes— used
for some time as a cover to The Bulletin ; a re-
production of that picture appeared in last week’s
issue.) What The Bulletin said about land
agents on September 26, 1885, is worth reprinting
now, the tribe having apparently undergone little
change in the course of 21 years:—
FATHER-IN-LAW FARNELL REPLIES.
For some time prior to the opening of the present
session of the Parliament of N.S. Wales, ugly rumors were
abroad concerning the administration of the Land Act of
1884. It was said that, like other acts of every day occur-
rence, the forfeiture or non-forfeiture of conditionally-pur-
chased land went entirely by favor. Some color was given
to this by the publication of a letter signed by a land-agent
named Frederick Cummins, a son-in-law of the Minister
for Lands. Judging by his letter, Mr. Cummins appears
to be a young man who thinks it stupid to have influential
relatives without using them. The use to which he put,
or intended to put, his father-in-law, Mr. Farnell, can
be gleaned from the following circulars without the
trouble of reading between the lines :
Dear Sir, I notice that your conditional
purchase has just been gazetted forfeited, and I
now beg to inform you that, if employed by
you, I will use my utmost endeavors to have the
forfeiture reversed, and you re-instated on your
selection. Upon receiving instructions from you
I will at once peruse the evidence, and place your
case in as favorable a light as possible before the
Minister for Lands, with a view to reversal of the
existing forfeiture. There can be no doubt that
many selections are forfeited on account of the
parties concerned not understanding the method
of conducting their cases before Inquiry Courts,
although in reality the selections are perfectly
bona fide. In cases of this kind the services of a
clever, experienced agent are invaluable, and
often result in his obtaining for the selector a re-
hearing of his case before the court, and ulti-
mately a reversal of forfeiture without rehearing.
In conclusion, I think I may safely say that I
have as much influence at headquarters as any
other agent in Sydney, and very much more than
a good many. I am, yours faithfully, F. CUM-
MING.
We believe this to be a model of perspicuity. From
the opening pronoun to the final adjective it is lucidity
itself. Whatever fault may be found with Mr. Cumming
for the part he takes in the administration of the Laud
Act, there can be but one opinion regarding the benefits
of early training in enabling a pushing person to express
his views with elegance and directness. If his influence
with his relative the Minister can be gauged by his fee,
he must have been a favorite son-in-law.
But it was only on the opening of Parliament that
these rumors became precipitated and crystallised. It
was then that pointed and uncomplimentary allusions
were made to the positions as “land agents” held by
some of the Minister’s relatives. Speaking from his place
in the House, Dr. Ross, member for Molong, made some
revelations, which fairly surprised and alarmed the
country. Among the doctor’s constituents were three
selectors— M’Cudden, Leary, and Scott. For various
causes, their selections were recommended for forfeiture,
and they applied to their member to use his endeavors in
getting the forfeitures revoked. He first, he said, tried
the Minister with M’Cudden’S case, but was unsuccessful.
The application for revocation could not be entertained.
Was M’Cudden satisfied to let the law take its course
without making a final effort to recover the land? Far
from it. He wrote to Cumming on the matter, and re-
ceived the following reply :
Dear Sir,—l am in receipt of your favor of the
16th instant, and hasten to reply. I really think
if you come to Sydney I can pull you through,
but, of course, it will be difficult. With refer-
ence to your inquiry about fee, I think it will cost
about £6O at least. Why not come for a trip to
Sydney ; it will not cost you much, and then I
will take you to see my relative, the Minister for
Lands, and I hope to get the forfeiture reversed.
—Yours fully, Frederick Cumming. P.S.—lf you
do come to Sydney, I shall not require you for
about ten days.
He came. He and Mr. Cumming saw, and between them
conquered. Thus we see that Dr. Ross tried to get
McCudden’s claim re-considered, and failed. Another
tried and succeeded, not only in getting the matter re-
opened, but in getting the claim allowed. That other was
the Minister’s own son-in-law, Mr. Frederick Cumming.
* * * •
Then there was Leary’s case. Leary came to Sydney
to have the forfeiture of his selection rescinded. For
him, too, Dr. Ross did his utmost, but in vain. And now
comes the most suggestive of the doctor’s statements—
one which, so far, has not yet received an answer either
in or out of Parliament; “ Immediately the £5O was
qiven, the forfeiture was revoked." The last case to which
the member for Molong referred was that of Robert
Scott. Scott was burnt out, and even the grass on his
land was destroyed. Dr. Ross, undaunted by his former
failures, pleaded Scott’s case also, but with a result simi-
lar to the previous ones. Indeed, as a land agent under
the present administration, the Molong representative
appears to be a dismal failure. Scott turned Sydney-
wards, expended £5O judiciously, and got his land back,
accompanied by the following receipt
Received from Mr. Robert Scott the sum of
fifty pounds sterling as a fee for having forfeiture
of his conditional purchase, 82-145, of 200 acres’
and 84-126, of 110 acres, reversed.— Frederick
Cumming, Sydney, August 11 th, 1885.
From a week from the time of the first allegations
against his administration, the Minister for Lands pre-
served a strange silence. On Thursday, the 17th Sep-
tember, his long-expected explanation was given. The
metropolitan journals, without exception, described it as
eminently successful. . . To us it appears that Mr. Far-
nell’s explanation is no explanation at all —that it leaves
the matter just where Dr. Ross left it.
• # • •
This is ivhat the Minister is reported to have said :
So far as I am concerned myself, I am not re-
sponsible for these letters. No one deprecates
the writing of them more than I do. I say it was
a most improper and indecent thing for these
young men to do—sons-in-law of mine though
they be. I say it is the grossest misconduct, and
if they had been my greatest enemies they could
not have invented anything more calculated to
do me harm in my ministerial reputation.
Whether they have done the thing innocently or
not Ido not know. Whether they have written
these letters on account of the position that I
hold, and for the purpose of getting business, of
course is known to themselves and not to me. I
know nothing of theiractions or their transactions.
Indeed, I very seldom see them as sons-in-law,
or in their capacity as land-agents. These gentle-
men were land-agents long before they became
my sons-in-law. They were land-agents—one of
them, at all events—before I became a Minister
of the Crown. I have really no defence to make,
for I don’t intend to defend those young men for
their misconduct—because it is misconduct.
Neither sons-in-law, daughters-in-law,mother, nor
sister would influence me in my administration of
the law. I have too much confidence in my rec-
titude to allow myself to be swayed by personal
relationships. This matter having been brought
under the attention of the Uou-e, and I, myself,
feeling its real tendency—because I dare say it
mav cause a severance of my family ties—l can
only say I shall do my duty honorably and fairly
to the public; and as my attention has been
called to the matter, I shall consider it my duty
to issue instructions that these young- men shall
not be allowed to see me personally in their capa-
city as land-agents.
In these sceptical times it is really pleasant to know there
are those amongst us who are satisfied with this explana-
tion.
* * * *
It is not with the writings of the letters we are con-
cerned. It is of little public interest whether Mr. Farnelli
considers them “ indecent” or not, whether or not they
were written in all innocence by his interesting relatives.
What we really want to know, and what has, so far.
remained unexplained, is why certain revocations were
denied to one agent and granted to another. If the
officers of the Department are responsible, it is for them
to produce the fresh evidence on which their change of
opinion was founded. It is reassuring to know that
neither sons-in-law, daughters-in-law, mothers nor sisters
can influence the Minister. But if they can influence
others who, in their turn, can influence him, how is the
matter mended? According to Dr. Ross, certain lands
were forfeited. He tried to get the forfeitures cancelled,
but failed. Other persons, who happened to be related
to the Minister, tried and succeeded. Why
We no more believe Mr. Farnell corrupt than the
member for Molong did ; but at the same time, we deny
that his explanation is satisfactory. It is a lawyers
explanation throughout. This might account for the
Minister’s silence till silence was no longer possible.
It might account also for other peculiarities in the reply.
If by the truth is meant the whole truth, and nothing
but the truth, then entire veracity is almost a
stranger to the legal mind. There is but one more
point in the Minister’s exculpation to which we
have space to refer. “As he (Mr. Farnell) understood
the word, they never had a corrupt Government, and, in
fact, he did not know what was meant by corruption.
It is clear, then, that he does not understand the word.
The friends of Democracy will take fresh heart when they
find that for years they have returned to Parliament an
important statesman who did not know what was meant
by corruption. Still, as even the purest-minded priest
has to read bad books in order to discover and refute the
errors they contain, it would be only fair to the public
for even the most ingenuous Minister to learn the mean-
ing of corruption, if only to destroy it. Should Mr.
Farnell seriously desire to learn the various transactions
to which this important word might be applied, we
engage—provided a free pardon is held out to us—to give
him the names of at least half-a-dozen members who, we
have no doubt, would gladly afford a good deal of infor-
mation on the subject.
• * * *
In succeeding articles it was suggested by The
Bulletin, then, that precautions should be taken
against the growing evil of influential land agency.
But the daily press—true to its traditions
no support, and Parliament no attention. The
germs of Lands maladministration were allowed
to come to the monstrous growth shown in the
1906 report. And that 1906 report will not be the
last unless the most stringent safeguards are now
taken against future malversation. The Lands
Department has control of many millions of
pounds worth of property. Its express duty is
to administer that trust under conditions of equity
and good sense rather than within the exact letter
of particular enactments. Necessarily, therefore,
officials of the Department, must have a wide
discretion. To see that that discretion is not in
the future used dishonestly is the task which now
awaits Parliament. If the N.S.W. Lands De-
partment has been under suspicion for 21 years
all the more reason why there should be no delay
in putting it beyond suspicion now, if such a thing
be possible.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Carruthers’ Slander upon N.S.W.
Last week there was laid before the Australian
Parliament certain correspondence that had
passed between the Australian Government and
Joe Carruthers. of N.S.W., on the subject of
the Australian capital. It is an amazing corre-
spondence on Joe’s side—a correspondence that
should make N.S. W. feel even more ashamed «f
its Joe than it ought to have been before—for the
case put forward is backed by such glaringly
false assertions as to be an outrage on political
decency. The following extracts show the main
features of the story which Carruthers told on
behalf of his suffering State
One of the main causes which led to the rejection of
the first Federal Constitution by the people of New
South Wales at the 1898 referendum was the indefinite
nature of the clause relating to the seat of government.
At the conference of Premiers at Melbourne in 1899 the
following resolution was agreed to : —“ It is considered
that the fixing of the site in question might well be left
to Parliament to decide, but in view of the strong ex-
pression of opinion in New South Wales, the Premiers
have modified the clause so that, while the site cannot be
fixed at Sydney or in its neighborhood, provisibn is made
in the Constitution for its establishment in New South
Wales at a reasonable distance from that city.” The
Premiers thereupon agreed to the clause, now section
125 of the Constitution Act.
. . . Mr. Reid endeavored to insert a provision that the
site should be in New South Wales and distant not less
than 75 miles from Sydney. Mr. George Turner pro-
posed as an alternative that it should be in New South
Wales, but not less than 200 miles from Sydney. Eventu-
ally all the Premiers agreed that the site should be not
less than 100 miles from Sydney, and as near to the 100-
mile limit as possible.
Subsequently the clause originating section 125 was
drafted, and the draft Constitution as amended was ac-
cepted in all good faith by the electors of New South
Wales.
How, then, can it be maintained that the choice of
Dalgety by the Federal Parliament is equitable, and in
accordance with the letter and spirit of the Constitution?
The electors of New South Wales would have given an
adverse vote at the referendum of 1899 had they not been
fully satisfied that the seat of government clause would
secure the capital for New South Wales at an early date
in the history of the federation, and, further, that it
would be within reasonable distance of Sydney.
For the hundredth time this paper takes occasion
to tell Joe the Slanderer that the people of
N.S.W. are not all—nor even half—the sordid,
selfish, grasping bargainers that he represents
them to be. They did not reject the Federal
Constitution at the 1898 referendum because
N.S.W. hadn’t been promised the Federal
capital; in fact, as Joseph well knows, they
didn’t reject it at all. They accepted it
by a majority of over 5000. The majority
counted nearly 72,000 votes, and the N.S.W.
Government and Parliament had pledged them-
selves to the other States that if there was an
affirmative majority, and that majority consisted
of at least 50,000 votes, the Constitution would be
carried; and they asked the other States to
go into the movement on these terms. The fact
that the Parliament of 1898 was a Parliament of
liars and faith-breakers, and that the Government
of 1898 had neither political honesty nor political
decency ; and that between them they broke their
agreement, raised the minimum to 80,000, re-
versed the people’s decision and nullified the
popular vote, makes no difference in the fact of
the popular acceptance. As regards the further
slander that the people of N.S.W. were so utterly
sordid that they would have rejected the Federal
Constitution at the second referendum if they had
not been bribed by the promise of the Federal
capital, it is only necessary to answer that
as Joe is not a prophet lie can’t say, and
as he is a most impudent and reckless asserter
his word isn’t sufficient evidence. Certainly
the fact that the people of N.S.W. accepted
the first Constitution, in which there was
no promise that the Federal capital would be


in any particular State, makes it eminently proba-
ble that they would also have accepted the second
one without the greedy bargaining that Joe the
Traducer ascribes to them. The three other main
points in the Carrutheiis argument—that there
has been discovered somewhere, after years cf
concealment, an allegedly authentic scrap of paper
on which four or five irresponsible persons jotted
down a suggestion that the capital should be
within a “reasonable” distance of Sydney ; thet
this document, of which the people of Australia
knew nothing when they accepted the. Constitu-
tion, should override the Constitution itself ; and
that Joe should be sole judge of what is a
“ reasonable distance ” —these merely go to show
the boundless cheek of a very commonplace little
politician. Australia couldn’t expect anything
better from Carruthers than it has got
in this matter, but when it turns to the
other side of the correspondence, it cer-
tainly has a right to complain of Prime
Minister Dkakin. If Deakin were a whole
man, instead of being partly a man and the
rest only an affable circumstance he would have
intimated in polished phrase to Carruthers that
unless the obvious and glaring falsehoods were
left out of the argument he would decline to dis-
cuss the matter, and that he would also decline to
discuss it unless Joseph stuck to the Constitution
which the people had accepted, and left out
irrelevant questions about alleged memoranda
conveniently found in a drawer or the lining of
somebody’s hat, or other unexpected places.
Deakin’s time is too valuable to be wasted in dis-
cussion upon such trash—or it ought to be.
+
Tlie Producer and the Middleman.
Tory newspapers have for weeks done nothing
but squeal at Lynk’s Commerce Act regulations.
Melbourne and Sydney butter-agents have howled
at the ruin awaiting the butter export trade. In
the midst of the lamentation came a conference of
11 Gippsland butter-factory companies, to re-
solve :
That the compulsory grading of butter for export is of
vital importance to the best interests of the dairying
industry.
That this meeting regrets the action taken by the co-
operative selling companies in offering opposition to the
compulsory grading and marking of butter without con-
sulting the factories supporting them, as it is.in direct
opposition to the best interests of the factories in the co-
operation and the industry generally.
The conference thereupon proceeded to sing
hymns to Vie. Minister Swinburne, and to set
up altars to Bill Lyne. And for the space of a
day at least the press, which had wailed that
Lyne was about to destroy the butter industry,
was silent and thoughtful. Newspapers like the
Argus study the interests of the Flinders-lane
agents who fleece the farmer and call it “ assisting
the producer.” The great cry against butter-
grading is caused by the fear that London firms,
satisfied with the State guarantee, will buy direct
from the factories, and so wipe out the Mel-
bourne middleman.
Greater Melbourne.
Greater Melbourne is steadily growing. The
success that has attended the City Corporation’s
socialistic enterprises is making the outside
municipalities think hard and push for a share of
the profits. The latest applicants for union are
South Melbourne (which is a city itself) and Port
Melbourne (a borough). A conference between
representatives from these two municipalities
and the Melbourne city has decided on a
scheme for unification. Apparently both “South”
and “Port” have everything to gain by the
union. South Melbourne rates have never for
years been below Is. 9d. or Is. lOd. in the £l. At
present, indeed, the rate is 2s. in the £1; but
South is busy paying off a loan. Port Melbourne
has always liad a rate of Is. 6d. in the £l, except
when special demands for loan sinking funds
necessitated a higher charge. The rate in the
city of Melbourne is Is. 4d. in the £l—ls. town
rate and 4d. lighting rate—while every year a big
lamp of money is added to the town fund—a fund
intended to ultimately extinguish rates altogether.
South Melbourne will, when it joins Greater Mel-
-1 iourne, have a one-and-tenpenny rate till the end of
1907, then a one-and-eiglitpenny rate for four
years, and after that the present city rate of Is. 4d.
Port will pay Is. 6d. for five years, and then drop
to Is. 4d. There is no doubt that both these
municipalities would have joined with Melbourne
long since but for Baragawanath, ex-Mayor
of South Melbourne. He has been dictator of the
two councils for years, and feared the obscurity
that the merging of the two with the city would
inevitably bring for him. He went away to
the Cold Country a few months ago, and
in his absence the other side got to work
with such zeal that he will probably come
back to find South and Port, two wards of the
City of Melbourne, each represented in the City
Council by an alderman and three councillors.
The things Baragawanath will say then ought
to be worth hearing. The City Corporation also
gains a great tactical victory over South Mel-
bourne on the question of markets. South and
the city have been at feud (Baragawanath
mostly ran the feud) for over a year regard-
ing their rival markets. Melbourne’s Victoria mar-
ket was a little cramped for the market gardeners,
and South Melbourne saw a chance of annexing
most of the fat revenue the city draws from this
source. It was proposed to borrow (Baragawa-
nath again) a cart-load of money, to build a huge
market on St. Kilda Hoad, and to catch
all the market gardeners before they got
to Melbourne. Now, of course, the scheme
falls to the ground, and Melbourne Council
will continue to socialistically reap a large annual
harvest from its markets. Still, South Mel-
bourne will gain more by reduction of rates than
it would have done out of its proposed speculation.
The annexation of South and Port will hurry
up the advent of a Greater Melbourne, covering
the whole of the nearer suburbs. Already Col-
lingwood, Pitzroy, and Richmond have expressed
themselves in favor of unification.
Bent’s Disfranchisement Scheme.
Premier Bent, of Victoria, has, it is alleged,
built up a scheme for disfranchising the public,
which is, in many respects, a great deal more logi-
cal than it looks. There is a grim humor about
Bent’s idea, and a certain grim fitness also. . Ac-
cording to report, he simply proposes that when a
man doesn’t vote he shouldn’t be allowed to vote;
that non-voters should be disfranchised for a fixed
period—a somewhat lengthy period it would seem
—to see how they like it. Put in the fewest
words, the theory is this: Smith asked for a vote,
and wasn’t satisfied till he got it. Then the elec-
tion came, and Smith didn’t use his vote. There-
fore, let Smith be deprived for a while of his vote
and his right to stand for Parliament, so that out
of the inherent cussedness of human nature he
will desire to use those privileges as veil
as merely possess them, and when he gets
back again on the roll after putting in a
spell as a degraded political outcast, he will
put more value on his status as a citizen.
And so long as allowance is made for cases of
severe illness or absence from the State, there is
only one flaw in the logic. Smith may have
stayed away from the ballot because there was not
in his electorate a single candidate that he could
conscientiously vote for ; he objected to vote for
Bad, M.L.A., and he didn’t feel that things would
be improved if he voted for Worse, J,P. The
remedy is to allow a negative vote, and then the
poll might occasionally turn oiit like this : —
John Bad 1,264
William Worse 1,122
No (just plain No) .. .. .. 1,360
In that case there would have to be a new elec-
tion, with both Bad and Worse disqualified as
candidates. And if, by some amazing combina-
tion of circumstances, the second election panned
out like this—
T. Fool .. .. 1060
G. W. Fuller .. 930
No .. .. 1123
there would be nothing for it but to let that
constituency do without any member for one
Parliament, it being reckoned as a place that
couldn’t make up its mind ; and thus a member’s
salary would be saved. The great point is that
under this policy Smith, the elector, would have
no reason left for staying away from the poll. If
he objected equally to Bad and Worse, and
thought Fool and Fuller were similarly unde-
sirable, he could still do his country good and
manful service by voting against all of them.
But if, with such complete facilities for expressing
his political opinions, he still didn’t express them
(unless he was kept away by severe illness or was
absent from the State), the best thing possible
would be to disfranchise him for, say, four years,
so as to make sure of abolishing him for at least
one election, and then to restore him only on
personal application. This could be done on
the fair assumption that he had no political
opinions to express, or that he took no
interest in his country’s welfare, and was an
unpatriotic citizen whose interference would do
more harm than good. Either way, four years
would be not too long a time to allow him, so
that he might get some political opinions, or
recover from being an unpatriotic citizen. But
without the negative vote any disfranchisement
scheme would be an outrage. And while Bent is
on the job he might consider, as a side issue, the
advisability of having a second ballot where the
candidate at the top of the list does not represent
a majority of the votes polled. Out of 68 mem-
bers returned to the Victorian Assembly in 1904,
13 represented minorities, which is a pretty bad
proportion.
Premier Price Hits Back.
Premier Price, of S. Australia, rose the other
day in a much-needed manner to a very large
occasion, and told things to the ladies’ branch of
the Farmers’ Political Union, which were cer-
tainly unpleasant but very probably true. Some
of the proceedings were reported as follows : —
Mr. Price : The Ladies’ Branch of the Farmers’
Political Union had issued a circular which said
that the Labor party desired to weaken the mar-
riage tie and burst up family life. That was a
wicked and malicious lie. He was prepared to place
his married life, and that of those associated with
him, alongside that of men on the other side. If they
wanted to find men who had more wives than one they
were not to be found in his party. He could, however,
take them to North Adelaide, and other places, and could
find them men like that; but they did not belong- to his
party. (Applause.) Yet he found here that the ladies’
committee at Mount Gambier took men into their homes
who were known to be “ dirty birds ” in Mount Gambier.
Cries of “Oh,” and of “It is a lie,” “Shame,” and
much confusion.
Mr. Price : No, it is not a lie.
Cries of “ It’s a lie,” and uproar.
Mr. Price ; It is not; and as far as women of the
league here are concerned, they honor men with dirty
characters.
Voices: “They’re as good as any other ladies,” “You
can’t prove it.”
Mr. Price said he knew what he was speaking about.
They excused a man here that was not clean.
Such statements as these may be uncivil, but
when anyone considers the impudence, the
silly, pompous, supercilious ignorance, and the
blatant lying of the “ladies” of various Tory
political organisations, he might fairly con-
clude that the time for civility is past. The
Bulletin has no knowledge of the Mount Gam-
bier Ladies Committee alluded to by Premier
Price, so it passes that by. But it does know that
with many members of the Tory female organisa-
tions which bleat about the “sanctity of the
home,” and the “sacred character of the marriage
tie,” and falsely accuse the Labor party of a
desire to destroy both—with many of them the
home is mostly a place to be absent from, and as
regards the marriage tie their husbands have
reduced it to a joke, apparently without
serious protest on their parts. As for Mr.
Price’s general statement that rich men of
infamous moral character are freely admitted into
the society which these Tory dames adorn, and
are allowed to deposit the cloven hoof of
sin on top of the “purity of the domestic
hearth,” that is so well known that it was
hardly worth while for the S.A. Premier to
mention it. If such things were not allowed
there would be very little “ society ” left.
Probably it is unkind to allude to such details of
social rottenness and social sham, but the super-
cilious manner in which the whited sepulchres of
fashion rise to accuse a class which is vastly their
superior in morality, of evil designs upon the
“ marriage tie ” (or as much of the “tie ” as has
survived frequent visits to Japan and its geishas),
and of conspiracies against the “purity of the
domestic hearth ” (which, in the most aristo-
cratic quarters, is already muddied all over with
the footprints of welcome visitors whose moral
characters are just about unspeakable) makes it
necessary. As a matter of fact, many of the
“ladies” of the Tory political leagues have put
themselves beyond the pale of ordinary courtesy.
Their methods are based on a cheerful assumption
that in dealing with the hated Democratic party it
is permissible to lay aside every semblance of truth
and fair-dealing, and that any lie or slander is
permissible. And withal they have shown a
childish inability to make the lie look plausible or
the slander seem feasible, which has covered them
with contempt. Their ignorance of history, poli-
tics, public finance, law, and all other matters on
which they touch, is that of an infant school, and
their minds have apparently been trained for large
public affairs by petty drawing-room discussions
on the nefarious proceedings of Mary Jane, the
servant, and by small social cacklings about a new
hat. It is reported that the ladies’ committee to
which Premier Price alluded intends to take
“ further action ” on account of his remarks—not
about its own morals, on which no reflection was
cast, but only about those of some men whom its
members admit to their acquaintance. If all the
Labor men who have been directly accused by
Tory dames of much worse doings—of immoral
designs on the “marriage tie,” and soon —had
taken “further action,” by summons or other-
wise, there would have been a very hysterical
time in well-dressed circles.
