The Men of the Melville Island.
Every day now produces some fresh development
in the astounding Melville Island case; but still
up to time of writing Captain Larkin refuses
with irritating obstinacy to deal justly with his
men, and, despite solicitor Joe Woolf’s perti-
nacity, nobody seems to be found whose business
it is to hammer some regard for law and justice
into the Bluenose skipper’s head. After promising
the shipping master that he would pay the men
off at Melbourne, after making appointment after
appointment for that purpose and failing to keep
them, this Nova Scotian sea-lawyer calmly turned
round and said that he had changed his'
mind and would not discharge his crew at all.
He went to each man separately, and for-
mally ordered him to return to the ship and
start work again. The men refused, and then
the captain’s solicitors — Malleson, Stewart,
Stawell and Nankivell —wrote to Woolf, an-
nouncing their intention of arresting the men
under a new section of the Merchant Shipping
Act. The men were originally hauled before
Panton, P.M., under a section which allowed
them to be brought before a court and only
authorised their conviction if they could show no
lawful excuse for absence from the ship. Now
this remarkable ship captain has found a section
which, he thinks, gives him power to cart the
men on board ship without reference to any court,
and set them to work. Woolf’s counter move
was to apply to the Vice-Admiralty Court—
which takes precedence over all others
in marine matters —for a writ attaching the
ship on behalf of one of the seamen. A
warrant was issued, and the marshal of the
court went to Queenscliff, affixed the writ
and warrant to the mainmast of the vessel, and
left two bailiffs on board. The same day a Cus-
toms weigher arrived at Queenscliff and sealed up
all the ship’s stores. He took an inventory of
the stores on board (a poor, miserable lot they
were), and demanded duty on all that had been
used since the vessel came into Australian waters.
Here again the conspiracy of inertness that has
helped this tyrannical captain was made evident.
The Melville Island cast anchor off Queenscliff on
May 14. It came from a foreign port —Mollendo,
in Peru—but the Customs never entered it in,
never went aboard it, never sealed up its stores,
never took the slightest official cognisance of its pre-
sence until Woolf found time to prod the depart-
ment into action. The order to review Panton’s
decision came before Justice CUSSEN, but was
adjourned for some technical reason. In the
meantime, Woolf has taken out 18 other orders
nisi to review decisions of Panton’s regarding
the Melville Island men. When the first charge
against the men came before Panton, there
were two other sets of cases listed, in which
all the nine sailors charged the captain
with supplying improper and insufficient food on
the voyage, and also applied to the court to
rescind their contract with the ship. The incredible
Panton convicted the men on the captain’s case
against them, without allowing them to come into
court and hear the evidence against them, and
without listening to any defence. Then, as regards
the men’s cases against the captain, he nodded his
godlike topknot and said, “ All dismissed.” The
cases were not even called, and the men were no
more allowed to state their case against the cap-
tain than they had been allowed to state their
defence against that Bluenose tyrant. But the
worst feature of the whole business is the flat-
footed inactivity of the Police Department. There
is nothing in the Merchant Shipping Act on
which more stress is laid than on the harboring of
deserters. Now, the police actually arrested two
Russian deserters aboard the Melville Island.
They had reason to believe that two other deser-
ters were still on board at a later date (so far as
is known there is no one in particular on board
now. for the new crew has left without its wages ;
it complained that it hadn’t been put on the
ship’s articles, and so had only the captain’s bare
word for its wages, and as he refused to give more
than that nude verbal assurance there was another
exit). And not only does the Police Department
make no move to enforce the law against the
captain, but the Chief Commissioner has, it is
said, stopped proceedings initiated by the
police at Woolf’s pertinacious instigation.
Nothing that has ever happened in last 20
years has brought Victoria into more contempt
than this Melville Island business. It holds that
State up to scorn as a place where the whole
machine of Government is so paralytic or
so corrupt that the skipper of a twopenny
sailing ship with a little decayed beef on board
can, by mere bluff, “ hold up ” the Police Courts,
the Supreme Court, and the Police Department,
can defy the shipping laws, can dodge the Customs
for a whole month, and can make a jest of things
generally. The poorest half-breed republic in
Central America would have done better.
The Australian Tory Press on Seddon.
Sydney Telegraph described Premier Seddon,
after .his death as “ the most notable Australian
statesman of his time,” and almost every other
Tory paper in Australia spoke in a very similar
strain. Yet if those papers had the courage of
their alleged opinions, and if they believed the
things that they tell the Australian public every
day, they would have rather stated that Richard
Seddon was the most notorious political quack,
charlatan, humbug, thief, confiscator, and repu-
diator of his day and generation, and that his
existence was a public calamity. If there is any
reasonable semblance of truth in what the Tele-
graph, the S.M. Herald, the Argus, the Ade-
laide Register, and a number of other papers
daily allege, then Seddon was such a monster
of folly and iniquity that the common
sayitig, “of the dead speak nothing but
good,” no more applied m his case than
it did to Deeming after he was hanged, or to
Cain, Gehazi, Judas Iscariot, C/Esar Borgia,
Henry VIII. or any one of a hundred others
on whose graves the public has poured centuries
of loose and general execration. All his political
life Seddon occupied himself in doing the things
which the Telegraph and papers of its kind
declare to this day are the pinnacle of folly, the
sum of political sin, the short road to ruin, and
the wild inspirations of untaught fanatics who
fool with things of which they know nothing—-as a
lunatic or adrunk might doif he were leftalone with
a steam engine and amused himself turning all the
handles at once. Unless every one of Australia’s
Tory papers is a liar or a fool, Seddon must have
dragged Maoriland down to the lowest depth of
poverty and misery, and it must have been glad
when he died, and the rest of the world must have
been glad likewise, out of sympathy. Yet the Tele-
graph and its unwholesome friends admitted that
he left Maoriland prosperous. And the Telegraph
went further, and called him a “ statesman, ’
which is something vastly higher than a mere
politician. Dor once it spoke with a fair measure
of truth, but if Seddon was a statesman, no term
of contempt is low enough for the Telegraph.
And if Seddon’s policy was statesmanship,
then a lump of mud the size of a cathedral isn’t
large enough to throw at the Telegraph’s policy,
which is in everything the reverse of Seddon’s,
and, by the Telegraph’s showing, the reverse of
statesmanship. The visit of Richard of Kumara
drove the Tory papers of Australia to take notice
of him—to admit that this man, who did every-
thing that they said was small, mean, foolish
and despicable in politics, was a man greater
than his fellows, and to say that this leader
who did everything they said was ruinous,
disastrous, and calculated to drive men and
capital from the country was politically an enin-
ently successful man, and one who had made his
country a notable success. They said these things
with renewed emphasis after his death. And
then they renewed the old business of denouncing
Deakin, Watson, Isaacs, and their friends for
proposing to do some of the things that Seddon
the “statesman” did. The eposode shows the
Tory press at its true valuation.

The “Argus” on the Advisability of Being
Dishonest.
Duncan Gillies, of Victoria—old, borrowing,
begging Duncan who put a mortgage on every-
thing in sight—is not dead but sleepeth. It used
to be a great sight, some 16 years ago, when
Duncan would rise with a bland smile on the
round, red, pleasant, hairless countenance that
always shone as if it had been washed with soft
soap at the kitchen sink, to tell a new tale of
boom, and propose a new loan; and in spirit
Duncan is still rising. Like John Brown, his
soul is marching on. Gillies represented the
invariable financial corruption of Toryism, and
Toryism is still the same. Witness this recent
yelp from Melbourne Argus :
INCOME TAX.
OBJECTIONS TO “SURPLUSES.”
EFFECT ON INVESTMENT.
There is no necessity for the income-tax in the present
condition of the State finances. It is our depression tax,
our drought tax, an impost, perhaps, to be re-enacted
should such times unfortunately recur. The question of
its repeal is one for the people. If this opportunity be
allowed to go by, the fears expressed in some quarters
when the Act was passed will be fully realised. The
income-tax will have “ come to stay.”
Now Victoria hasn’t had one real surplus yet,
though some things it has had have been so much
more real than the average that they have 1 een
called surpluses by courtesy. Victoria had a cour-
tesy surplus last year and an unusually good one.
Premier Bent put up an almost unparalleled record
for honesty by making a small reduction in the
public debt, and a little was, paid off the
accumulated deficit. The rest mostly went to
repair assets, paid for out of loans, which had
been allowed to depreciate till they only
represented a half or a tenth of the loan money.
The next courtesy surplus is to go in further
reduction of the debt and in more repairs like
those of last year. Every one knows well enough
that several millions of nominal surplus are
required before Victoria has any real net surplus.
These millions are required to wipe off the
accumulated deficit; and to pay off enough per-
manent debt, and to sufficiently repair existing
loan assets or to sufficiently replace vanished loan
assets, to again put the State in the com-
paratively honest position of having fair
value in assets to show for its loans
All the prospective surplus this year wouldn’t
suffice to wipe off one old swindle alone —the bogui
assets of the Railway Department, consisting of
Downing-Street.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australia and Maoriland would be much happier if they
were placed beyond the reach of Downing-street alto-
gether. (Applause.) In the New Hebrides, the cause of
the trouble was the supineness of the British Govern-
ment. . . . One thing had happened so often that he was
almost tired of it. Despatches were sent to him, and to
the Prime Minister of Australia, time after time, marked
“Confidential” and “Secret.” . . . What were they afraid
of? They said that it would prejudice their position
with a friendly nation. The fact was that it prejudiced
Australia’s position.— Appallingly disloyal remarks from
Seddon, reported with fear and trembling by the Argus.
Dream and doze—while the Empire fails.
This is the motto of Downing-street.
Worship the King and the Prince of Wales,
Loyally crawl and lick their feet.
What do things matter ? We’ve got the Fleet.
Why should we trouble ? We’ve got the cash.
What do we care if things go smash ?
That is the method of Downing-street.
“ We are all right if we’re left alone
Here in our offices snug and neat;
Note our respect when we speak of ‘The Throne’;
See our despatches—a perfect treat!
Lovely chirography, sheet on sheet,
Full of grave platitudes, sealed and tied,
Scattered abroad through the world so wide.”
These are the answers of Downing-street.
“ Out on the beastly Colonies’ ‘ needs’ !
Why do they seek us in our retreat?
Why do they urge us to action and deeds?
Why do they ask an impossible feat ?
Ah, but our life is so pleasant and sweet!
Why can’t they see it and leave us alone ?
Haven’t they got any pride of their own ? ”
Such are the comments of Downing-street.
“ Here we are, ruling them, don’t you know,
Saving the Empire in Downing-street;
Who are these persons who call US ‘ slow ’ ?
How dare they criticise Britain’s elite ?
Really such language is hardly meet
When it’s applied to fine gentlemen
Related, of course, to the Upper Ten !”
This is the feeling in Downing-street.
“ What have we done to be worried and bored ?
Why do they write with such rudeness and
heat?
Why don’t their letters begin with ‘My Lord,’
Instead of plain ‘Sir’? Why don’t they repeat
The styles and the titles wherewith they should
greet
Britain’s ‘ great governing families,’ eh ?
It must be their beastly familiar way ! ”
Such are the questions in Downing-street.
“ Why do they harp on mere trifles, like trade?
Why do they say that our rule is effete ?
Why do they think that we’ve never displayed
Interest in them ? It’s most indiscreet!
Haven’t we sent them a Prince to greet
The Commonwealth —whatshername ? Where
is it ? Eh ?
Yes, it’s gone three ; I must hurry away ! ”
That is the motto of Downing-street!
VIC.
O. C. CABOT.

The Anti-Trust Bill.
The Foreign-trade agony over the Anti-Trust Bill
has its amusing- side. If the Foreign-trade advo-
cates are to be believed, their greatest objection to
Protection is that.it will encourage the growth of
rapacious local monopolies which will ravage pro-
ducers and consumers alike. That Freetrade
encourages the growth of foreign monopolies like
the Beef Trust is, of course, not referred to. A
monopoly finds absolution from all sins in the
fact tliat it is foreign. But, to meet the
objection that a high tariff, in addition to
stopping the ravages of the foreign monopoly,
may encourage local Trusts, the Deakin Govern-
ment (in preparation, let us hope, for a high
tariff) has brought down an Anti-Trust Bill
which, if enacted; will prevent monopolies and
combinations so far as legislative effort can do so.
And the Bill meets with frenzied Foreign-trade
opposition. There is no welcome for this measure
which is to do away with the possibility of the
more or less real evil which is said to follow on
the heels of Protection. It converts no Foreign-
trader whose previous antagonism to Protection
arose from “no hostility to local manufactures
but from fear of the up-rise of Trusts as in the
United States.” But it makes the Foreign-trade
press as furious as a trapped dingo, arid Conroy’s
remarks simply are not fit for publication. Here
is a brief summary of the Bill which threatens to
drive the anti-Socialist party to the mad-house :
Anybody joining a Trust designed to injure the public
or to destroy an Australian industry is a criminal.
Penalty, £5OO or J ug.
Anybody trying to injuriously monopolise any branch
of trade in Australia is a criminal. Penalty, £5OO or Jug.
Goods must not be dumped, i.e, , imported at unfair com-
petition prices, to the injury of Australian manufactures.
The Minister has power, after full investigation of the
facts by a Board, to stop such importations. The goods
simply go to Jug.
There is nothing unfair in this. It is all directed
against unfairness in others. To be a practical
safeguard against the shifty devices of Trusts and
Combines, the Bill has to be drawn on a broad
and sweeping scale. Otherwise, loopholes would
be found to make it ineffective. But ample
provision is made against any tyrannous use of the
Bill’s wide powers. The Minister for Customs and
the Attorney-General, in the exercise of the pro-
posed Act, are checked by the officials of the
Customs Department, by special Boards to be
created to investigate cases of dumping, and by
Parliament and the law courts. There is no
real danger of any injustice unless there is pre-
sumed a corrupt department, a corrupt legisla-
tion, and a debauched High Court. But, of
course, it is not the fear of injustice that worries
the Foreign-traders. It’s the dread of justice, and
the fear of losing a handy excuse for hostility to
Australian industry.
Bushido.
About our noble ally the Jap:—
There are some useful lessons to be gleaned from the
story of the brutal ill-treatment of Australian citizen
Weigall and his wife in Corea bv Japanese soldiery last
December. Weigall, who is a son of the headmaster of
Sydney Grammar School, is a mining engineer, and was
travelling through Corea on business. At Hein-Tchieg they
—he, his wife, and his assistant —were accosted by some
Jap soldiers. One soldier used “the lowest form of
language in framing his questions,” his manner was “ ex-
ceedingly rough and insulting,” and, on addressing Mrs.
Weigall, he referred to her as “ that thing,” and pointed
to her with his foot. Then, when Mr. Weigall produced
for his benefit the passports of the party, they were handed
round amongst the crowd collected about the house to be
dirtied, torn or lost, as the case might be.
Soon after, the three white people (Mr. Weigall, his
wife, and Taylor, his engineering assistant) left the town,
but had scarcely covered two miles when they were
startled by cries of “ Yobo, yobo,” and, on looking back,
saw three Japanese soldiers advancing at a trot. The
Wiegalls’ both dismounted, but continued to go forward
as before. Taylor still stuck to his horse and rode slowly
in the rear. The first soldier caught the engineer by the
arm, while the second made a pass at the assistant with
a fixed bayonet. Taylor was quick enough to save his
life, and the steel point missed going through his body by
half an inch, whereupon the maddened beast who had
wielded it turned his attention to Mrs. Wiegall, swinging
her round at the length of his arm, and, finally, striking
her a violent blow full on the breast. Mrs. Wiegall
staggered into her husband’s arms. Then this chivalrous
child of “ The Coming Power ” threw back the breech of
his gun, and, in another moment, would have blown both
man and woman out of the world had not a Wiser
brother stayed his hand before it was too late.
Then came a march back to the magistrate’s quarters,
outside of which Mrs. Wiegall was kept for 35 minutes
in a freezing atmosphere without a rug or cloak, while,
in order to pass the time more pleasantly, one of the
Monkey men took upon himself to behave “ grossly inde-
cently before the unfortunate lady.” After a long delay
the party were ranged into “an open shed, where
criminals are tried,” and where justioe (?) was found in
the shape of a Japanese officer. A long list of questions
and answers were then given, and the opportunity of in-
sulting Mrs. Weigall was again taken to the fullest
advantage. The members of the party then were rudely
ordered away from the premises and told to continue
their journey.
As soon as possible Mr. Weigall sent a complaint to
the British Legation at Seoul. It referred the matter to
the Japanese Minister, and he referred it, in his turn, to
one General Hasegawa. The two latter offered “ expres-
sions of regret as sufficient settlement of the case.”
And now Mr. Weigall is trying to force the hand of a
lackadaisaical Ambassador from Great Britain (by com-
munication with the Foreign Office in London), that he
may secure reparation for the abominable assault made
upon his wife and himself. But it is not likely that
Britain will chance unpleasantness with its blessed
“ ally ” to compel the offensive Japs to give satisfaction.
And Australia is in no position to speak for itself—un-
happy Australia with its blind and stupid neglect of even
the rudiments of national defence.
♦
It remained for Melbourne ’Erald — a journal of
intense culchaw—to reprove the irreverent Vic-
torians who attend public meetings with a view to
addressing Premier Bent as “Tommy.” The
term “Tommy,” from the reporter’s point of
view, “ belittles the people who have placed the
gentleman so named at their head. Contemptuous
banter of the holder of the chief office in the
public gift deprives the givers of that dignity
which a free people should be careful to
sustain.” But, on the other hand, “Tommy”
may be quoted as evidence that Bent
belittles a most honorable office by so
bearing himself as to invite banter. No Premier
in the Commonwealth has had a better chance
than Bent to rise superior to the familiar appel-
lation he earned in his early political life. When
he was elected Speaker of the Vic. Assembly
years ago he belittled his elected electors by con-
tinuing to act the “Tommy” instead of playing
the “Mr. Bent.” Parliament conferred upon
him the symbols -wig and mace—of the First
Commoner in Victoria, yet he remained one of
the commonest. The office had lots of dignity.
Bent had none. As Speaker he was still called
“ Tommy.” Effect followed cause, as in the case
of King William IV., whose pomp and
circumstance couldn’t protect him from the
contemptuous nickname of “Silly Billy.”
If ex-Speaker Bent can’t command, a feeling of
general public respect for Premier Bent —which
would entail popular disapproval of the “Tommy”
that wounds the Herald’s national pride—then
the fault is in the man. A Premier addicted to
the singing of snatches of comic songs, and the
babbling of reminiscences on the platform, invites
all the banter he gets.

“ Binnacle ” writes :
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers has weird ideas about
Steamships. On Friday last be informed the papers that
the tonnage of shipping on the Sydney register was woe-
fully deficient, caused by the action of the Labor party in
insisting on a half-yearly survey instead of the annual
one that suffices in some other ports. Incidentally,
Carruthers dropped this pearl of information :
“ The present law also had the effect of sending all the
work away from Mort’s dock to other places. The ships
had to be surveyed at the port where they were registered,
and a certificate had to be obtained from the marine sur-
veyor of that port, and of course the repairs and altera-
tions were done in the port.”
The man might have done the thing properly and said
that ships had to be surveyed in the Customs House
because they were registered in the Customs House!
Wonder if he ever thinks ! If so, please ask him to work
this out. The Canadian-Pacific steamers are registered
in London. So are the China’s Co.’s boats, and the E.
and A, None of these vessels ever go nearer London than
Vancouver or Hongkong. How do they get surveyed?
Huddart Parker’S Victoria has just spent thousands of
pounds in Mort's Hock being surveyed. It is registered
in Melbourne. How did it manage to evade the law?
Carruthers apparently does not know that the Adel-
aide boats, registered in Adelaide, are surveyed in
Sydney, and so are many of the Union Company's (all
registered in Dunedin). The port of registry is of little
importance, and, as far as survey goes, has no more to
do with this subject than Willis has.
Wellington (Maoriland) has been eating fish for
breakfast, dinner and tea ; for one Moore has got
up and talked some about Wellington’s meat
supply. He alleged that inspectors’ palms were
oiled so that diseased carcases would slip through
their hands, and that there was as big a percen-
tage of diseased meat going through the slaughter-
houses in Wellington as in Chicago. His allega-
tions have not yet been thoroughly gone into, but
in the face of the public’s cusses, the council will
have to investigate matters. There are no muni-
cipal abattoirs, -and, while the larger concerns are
well enough managed, inquiry shows that the
smaller ones are practically under no supervision,
and do just as they like." One or two inquiring
individuals give some of their experiences. At
one boiling-down factory a rat, covered with sores,
was climbing along a rafter, and was promptly
knocked over with a lump of wood by one of the
men. It fell into the soup, so to speak, and was
stewed up along with the rest of the savory
morsels, the nice; greasy, yellow fat from which is
carefully preserved for the biscuit manufacturers !
Another found, on visiting a piggery near the
city, that 30 or 40 of the darlints were feeding on
the diseased body of a horse which had been
condemned by the inspector. Some had managed
to work right inside the body, and were having a
gorgeous time at the entrails. Dairy fed pork !
It turns out to be a common thing for the
porkers to be fed on the garbage of Wellington.
One ex-managei of a bacon-curing factory relates
how, some time back, he started to condemn all
pigs suffering from tuberculosis. But “ the
directors commenced to ‘squeak’ at the loss,”
and he was told he must utilise them, diseased or
not, as the other factories were doing so. Butchers
are a filthy crowd • seemingly the world over.
There are exceptions—many exceptions—of course,
but the average man whose business it is to
be always slushing round among blood and
entrails gets the stain of them into his soul.
“ Snob ” : “ Gent’s evening dress shoes ; price in
England, 245. per dozen pairs.” The sample has
just been submitted to me as a boot buyer. I
pulled the thing to pieces. Uppers, blank oiled
baize (commonly known as black American oil-
cloth) ; soles, papier machi, with a shaving of
leather pasted over ; heels ditto, with last lift of
leather nearly |in. thick. Uppers glued on to
soles ; backs strengthened with cardboard; lining,
cotton, thin enough to strain Reid’s political
principles through. But the shape of this iniquity
was A 1; and it looked a clinking line to sell at
3s. lid. retail. The unfortunate devil who put a
pair on his feet, though, would be down on his
uppers before the first set of Lancers was finished.
Meanwhile, the Australian factory —which should
be supplying him with decent shoes at 7s. 6d. and
10s. 6d.—is mostly idle, waiting till the truth
soaks through the public’s thick hide. Germany’s
tariff, by the way, is death to this shoddy—the
duty is calculated on the weight of the boots.

Miss Marie Narelle started her Australian
tour inost appropriately with “ The Exile’s Re-
turn.” There’s one fact that the Hibernian exile
can lay with unction to his soul, and that is that
the Irish exile mostly returns in triumph to her,
or his, native land whether that land be this or
the one that is padlocked, against its will, to Eng-
land. “ There came to the beach a poor exile of
Erin ; the dew on his thin robe was heavy and chill.
Ere the steamer that bore him had passed out of
hearin’, he was Alderman Mikeinthrojucin’ aBill.”
However, that’s by the way, and just to remind
the Sassenach that the .Irish govern in other,
countries if not in their own. Miss; Narelle, in
Irish ballads of pathos,; has so piercing a sweet-
ness and i so.,deep-seated an appreciation of the
sentiments she enunciates in song, that; in the
really sympathetic listener she causes mental dis-
turbance. Many of her audience were in tears,
and tears, as even a mullet might know, are some-
times more subtle flattery than praises shouted
ALMA GRAY (formerly “ Little Alma Gray,’
of Australia) in “My Little Bush Girl”
(London).
Photo, by Hama, London.
to the concave vault of heaven. This writer
would have thrown her string of pearls at
the singer for her singing of “ Believe Me,
Me of All ” only she has doubts of their being
genuine gems. (They cost Is. Bd.) “ The Last
Rose of Summer ”jwas another warmly received
number, and in “ Y ou’d Better Ask Mama ” Miss
Narelle, though as coy as an early Victorian girl,
added richness and vigor. As for the Jewel Song
from Faust, no one cares whether Miss Narelle
can sing the florid, squealy squally number well
or not. Anyhow, it’s not a good concert number,
but the soprano treatment of it pleased a section
of the audience. With “ O’Donnell Aboo,” Miss
Narelle bowed herself out from a successful pro-
gramme and an enthusiastic audience.
* * ■* *
Tenor Chester Fentress —his own name,
not transplanted out of a girl writer’s first
novel —is a fine contrast to Miss Narelle in color-
ing. Her hair is as black as the darkness of a
tarred African at midnight. His hair and com-
plexion (yes, he has a complexion, yet he doesn’t
look effeminate) are like those of Percy Grainger
—a fairness that is almost golden. Also in face
and figure and coloring he’d pass for a twin brother
of Nance O’Neil. Like that lovely giantess he is
a Californian. Mr. Fentress took his musical train-
ing in Paris, and he does Paris proud, for his style is
as finished as a polished precious stone, and his
voice is of pure though not of powerful quality.
The Californian is on the rails for a sure and easy
success in the Commonwealth. Miss Constance
Brandon Usher presided over the ivories with her
usual brilliancy. The remaining Narelle concerts
of this season here will occur to-night (Thursday)
and Saturday next.
“For England” gives way to “InSight
of St. Paul’s” at Sydney Royal on Saturday.
“For England” is not at all the best in Bland
Holt’s list, yet it has done excellent business. In
Sydney there is a steady demand for sound melo-
drama, and with Holt it really is only a question
of putting on pretty good plays to crowded houses,
or rather better plays and being fined for over-
crowding. The “ St. Paul’s” revival will probably
be followed by something quite new.
** * *
The crowded houses which greet “The
Little Michus ’’ at Her Majesty’s, these times, are
probably in part due to the desire of Sydney to
give a cordial farewell greeting to Miss Margaret
Thomas. Seldom has a sweeter, more artistic
singer than this winsome little lady been heard in
light opera in Australia. Her manner recalls
Violet "V arley, though her voice is of much higher
quality. Apart from farewell greetings, however,
“ The Michus ” earns warm approval on its merits.
Miss Florence Young as the other Little Michu
makes much rollicking humor, and Lauri, as the
gouty old general, proves that his capacity for
fun-making is still fresh and strong. In such
parts as are provided by “The Orchid” The
Bulletin confesses to finding this comedian a
little same and tame now and again. But given
any sort of a chance to develop on new and
original lines and Lauri shows himself a first-class
source of mirth.
* -*■ * -*-
Czerny, magician, comes to the Sydney
Criterion on Saturday, “The Man from Mexico”
departing, after a very successful season, for Mel-
bourne. Czerny is under the management of
William Anderson, and Maoriland reports speak
highly of his power to represent the things that
are not, and to make the things that are look like
the circumstances that never were, and to cause
the present to seem to be the past or the future,
and to dig out the fourth dimension of space from
its lair, and the myth, the chimera, and the
delusion from their hiding places.
-* * JK- *
Miss Leila Doubleday, aged 12, whose
photo appears in this issue, is to give a concert at
the Y.M.C.A. Hall, Sydney, on the 80th. The
child’s recent performance at Melbourne Town
Hall as a pianiste and violiniste exceeded the
expectations of everybody save the few who had
heard her on one or two previous occasions, ere her
age had advanced to double figures. The Sydney
concert is part of a scheme for raising £lOOO to
take her to Europe in charge of her mother, for
five years, and if Sydney desires to hear the
gifted and winsome little Leila in the short-
frocked stage of her career it had better seize this
only opportunity. At: the Melbourne concert she
sat down to the piano before standing up to the
violin, but in Sydney the order of things will be
reversed, and the audience will be practically
asked to express an opinion as to which instru-
ment the young artist should take up for good
and all—not that she will ever be able to take up
a piano, except in a figurative way of speaking.
** * *
The boy comedian who tells a smoking-
room sort of story with a leer that is most unboy-
like, prompts the thought that a good cuffing is
the proper greeting. There is nothing more pro-
voking than juvenile precocity in the peppered
story line. Writer confesses to feeling that way
towards “Little Cliff,” who appeared at Sydney
Tivoli for the first time last Saturday ; but only
for a while. He felt that he wanted to biff Cliff,
and that he would like to give Little Cliff
no small biff. But the boy—he’s only a kiddie
—in time compels admiration. He is such
a clever comedian and such a wonderfully
comic dancer. “Little Tich the adult' man
in a boy’s size body—is inevitably suggested
by this precocious youngster, who really is
in some respects quite his equal. Cliff sings, not
imitatively, but with a humor of his own, and his
dancing is full of quaint fun. He will be a Boom.
Miss Lilieth Leyton (assisted by Mr. Harry Ken-
dal) is another new turn at the Tivoli in a comedy
“The Order of the Bath,” which is billed as
having been “ patronised by King Edward.” If
he enjoyed it, Ned VII. is an easily-amused
monarch, for the comedy has little of wit or fun.
A girl goes to the bath and is followed there, in-
advertently, by her Johnny lover, a British army
captain. The door slams on him, and they are
alone. Except that the British officer wears an
eye-glass to his bath—that may be a habit of the
British aristocracy—-there is very little to laugh
at. A well-varied bill is filled out with the
Stavordale Quintette, the O’Natis jugglers,
Omega, wire- walker,_ and Blake and Granby (in
a farce), and other items.
m* * *
The Fisk Jubilee Singers—who have
wandered over the earth so long and changed
their composition so often that the original Fisk
is hardly even a memory now, and yet have
preserved a high level of excellence all the time —
open in Sydney Centenary Hall on June 30.
With them comes a “ Thaumaturgic tableau ”
which is presumably just a biograph—with pic-
tures of the burning of what was once San
Francisco, and with other novelties. Every show
seems to bring along a biograph nowadays.
** * *
“On the Quiet,” the farce with which
William Collier and co. are making their second
bid for popularity at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne),
is a more rough-and-tumble piece than “The
Dictator,” and when it got fairly going on Satur-
day night the house started to roar. Moreover,
the roar was almost constantly in evidence until
the finish. Described as a fun-problem in three
acts, “ On the Quiet” belies its title after Act 1,
and becomes “On the Riot.” All the men,
women and things move fast and furiously, with
the exception of a large, slow-witted British duke
who has married a Yankee heiress, and loves the
girl better than he loves her dollars, dashitall !
If he had the least sense of humor he would prefer
the dollars, but his eye-glass prevents him seeing
a joke. He walks seriously amidst the madness—
a strange duke in a strange land—a meritorious
product of deplorable social conditions, imparting
just one touch of nature to a hustling American
show. Perhaps the duke is the reason why the
show was called a “ problem.”
* * * *
William Collier, who is rarely off the stage,
plays the supernaturally cute New-Yorker of the
piece, the hero with the iron nerve and the
irresistible audacity. At the outset he is involved
in complications which have a savor of melo-
drama. He wants to marry the duchess’s sister
and co-heiress with the consent of the family,
but the ruling family authority and chief trustee
of its millions is a snobbish brother who blows
cold on Collier, and would rather “sell” his
remaining spinster sister to a titled person, as he
is said-to have sold the duchess. It subsequently
transpires that the duchess is quite happy with
her duke; but if this fact were admitted in the
beginning, the audience’s sympathies would be
somewhat turned against Collier. He is a
heartlessly comic suitor with a record for

between his love-sick sister and her Collier,
on the understanding that the inveterate comedian
in question will go back to college and complete
his education during the next four years. Collier
meets the difficulty by persuading the devoted

mostly happen again at the hotel and get into a

duke. In the last act the confusion is shifted to
Collier’s yacht, where the dissenting trustee
arrives and is bluffed into demanding an instant
marriage between the gay deceivers who are
married already. Being now in full possession of
his twenty-million-dollar wife, Collier scores a
final joke off the duke as the curtain falls.
In a farce that is much more farcical than
“The Dictator,” the leading man appears to no
less advantage than before. There’s no oil in his
humor, nor any milk of human kindness, but as a
“droll ” of the purest American quality Collier
stands for excellence. Miss Taliaferro, the quiet
little girl of the previous production, is a brighter
and sweeter ingenue in this, but clever John Barry-
more has no chance to do more than show his
agility at boisterous moments. Grant Stewart’s
artistic sketch of the harmless duke is as con-
vincing as his sketch of the blustering General
Campos in “The Dictator.” Some of the other
members of the co. are not so well fitted to their
parts this time, and both the duchess and the
angry brother fall short of reasonable require-
ments.
At the Princess’s the days of “Little
Mary” were longer than six in the land, and
Barrie’s comedy pointed its moral to a large audi-
ence on a second Saturday, the revival of “ A
Village Priest” being held over. To-night
(Thursday) the season of the Brough-Flemming
co. is to conclude with “Quality Street,” and on
Saturday comes “The Man from Mexico.” Mel-
bourne is prepared to rush the forthcoming festi-
val. It has a welcome of great warmth in readi-
ness for Hugh Ward and Willoughby, and its
heart is full of joy at the thought of Grace
Palotta. The reception that awaits “The Man
from Mexico ” would be fiist as hearty if he were
coming from Jericho.
** * *
The present and particularly foolish thing
at Melbourne Royal will be withdrawn from
circulation on Friday, therefore anybody desirous
of witnessing British jingodrama in its last stage
of imbecility must buy a ticket for “Death or
Glory Boys” to-morrow night, at the latest. A
comparatively brilliant entertainment, “Hands
Across the Sea,” is announced for Saturday.
Henry Pettitt was responsible for “Hands
Across,” and it’s a pretty good melodrama that
can achieve a revival fifteen years after the
author’s decease. At any rate, it is vastly superior
to the works of many modern authors who ought
never to have been born.
** * *
The change in the Opera House bill this
week is a very decided improvement. Homer
Mason and the beauteous Miss Keeler, assisted by
a third party, have taken over the comedy-sketch
business, which was previously in a languishing
condition. “ Hooked by Crook ”is a new treat-
ment of an old idea, and Mason has given popular
approval a good hard knock.

Hackenschmidt is just now downing the
Terrible Turk in a West’s Picture at the
Athenaeum Hall; also, Vesuvius is erupting, and
the Brescians have a new musical programme.
Unfortunately, the Athenaeum Hall continues to
be the same old place as before.

“ The State Fair,” a tented show devised
by dentist Dr. Russell, of Melbourne, who lately
took it for a brief Victorian tour, is now in course
of shipment to Noumea, where visiting shows
have hitherto been of less account than this one.
Impressive posters, printed in French, warn the
Noumeans to be prepared for amazing attractions
on the first Sunday of next month, and among
names of the artists appears “Dtino —sorcier,
prestidigitateur, &c.”—inanagerially known as le
Docteur Russell aforesaid. His partner in this
foreign expedition is J. A. Miller, the printer
responsible for the postewr en deux couleurs,
begorra ! Le Bulletin seizes the opportunity to
wish them un tres bon temps.
** * m
Miss Tittell Brune finished her Adelaide
season with “Sunday,” which is probably her
best part after the cold-blooded young woman in
“ The Light That Failed.” Cuyler Hastings used
to play Jacky, but Kingston prefers the part of
the English lover. This co. is a very strong one
all round. Atholwood and Mervale had their
chances before, and now Staveley, Maurice
Dudley, and T. W. Lloyd all came out strong.
The capable Miss Susie Vaughan has also done
good work ; but somehow Miss Brune’s favorite
pieces do not run much to opportunities for other
actresses. The co. has gone West, and the Royal
has an annoying, unusual vacant week, which
would have been occupied by Collier and the
“Dictator” crowd but for earthquake con-
siderations.
An interesting document was placed upon
the table in the Board Room of the Sydney Hos-
pital at a recent meeting of the Board of Direc-
tors —the original deed of grant of the land on
which the hospital stands, with the transfer from
the original trustees to the Sydney Hospital en-
dorsed thereon. The original hospital—the Rum
Infirmary—of which the Parliament Houses and
the Mint formed a portion, was built by D’Arcy
Wentworth, Garnham Blaxcell and A. Riley at the
instance of Governor Macquarie. In return for
the hospital the trio had the exclusive privilege of
trading in spirits for three years. Lang says that
the spirits were bought by the contractors at 3/-
per gal. and sold at 40/-. The British Secretary
of State severely censured Macquarie for granting
this monopoly, but in a sense it made for temper-
ance. Only the rich could afford to go on a really
large jamboree, while these three hungry pub-
licans ran the business.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Marie Narelle made her debut at a Sunday school con-
cert at Temora (N.S. W.) at the age of eight. Marie thinks
that, next to Australia, America is the best country in the
world. In America she had her biggest successes, she
dined with the President, was entertained by 34 million-
aires, and altogether had a fine time. America is going
to be a great musical country, she thinks, and she advises
Australian warblers, if they must rush away somewhere,
to rush there. The Australian woman will be flattered to
know that she is, in Marie’s opinion, the most domesti-
cated woman in the world.
Ellen Terry’s official jubilee matinee, at Drury Lane
Theatre, London, produced close upon £6OOO. Many
persons waited outside the theatre doors for 24 to 30
hours in order to secure seats in good positions, and were
so dog-tired that when they got the seats they fell asleep
and didn’t know whose jubilee it was.
Died in Melbourne the other week unnoticed by the
press, good old Kreitmeyer, the father of the local Wax-
works. In his prime Kreitmeyer had pretensions to be
called an artist, not on account of the orthodox effigies
that figured in the main hall of the Waxworks, or the
inner Chamber of Horrors, hut for the “anatomical”
modellings which he executed with particular careful-
ness, and of which £. £. Smith has, or had, a choice
collection of specimens. The show place, in Bourke-
street, Melbourne, brought a good deal of money to Kreit-
meyer for many years, and was quite a popular institu-
tion up to the time of Deeming, when Phil. Stuart was
managing the business. Deeming was about the last of
the murderers to draw anything of a daily crowd to the
Chamber of Horrors. He was waxed for purposes of
public detestation long before he was found guilty,
stormful old actor (Newton Griffiths) was engaged by the
management to work himself into a profuse perspiration
asa reciter of the numerous murders, real and imaginary,
that Deeming had committed. Kreitmeyer’s waxen re-
presentation of the “Foul Fiend” planting his wife’s
corpse under the hearthstone was a mild matter compared
with Newton Griffiths’ verbal picture of the event, and
the epithets that issued from his foaming mouth. Heigho !
We don’t have such murders nowadays, as Mr. Parkyn
remarks in “ The Silver King.”
At a recent Adelaide amateur dramatic splash a sort of
tragic comedy happened. A bouquet arrived to be
handed up to Miss Blank after her performance.
It was from an admirer, so the note said, but the
note bore no signature. At the conclusion of the playlet
a waiter awkwardly approached the footlights and niade
the terrible mistake of handing the bouquet, not to Miss
Blank, but to her bosom pal, Miss Blanky. Miss Blanky
blushed and hurriedly passed the blossoms on to Miss
Blank. Then people asked if Miss Blank had sent the
bouquet, to herself, for if it had come to hand quite un-
expectedly how did Miss Blanky know it was for Miss
Blank ?
LEILA DOUBLED AY, the 12-year-old Mel-
bourne girl who will appear this month in
Sydney as a pianist and violinist.
Photo, by Talma, Sydney & Melbourne.
Comedian J. L. Toole, who died (or was reported as dying-)
the other day, had long been a pitiable, paralysed figure
in an invalid chair at Brighton, England. The grimace
which he used to make in his ordinary ancient way of
comedy business had become a fixed grin on the poor old
mummer’s countenance, and people wondered that he
could still delight to appear in public. Toole was nearly
played out, physically and histrionically, when he came
to Australia in 1890. Of all the imported English stars
this country has been asked to accept on their reputation
J.L.T. was the most unquestionable fraud, for his English
reputation at that time barely sufficed to keep him going
in a mere bandbox of a theatre, where his audiences were
mostly country folk with very primitive notions of
humor. Among Londoners his name had been a dimin-
ishing quantity from the time he went into man-
agement and began to appear in new farcical
comedies, written specially for him and his atten-
dant co. Toole was never a new humorist. At
his best he was a mummer of the old broad-comedy
school, with a considerable strength of personality and a
lot of experience in a few parts. His newest characters
were out of date when he visited Australia in ’9O. The
younger generation saw nothing funny in his winks and
nods and baggy breeches, and even his elderly admirers
had to admit that his methods were best adapted to the
antique playlets with which he had been identified for
30 years and more. He made his first appearance as the
Spitalfields Weaver in 1852, and his Caleb Plummer origi-
nated in 1860. These parts he travelled upon all the
years of his stage life, but they were not big enough to
support an overloaded “ reputation.” America saw him
in 1875, before he had begun to grow feeble, and the
Yanks found him tiresome as a rule. Australia saw him
to less advantage, and made something of an effort to
treat him kindly. But the disappointment was inevitable.
“0.K.”: What ‘‘Hiphen” says on the question of
raising money for the technical musical education of an
Australian child in Europe, when she could get a good
deal of advanced training in her own country, may be all
right enough. But public patriotism doesn’t yet run to
a faith in the local educational powers that be. It is use-
less to attempt to raise any considerable sum of money
for the benefit of a promising young artist who isn’t going
to Europe. And “ Hiphen ” overlooks the undeniable
fact that Germany is a better country than Australia for
the student who would hear, at every possible
opportunity, the finest interpretations of the finest
music.
The Payne Family, of Ballarat, well-known in the Vic.
country districts a dozen years ago, has popped-up again
as “the Steel-Payne Entertainers and Bellringers,” and
they are singing and ringing on a somewhat larger scale
than of yore. A sharp-pointed name is half the battle with
a travelling side-show. There’s a hard, cold incisiveness
about “Steel-Payne” which could not be excelled by
Steel-Pen or Brass-Nib.

posed that it would ?
“Hamer”: In “ Pov. Point” (B. 24/5/’O6) there are
remarks about two stage people who hate the stage.
It s a tale that has been told before ; in all probability the
ladies referred to will be seen on the boards again. That
sort of charming hypocrisy arises from a sort of feminine
coquetry that actuates both actors and actresses —the
Resort of coquetry that often makes a girl pretend
a dislike for the boy of her preference. Australia has at
least one actor who came in for a respectable fortune
had been for about twenty years announcing
tnat he hated the stage. And the first thing he did was
to launch a company with himself as star. He is, for all
I know, grumbling about the stage to this day ; but he
nasn t half the cause to do so that the stage has to
grumble about him.
Cockaigne ” : Cyril Keightley, from Australia, made
good appearances with Benson’s Company in the
otratford-on-Aron performances this year, held in honor
of Shakspere’s birthday. He acted Pistol, in “ Henry
, and yet better parts. The performances are endowed,
wherefore Benson was able to produce the whole of the
Plantagenet dramas, except the first part of “Henry IV.”
That is to say he staged “ Richard II.,” “ Henry IV.”
(second part), “ Henry V.,” the three parts of “Henry
and “Richard III.” They were all reeled off
consecutively, one night to each, proving that Thespians
can do a bit of study, even nowadays. There is no record
of the third part of “ Henry VI.” being acted in England
since Shakspere’s era. The first and second parts were
acted. It is alleged that all the three parts have been done
in series in Germany, where playgoers are treated to
Sliaksperian cycles. A German says you cannot under-
stand the good plays without seeing the comparatively
bad ones. Sarah Bernhardt once had the cheek to act
Hamlet in Stratford-on-Avon. For Ellen Terry’s recent
benefit at Drury Lane, London, the programme included
the second act of “Much Ado About Nothing,” in which
Ellen, Kate, Marion, and another Miss Terry were all
billed. One of Ellen’s sisters is dead. The male cast in
the second act of “ Much Ado ” was : Benedick, Beerbohm
Tree ; Claudio, G. Alexander; Don Pedro, F. Terry;
Leonato, H. Vezin ; Don John, H. B. Irving.
“ J.A.C.” : I have received a prospectus of “ The Aus-
tralasian Grand Opera Company, Limited.” It is a pro-
posal made by George Musgrove that confiding Aus-
tralians should furnish him with £4OOO to bring out a
company to perform what he is pleased to call “ grand
opera.” As used in this country the term is a misnomer,
and is a relic of the old Lyster and Cagli days, when any-
thing which was not Offenbach’s “ Grand Duchess,” or
something similar, was dubbed “ grand opera.” “ Mari-
tana” was, and, I presume, is still, “grand opera.” If
the term has any significance at all it is only as applied
to the repertoire of the Paris Thbatre National de l’Opera,
as represented in the big capitals of Europe and the two
Americas. Gounod’s “ Romeo et Juliette,” promised in
the prospectus, is not, technically speaking, “ grand
opera,” although it ought to be ; it belongs to the opera
comique repertoire. But all these are details. What is
wanted is “ good ” opera ; let “ grand ” opera slide. It is
useless to expect a first-class rendition of opera seria—
that is the Italian term, and a much better one than
“grand opera”—here. It won’t run to it. Artists of the front
rank would not come on the mere guarantee of a manager
with £4OOO behind him. All we can hope for is fair second-
class, but even that would be a boon to the true musical
amateurs of Australia. It is a poor job to throw cold
water on any well-intentioned scheme, but the fact
remains that since the late Martin Simonsen, 20 years
ago, brought to Australia his Italian opera company, of
which Madame Ciuti and Signor Lazzarini were the lead-
ing- members, all other attempts have resulted, more or
less, in financial failure. It is true that the polyglot com-
pany which Musgrove brought here some few years back
was not an absolute failure, but that is all that can be
said of it. Musical comedy, admirably represented by J.
O. Williamson for many years, and recently by George
Stephenson—who, by the way, appears to be the only
Australian manager who knows how to spell ballerine—
has too deep a hold on our “ musical ” public to be easily
rooted out.
The Yankee comedy at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne)
threw further light on the “ tipping” infliction, which
Americans protest against when they are abroad, but
accept as a national institution under their own Flag of
Freedom. Brooke Travers, passenger from New York to
Porto Banos on an American vessel, is expected to part a
dollar or two to every blessed serving-man in the ship’s
company'.
Mabel Munro, a 17-year-old Maoriland girl, now playing
with George Edwardes’ Co. in “ The Little Cherub ” at
the Prince of Wales Theatre (London), is a daughter of
G. S. Munro, Executive Commissioner of the M.L. Inter-
national Exhibition. Miss Munro won the Edwardes
Scholarship in 1904 at Barrett’s Lyric and Dramatic
Academy, and the scholarship carried with it an engage-
ment with Edwardes.
One Australian singer made a terrible mistake when he
first arrived in London. At a swell “At Home ”he heard
various vocal efforts which he considered much inferior
to his own. Going up to one of the perpetrators, he
remarked, “ You have a very good voice, indeed. It’s a
great pity you don’t study' a bit.” A very astonished
person fixed him with a resentful eye, and then, instead
of punching his critic, he gave him the metallic laugh
and handed him a card. Two minutes later a very shrunk
vocalist rushed away from that house with a bit of crum-
pled pasteboard in his hand. He had been advising one
of the world’s most famous baritones to take lessons in
singing. (Perhaps the most famous baritone needed
them.)
“Hiphen”: I saw Harry Rickards at his own show
some time ago. He sat in his box with the emotionless
visage of a poker player or a butcher’s chopping block.
Twice a smile almost struggled across his countenance,
but died away again on that weary expanse, and once he
expressed his appreciation by allowing one gloved paw to
fall wearily on the other. After all it must be a tired
thing to go and look at your own show. It suggests the
bush editor who collects his matter himself, writes it him-
self, sets it up himself, reads it in the proofs himself, and
then would sooner fall down dead than read it all over
again in the paper.
Lawrence Barrett, one of America’s best Hamlets, first
played that character under the auspices of a manager
who was used to staging the “ Fiend at the Fowl-house ”
type of drama. The manager regarded the simple title
“ Hamlet ” as too weak altogether, so he billed it as “ The
Grave Burst, or the Ghost’s Piteous Tale of Horror, by W.
Shakspeare, Esq.”
“ Quidam ” : Why doesn’t someone get out a whole
series of real Australian scenes for bioscope business ?
What a wealth of variety' there u'ould be to deal with.
Mining in Kalgoorlie, Bendigo, Gympie, and the Towers ;
ocean liners leaving Circular Quay ; branding, horse-
breaking, or droving out-back ; cutting cane in the field,
the sugar mills, loading bananas at Cairns, pearling at
Thursday Island, and so on. Surely in a country of
wanderers like Australia, such a display would take on
better than the eternal “ Boys in Blue,” the relics of the
Cuban war-boom, and the fake effects such as the
“ Soicerer’s Dream.”

The S.M. Herald tries to be a bigoted
Tory with a conscience, which is much the same
as trying to lie truthfully, or to be an honorable
burglar. The results are curious at times. On
June 13 it published an article in which it
admitted that the Government Land Banks of
various States were so splendidly managed that
they had made no losses, and that—
The expenses had been kept at the lowest limit com-
patible with efficiency.
Then it went down to its dusty lumber-room and
found an axiom :
It is an axiom in prudent financing that the less the
Government has to do with banking the better for the
country.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wade, who is
preparing a Bill to prevent bottles being taken
away without payment, got a shock last week.
The couticil of the Bottle Accumulators’ Associa-
tion called about the Bill, and asked him to bring
it right along. Wade’s deputationists wanted
every retail bottle accumulator licensed at the
rate of £1 a year, and every wholesale aggregator
regulated by law. They also spoke loftily, as
dead marine specialists, of the prospects and
resources of their profession, and Wade could only
gasp that his Bill intended to deal with all
branches of second-hand trade, and that their
claims would have his abject attention.
Henry Carey Dangar’s contemptuous
reference to those who desire to apply legal
restrictions to Dr. Danysz’s microbic experiments
as persons “who own hardly a sheep among
them,” is quite true. A lot of these people own
no animals except themselves; otherwise the
only live-stock they have an interest in are their
families. And they want to be sure that these
animals won’t be endangered. Henry Carey
Dangar, the sheep man with the sheep intellect,
can’t understand how any man who doesn’t own
sheep has a right to lift his voice in the community.
A Parliamentary report in his idea would read
something like this: “Mr. Blank (105,000 sheep)
approved of the motion ” &c.

£3OOO worth of land; also, that soil which the
State had sold to the people thereabouts at 12s. 6s.

to show that the local landowriers' weren’t

there were some very smart rate collectors to be
had if they looked for them ; and that a few
honest valuators were still left in Melbourne ; also
that the evening train left at 5.12, and they would
catch it if they hurried.

through. And the Telegraph yells that the
Deakin Government never intended to goon with
the scheme, and was only forced to it by the
astuteness of the Opposition. The Telegraph’s
evil mind is only fit to be potted as Chicago beef.

Newcastle Harbor now holds between 80
and 100 deep sea ships waiting for coal cargoes, a
sight which cannot be seen in any other port ih
the world. The whole of Ooalopolis is crying to
Government for more rails, more trucks, more
wharves, and more cranes sO that the very bowels
of the city may b'e hi ore expeditiously torn out
and earned Over sea. And when Newcastle is
fiHly disembowelled, Newcastle will fall into the
hole and be happy at last. There are no voices
heard crying out for Australian industries to use
Australian coal in making Australian goods for
Australian people. Instead everyone is fright-
ened of offending the West coast of South America,
or the Straits of Magellan, or the Antarctic Circle
by not sending over the coal in time.

* * * *
The temperance people in Adelaide won
the fight at the recent local option polls, but the
licensed victuallers are making a big skirmish
before the law courts with the object of disquali-
fying Cold Tea on technical points. Symon,
K.C., who is proprietor of the Auldana vine-
yards, is their chief counsel, so that he is being
well paid for battling in his own interests. In
the meanwhile the teetotallers are girding up
their loins with the object of getting Parlia-
mentary authority to turn the bewitching bar-
maid out into the wilderness.
* * * *
Adelaide Police Court lias cleared its dock
of three familiar faces at one blow, sending down
a “habitual drunkard ” for two months, and two
“incorrigible rogues” for a year’s hard labor
each. All three were women ! Two of them had
476 previous convictions between them, and the
P.M. thought they would be just as well where
the beer couldn’t' get at them.

The shepherd roaming o’er the plain,
The stockman on his lonely run,
The teamster with his bullock wain,
The sportsman out with rod or gun,
The digger on gold-bearing reef—-
While firm to duty’s call they stand—
For coughs and colds they find relief
With Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure at hand..

London Missionary Society, which is
one of the many organisations that spend
good money in trying to make savage races
go to church and put threepence in the
plate every Sunday, last year ran itself
into debt to the extent of £17,000. It has
205 men and 71 women working for it, and
those 276 people managed to spend last
year £156,044, or an average of about £11 a
week each, When it is considered that this
is Hot inclusive of the cocoa-nut and yam
contributed by our saved bruddef, it will
be seen that tnissiotiarying ia a fairly
expensive business^

It’s the lady who promptly marries
again when the Divorce Court cuts
tlie first knot. During the last 1 0 years

The Melbourne unemployed were even
more demonstrative in the church of Bevan, the
cherub, than they had been on the previous Sunday
in the Anglican cathedral, and interjected freely.
The lesson they deduced from the observations of
Mr. Bevan—who gets £3O a week for preaching
for about an hour in all to a mere handful of
people—was that their troubles were entirely
their own fault. Dr. Bevan’s idea seems to be
that as we are all free men in a free country, with
equal advantages, every one of us may become a
parson, and get £3O a week, and if we are not all
parsons getting £3O a week it is due to our innate
depravity and bone laziness. If we were all
parsons, the assumption is that we would get the
£3O a week by taking up each other’s collections.

three-panel picture, “The PiO'nfeefs,” to the Mel-

Felton bequest will soon be worth about £IO,POP
a year, so Australian painters who are. worthy
enough ought to have a chance of living in their own
country instead of being obliged to scatter them-
selves over Europe and America. If Australia
could keep at home painters like Gonder, Long-
staff, Streeton, Roberts, Bunny, Fox, &c., it
would soon have as good a record in art as in
sport or public borrowing.

“ W. 8.” : Blank, the much-advertised
preacher (the contributor gives the real name, but
The Bulletin is dispirited through a cold in the
nose this week, and hasn’t the heart for libels) of
(he mentions the city, but owing to a cold in
the ear this paper doesn’t feel its usual yearning for
accuracy)of course may be a good enough missioner,
but he has no sense of the incongruous. A man-o’-
war’s-man named Moses Bunker was drowned
lately in the wind-swept harbor, and the body not
being recovered, Rev. Blank did the best he could
in the way of a really impressive and dramatic
funeral. A boat was moored on the water where
the dead was last seen alive, and a service vvas
held with eloquent perorations something like
this: —
So, crowned with asphodel, and bearing aloft the white
flower of a blameless life, glides into eternal bliss over
the Ely si an fields, the blest spirit of the late departed
Moses Bunker... On behalf of his absent, sorrowing
mother, I place this beautiful floral tribute on the watery
grave of the late lamented Mo.
And then there was a response, for Moses Bunker
rose, and Moses was not good to look upon, after
the long immersion of Bunker, and the funeral
dropped its peroration and fled.
Clairvoyants and spookists find it hard to
earn a crust in Melbourne now-a-days. One of
them has come down to the price of “ fish and
chips,” and advertises :
Free Clairvoyant Character Delineations.—State age,
sex, married, single ; enclose Id. stamps.
The man who replies gets for his 4d. a splendid
character—one that would make Shakspere blush
—and an offer of further beautiful and valuable
information for 2/6. Probably he seldom forwards
the half-crown, and there is mighty little profit in
the 4d. after paying for the stamp, and the paper
and envelope, and inventing the lies.

“H.G.” : A recent Melbourne religious
gathering was graced by the presence of a South
Sea Island missionary, who proudly announced
that he and others of his craft were making
splendid progress in their work, “imparting to
the natives the rudiments of civilisation and
teaching them useful trades.” And yet that
missionary was apparently a good-natured, kindly
man. Like certain leaders of the Inquisition, he
believed that all he did was for the best. Imparti-
ally analysed, it is difficult to conceive a more cruel
and wicked act than that of this purblind altruist
and his accomplices. The rudiments of civilisa-
tion in missionary parlance mean trousers. Every
nigger now walking
about naked and un-
ashamed is to have a
sense of shame drilled
into him, and to be
covered with an un-
comfortable tog. He
is to learn that there
is harm where he
now sees none. His
undeveloped brain is
now to be developed
along the lines that
lead in civilised man
to degeneracy. In
short, he is to be
made to skip over
several thousands of
years in progress, and
start at the point
from which every
thinking civilised
man is trying to
drag humanity back.
Having forced into
the mind of the
nigger that his body
is a thing that must
not be looked at, he
is to be set to work.
The once naked, con-
tented nigger is to be
encouraged in the
task of wearing out
superfluous trousers
and must put in
superfluous labor at
the useless trade of
making superfluous
trousers to be worn
out. These same
niggers, who have
lived all their lives
without more work
than is necessary to
pluck a cocoanut or a
banana from a tree,
are to be driven into
the turmoil of indus-
trial competition,
with an unemployed
problem and all the
other blessings of
civilisation awaiting
them, and with an
army of Fat persons
crying at them for
raw material. Had
Nero, or any other
Pagan, committed,
in the name of Mars or Vulcan, the unspeakable
crime that these well-meaning missionaries are
committing in the South Seas, the awful busi-
ness would have been handed down with
scathing footnotes for every student to ponder
over, but because the crime is committed in the
name of Christianity, nobody, not even the most
advanced guard of the Labor party, is game to get
up and show it to be a whited sepulchre.

T’other day at Nhill (Vic.) a well-known
bagman, while crossing the railway line in a cab,
was struck by a train and killed. The smash was
a tremendous one, cab and man being knocked to
pieces. A crowd swiftly collected round the
scene of the accident, and, with a ghoulish
instinct, gathered up bits of things as mementos.
One man secured the torn and twisted cover of
The Bulletin, which the bagman had been read-
ing. Another seized a boot-heel, another a
crushed match-box. The dead map’s crunched
cigar, a piece of his hat —all sorts of relics were
collected and carried off as prizes. What a liearsey,
tombstoney lot the people of Nhill must be 1
The tale of the short-skirted damsel with
an imaginative mind took an entirely new form
in Melbourne lately. She was a nurse-girl of 19,
and was discovered wheeling her charge in a
“pram,” which had previously been left outside
a shop, and had mysteriously disappeared. Asked
to account for the vehicle, the damsel asserted
that her “boy”—one Roy Goldstein, of St.
George’s-road, Toorak—had been so overcome by
the sight of her carrying a weighty infant that he
had presented her with the pram and his regards.
Also, she produced a long letter from the said Roy
in which he said he had bought the machine
from some unnamed woman in Fitzroy, who was
going to the West, and that he would be happy to
come to court to clear his girl’s character but that
his father assured him such an action would im-
mediately bring on haemorrhage, which would be
fatal. “We have,” he added, “all cleared out
last night on the motor-car.” It was a fairly well
built yarn, and might have passed muster in the
Young Ladies’ Novelette; but the police re-
Sorted that there wasn’t any Goldstein in St.
-eorge’s-road, and the damsel was sent for trial.
“WHEN THE GUNS BEGIN TO SHOOT.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Residents of Watson’s Bay were notified (as usual) to
leave doors and windows open and take other precautions
against calamity, as the big gun at the fort would be fired
on Monday.
Oh, hurry up, Eliza, take the pictures from the walls !
And, Mary, get the carpets off the floors ;
Jemima, wrap the ornaments in blankets, rugs and
shawls;
And, Johnnie, screw the handles off the doors.
Quick, Jennie ! take the beds to bits and put them in the
yard.
What ? Moving 1 No ! but don’t stop talking now.
Go, someone, see the stable doors are bolted, locked and
barred,
And put the bells and hobbles on the cow.
Now wrap the books in wadding; take the mantles off
the gas,
And put the clock in father’s Sunday hat.
The cellar is already packed with crockery and glass,
And likewise with canaries and the cat.
You think I’m going crazy ; but you’d better fly around ;
Throw wide the doors and windows for a run.
You’ll have to get a mile away and lie upon the ground,
For the Government is going to fire a gun.

WAIP.
THE CASH BASIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The church could not do its work unless it was armed
with cash as well as spirituality. Rich members of the
church failed lamentably in mission work— Vietorian
Archdeacon Peacock, at a meeting held to boost the
Melanesian missions.
In pleading for the mission, or
The Church, with rich parishioner,
We praise the Melanesian or
Kanaka with a splash ;
We find in each locality
A growing spirituality,
But wonderful frugality
In cash !
They are not rash !
They talk with cold formality
Of cash 1
The souls of our gentility
Are filled with meek humility,
A holy affability,
The rough world cannot dash ;
They’re full of earnest prayerfulness ;
They seek the Lord with tearfulness ;
But eh, they show their carefulness
In cash !
Our hopes fall crash !
They give us Christian cheerfulness,
Not cash !
Oh men with purses ponderous !
The Church is groaning under us ;
She asks in accents thunderous
To share your yellow trash ;
And so whate’er our station is,
The theme of our oration is,
“The Church’s one foundation is
On cash! ”
Or else a smash !
The cry of our vocation is
For cash !
vie.
LAM.
BRIGHT’S DISEASE.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“ For twelve long months I never put my foot
outside the bed-room door,” said Mrs. Frederick
Whiteiey, 20 Seymour Crescent, North Ballarat.
“The best doctors could do nothing for me. I hey
told me that I had Bright’s Disease and Call
Stones. Often I was in such agony that I begged
for something to put me out of my miseryt. Ihe
doctors tried hard to get me to go to the hospital.
They all agreed that if I didn’t have an operation,
the pain would kill me. My husband said Id
gone through enough torture already, and he
wouldn’t listen to the doctors. As a last hope, I
started Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
In a few months I was working about the house
again, as strong as any woman in Ballarat. l)r.
Williams’ Pink Pills saved my life.
“ It’s between five and six years since my health
first began to fail,” said Mrs. Whiteiey. “I was
always tired, and got quite sallow in the face.
Smoky black rings came under my eyes. Just
below those rings the flesh was yellow, and all
loose and pouchy. I looked so ill and wretched
that I hated to look at myself in the glass. Often
I didn't care how things went. Many a day it
was as much as I could do to get the dinner on.
When it was ready, I never wanted a bite.
Everything I ate lay in a hard lump, at the end of
tny breast bone. My chest felt as if it was wedged
between great heavy weights. I could only get
my breath in gasps. After every meal, I went
through pain that was little short of torture.
The doctor gave me medicines, but they made me
worse. Nearly every morning I woke with a
splitting headache. As the day wore on it got
worse. In the end, I had to give in and lie down.
My stomach turned at the thoughts of eating. A
cold clammy sweat broke out all over me, and 1
felt that I was going to faint.
“ When I look back now, I don t know how 1
ever got through the days,” added Mrs. Whiteiey.
“ Half an hour’s work about the house, left me fit
for nothing. I was so heavy and drowsy that 1
could have dropped off to sleep any hour of the
day. When I went to bed, I tossed from side to
side. Often half the night was gone before I
closed my eyes. Even when I did doze off, I
woke with my heart thumping like mad. When
it was time to get up, I would have given any-
thing to stay in bed. Every bone in my body
ached. When I had this sort of thing to face day
after day, it was no wonder that I lost all heart.
I couldn’t help getting the miserables. Over and
over again, I said there was nothing for me to
live for, and that I would be far better out of the
world.
“ The pain in my back was enough to make any
woman wretched,” Mrs. Whiteiey went on.
“When I bent down it was agony for me to
straighten up. Washing day was what I dreaded
most of anything. At first the doctor said
it was Lumbago, hut he soon found out that
it was something more serious than that. My
kidneys were all inflamed and poisoned. There
were sure signs of that, as I could see for myself.
For five years I went through torture. In the
mornings I was always worse. All day long,
across my hips there was a dull dragging
ache that nothing could ease. It was like some-
thing stabbing me every time I moved.
“ At last, I had to take to bed, and there I was
(for over a year. The doctors said that my liver
was in a terrible state, and gall stones were
•forming. Every month my case got more and
more hopeless. Terrible pains took me in the
right side, and worked up to my shoulder blades.
Many a time I was bent double with the agony.
It was like something tearing at my inside with
red-hot pincers. Often these pains took me in the
dead of night, and the doctor had to be sent for.
He gave me a medicine that had to be made up
without delay. I rolled on my bed till the sweat
teemed off me. After hours of terrible torture, I
might get a little ease. Every attack left me as
weak as a baby in arms. For the next three or
four days I lay on my bed, too feeble to lift a cup
to my lips. At last, the doctors told my husband
that I-must have an operation, and have the Gall
Stones cut out. That was the only way they
could give me a little ease, and, perhaps, save my
life for a few months. Mr. Whiteiey would not
hear of that, and I felt that I would never come
out of the chloroform alive.
“We had given up all hope, when a very old
friend told us how Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills had
cured just such another case as mine. That very
day we sent to King, the chemist in Sturt St., for
some. The first box made me hungry, and from
that out, I mended. Weeks went by, and I
never had a bilious attack—and I’ve never had
one since. In a little while I was able to get out
of bed, and get around the house again. The pain
in my hack eased up, and I could see for myself
that my kidneys were better in, twenty ways. I
kept on with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and every
dose did me good. My face lost its sickly look,
and I felt that it was a pleasure to be alive. At
the end of ten weeks, I was working about as well
as ever I did in my life. All that time I never
had one attack of the terrible colicky pains in ray
stomach. I tell everyone how Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills saved my life when the doctors gave me up
to die any day.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills strike right at the cause of
disease in the blood, and so cure indigestion, kidney and
liver diseases, palpitation of the heart, nervousness,
neuralgia, rheumatism, sciatica, partial paralysis, and
the ailments that fill the lives of so many women with
misery. Sold by all chemists and storekeepers, or sent
post free by the Dr. Williams’Medicine Co., Sydney, at
2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 15s. 3d. Medical advice given
free.*

COLUMN
The great Melbourne brewery combine is now
arra ''" p ' l ,- It is to take over the business of
S r^ e oa^t° n . M’Oracken’s, the Castlemaine,
the Shamiock, Foster s, and the Victorian brewery. It
proposes to close down three of them, and concentrate
the business in the other three, which it will lease for 50
years with the option of purchase. The companies which
own the three closed breweries can sell them to the
oalvarray. The new co. takes over no liabilities, only plant
stock, and goodwill. Out of its £1,000,000 capital each old
brewery co. gets a certain number of shares proportioned
to the value of its business, and each old brewery co.
nominates one director of the combined co. Each old
brewery co. will get its dividend, and go away with it to
see its creditors if it has any—and do the best it can
with them. If any old brewery co. can’t keep its
creditors at bay with the dividend it draws from the new
brewery co., presumably its creditors will seize
its shares in the new brewery co. and divide them
among themselves. Any directors of the new brewery co.
who represent very much embarrassed old breweries will
probably want the profits divided to the last penny, so
that they will have the more dividend to fight their
creditors with ; those who represent more affluent old
breweries may prefer to vote for smaller dividends and
the building up of large reserves. The shareholders of
the old breweries will depend for their dividends on the
dividends of the new brewery ; it will be, in a manner of
speaking, a dividend within a dividend. It is a compli-
cated arrangement, but it is probably the best thing that
could be done with over-breweried Melbourne. Probably
in time, the old brewery cos. will simply divide their
shares among their shareholders and dissolve, and then
the new brewery co., instead of having six shareholders
and each shareholder a brewery, will become an ordinary
brewery co. just like any other brewery. Looking back
on this paragraph, there seems to be a great repetition of
the word brewery in it. 1

* » * *
A fine bit of work has just been done by
Melbourne shipbuilders in the Alfred graving dock at
Williamstown. The coastal steamer Peregrine was cut in
two just forward of the boilers, and with hydraulic jacks
and capstans the halves of the vessel were hauled 40ft.
apart. A new ’midships section has been built, and
will now' be lowered between the two halves, and built in
making the Peregrine 40ft. longer than it was before. If
the job had been imported it could not have been
done better.

In New York, the wild-cat acquires nocturnal
habits. The success of an “all-night” bank there has
induced a big Broadway firm of real estate agents to keep
open until midnight to facilitate land-dealing; and a
prominent bucket-shop keeps its doors open until 2 a.m.
and touts for late customers among theatre patrons.
