The Broken Hill Water Supply octopus coolly
suggests that if the N.S.W. Government would give it a
lease in perpetuity it could reduce the cost of water on
the Hill by half. It doesn’t say what it is prepared to
pay for such a valuable franchise, and so far it seems to
offer only its verbal assurance about the reduction in the
price of mud, or whatever the substance is.
* * * *
“M.C.”; The idea that, with depth, Broken
Hill ores get richer is not sustained by a comparison of
past and present milling- returns, nor does the idea fit in
with old dumps of tailings, some of which have proved
as rich in lead as the crude ore now being mined and
treated. Formerly the extraction in units of lead and
ounces of silver was better than it is now, because the
ore was much richer, and for the same reason the old
nulls lost more in the tailings than the modern machinery,
treating 12 per cent, and 13 percent, ore, can be expected
to do. Mill returns say plainly that ever since the
beginning of concentration, ore at a depth has got poorer
in metals and more difficult to concentrate. Assays and
reports cannot explain away a continuous record of
milling returns. No doubt all the mines have better ore
than they are now treating, because poor ore has to be
taken with the richer to keep down mining costs and to
prolong the life of the mine. It would be a poor look-out
lfßlock 10 and Block 14 had not some better ore than
the late returns have averaged, for the extraction of
metals compares very badly indeed with the results
obtained by the old rattle-trap mills of a by-gone age.
Block 10 (Broken Hill) has squeezed out of the
tight corner it was placed in by the creep without
- loudl - v erlou gh to startle shareholders. Harvey
shaft is now sunk right through to the 1200 ft., and is
being timbered as rapidly as possible. But, what is even
more to the purpose, the lode at the 1200 ft. is opening
up oltt. wide, so that now the shutting off of the stopes
in the upper levels won’t matter so much. The new
winding engine, which has been erected on the North
side of Harvey shaft, is working all right.

*** ’ *
, “Js i* to commit evil to do good’” is
what both speckers of Caledonia Consols, Warrandvte
(Vic.), and their opponents would argue over to the
bitter end. Bears have gone nap over the stock, their
justification being a belief that the mine is not up to
much, as indicated by the estimate they have framed of
the stone broken so far, and their belief that faulting will
make the show unreliable. The bulls go on the other tack.
They reckon that as the mine had a decent past it will
live up to that reputation on its second advent. This in
mining is like expecting to throwsixes continuously when
the Christmas raffles are on. There is a good deal of
certainty, however, about the bulls’ next position. That
is that as four-fifths of the shares are in safe hands any
large exchange specking means that it will be easy to force
the market against the shorts. This is where the greatness
of ethics arises. Is it right to pinch the Stock Exchange
bear speculator at any time, even if he does wallop your
stock. He says “ No.” His view is that, instead of being
a wrecker of your enterprise, he has sized it up properly,
and specks to prevent the public taking the stock at
inflated prices. Caledonia Consols bulls don’t admit this
reasoning, and are squeezing profits by insisting on
delivery. While this battle is being waged, the public
are drawing the only possible conclusion—that a co.
whose stock figures in this way is best let alone.
* * * *
Star of the East, Sebastopol Plateau (Vic.), gleams
brightly in a preliminary puff from the Argus. For-
merly the Age did the trumpeting, while the Argus
avoided the stock like sin. This was while the co. was
working at depth, and Granny’s renewal of interest in the
mine now the bottom levels are left, indicates pretty
plainly that it did not think the co. was worth
worrying over while it was poking about at depth. There
isn’t much to talk about at the 1250 ft. level. Tributors
on the western lode have a bit of ground which goes well,
but there have always been patches in the upper level
which have done that. Witness the last divs.
from the mineral lode which cut out so com-
pletely. There is plenty of untouched ground ahead
of the tribute block at 1250 ft., and it is the chance of the
extension of the shoot that prompts the present gamble.
Co. at 250 ft. deeper hopes to find the gold living down.
If it does the most will be made of the fact, for Ballarat
has been nursing a very heavy babe in the Star; but it
will take a mountain of inducement to get anyone outside
Ballarat to take other than a long shot at any of the
distant shows. Dealers play too much on the home
wicket for outsiders to score there.
Bendigo’s deepest shaft just now is that of the
Victoria Quartz, which has got ahead of the New Chum
Kailway, its depth being 4255 ft., or 7ft. more than that
of its rival. To imagine the depths of these shafts one
has to remember that there are not many peaks as high
in N.S. Wales.
* * * *
Victorian Mines Minister M‘Leod’s campaign
against the locusts of mining—the royalty holders
received an unfortunate set-back in the judgment of the
High Court dismissing the appeal of the Duke of Welling-
ton Co. against C.-J. Madden’s decision Ordering it to
continue paying 3 per cent, royaltv to the owner of some
land at Bamganie. The company up to March, 1904, had
under agreement paid the landowner £lO5 for land taken
and surface damage, and £1444 for royalties at the rate of
3 per cent;, on all gold won. Then the co, surrendered
its lease to the State and received back a consolidated
lease, and it brought the landowner before the warden to
assess compensation. The warden found that the
landowner had been fully compensated already.
Then the co. refused to pay any more royalties
and the landowner dragged it before the Supreme
Court. Chief Justice Madden gave him a ver-
dict, and the co. appealed to the High Court. The
High Court unanimously found for the landowner, stating
that the royalty was to be regarded as rent and not as a
loyalty such as had been forbidden by Victorian law. As
rent, the royalty was binding on anybody to whom the
lease might be transferred. This decision goes a long
way to support the Claims of all holders of mining royal-
ties in Victoria. McLeod recognises this, and has
announced that the amendments he proposes to introduce
into the Mines Act next session will effectually deal with
the evil. Every technicality that has been discovered in
the Act so far has been on the side of the royalty-holder,
and McLeod has been bumped rather badly by all of them.’
It is strange how these old laws made by the squattocratic
crowd all serve to boost up the man who owns the land
and provide him with money for which he does not do
any hard work.
Re Cornish United, Daylesford (Vic.). “The
management is everything that can be desired, and the
local board consists of men with practical experience and
high position, the chairman being the Government
Minister of Mines for the State of Victoria.” This sen-
tence is taken verbatim from the advertised report of one
of the outside London jobbers. If Mr. McLeod wishes to
know why he should take to heart the advertisement, he
can turn to the extravagant assertions made at the meet-
ing of shareholders. Here speaks managing-director
Bonnard : —“ We have a vast amount of virgin ground un-
touched, and I am not going to attempt to lead you on to
the consideration of any Arabian Night theories, but if
they (the old co,) were able to take out over
£1,000,000 worth of gold in the way I have
described, and we have, by working in a small manner at
the outset, taken out £65,000 worth of gold from a dis-
carded level, how many millions of gold do you think
still remain in our properties for extraction?” Those
who would say that because Ballarat produced tons of
gold at shallow depths it must be to-day the biggest
mining district in Australia, could perhaps answer such
a question, but the ordinary mining man is a different
being. Here is another gem from the same Bonnard set-
ting : “ I think it a veritable El Dorado, and out of this
El Dorado you possess in these four lode channels an
area of ground of no less than 400 acres in its
extent.” This is another old friend introduced very
skilfully. Area means extent of lodes, and ex-
tent of lodes means rich returns. Boiled down,
£65,000 worth of gold, won in eighteen months, does not
indicate a veritable El Dorado, and there is no more
chance of the Daylesford mines proving El Dorados than
those of Ballarat. They have average grade, big forma-
tions, which should pay to handle if worked cheaply and
with skill. Minister McLeod, assuming he is worth his
salt, should show resentment at such extravagance
Anyway, shares would not flag as they do if there was
anything marvellous about the Victorian Cornish.
Apropos of Daylesford, it may be pointed out
that the smart Ballarat crowd are already preparing the
way for little Stock Exchange pickings over the district.
They have got the Ajax, and the Nuggetty Ajax, and
the South Nuggetty Ajax, and goodness knows what else.
The Ajax ground is worth atrial, just as any other decent-
looking formation is, but to rush Ajax scrip up to 11s.
or 12s. on untried possibilities is a feat as
sensible as the carrying of Port Arthur shares to 17s.
because a sft. lode had been exposed. However, another
Daylesford mine has gone out of the State. This is
the New Glenmona, which Hunter Patterson and his
brother and Tom Hills ran for years with almost con-
sistent bad luck, Hills never had a doubt where the gold
was, but others thought differently. So he had to let go,
and Devie, of the Victorian Cornish, and the Fields getting
hold of it for about £2OOO, picked up the gold and started
dividends. Next Lees Field realised on the mine in Lon-
don, and, “ that’s the way money goes.”
* * «* *
Weldbrook (Tas.) is the first of the little group
that has been a gamble on the tin-market for the
past 12 months to practically turn up its toes. It is
only a fortnight or so since the meeting, when
the Melbourne shareholders dispersed, thanking
their lucky stars that they had such a mine.
The board now admits to £llOO of debts, and trusts
that the present run will ease that liability. How
can it ? Costs must be from £5OO to £6OO, and the general
belief is that two tons will be the utmost that will be
cleaned up. Judging by the long faces drawn, there is
a very hard-hit crowd about the Stock Exchange just
now. The often-heard recriminations, shouted in no
subdued tones, tell of the same experience. Pity
might be felt if there had not been such open market-
boosting. But what about the highly-paid experts who
very recently commended the show to Queen-street and
Collins-street magnates. They are singularly quiet.
Board is to make a 10s. call and wants the amount of the
shares increased from £6 to £lO. That means preparing
for further contingencies. Briseis Extended is likewise
in the doldrums with a five-ton return, or half that of the
previous run, though the barge is in the ancient drifts.
Result, a debit balance of £lOO. The prehistoric man
certainly did his work very well before moderns got on
to the claim.
* ■* * *
The drop in the Westralian gold yield continues,
the figures for May being 157,0210 z., against 157,6850 z.
for May, ’O5, and 160,2800 z. for May, ’O4. For the first
five months of the year the totals are : 1904, 817,1670 z. ;
1905, 810,2240 z. ; and 1906,761,5420 z. The way things are
shaping it looks very much as if the year’s shortage would
be 120,0000 z., as compared with 1905, and 150,0000 z., as
compared with 1904. Prospecting is practically dead
chiefly owing to the stagnant and drooping condition of
the Stock Exchange. Most speculators are more or less
stranded by having to pay up continuous calls on scrip
held under cover. Until there is a revival in scrip
generally there will be no money for prospecting. The
only prospective producers are the Oroya-iuack Range, of
probably 1800 oz. monthly and the Lancefield of 36000 z.
The goldfields generally are looking very depressed.
* * * *
W.A. dividends, like W.A. gold returns, begin
to show a serious decline. For the first half of 1905 the
divs. totted up £1,148,640, while this year the total has
fallen off to £980,850. The fall is pretty general. Last
year Oroya distributed £258,750 till June, and this year
only £lBO,OOO. Great Fingall last year panned out £187,500,
while this year only £150,000 was forthcoming. Golden
Horseshoe toes the mark w'ith £75,000, against £90,000
last year. Even the much-boosted Great Boulder shows
£131,250, against £153,125 last year. Ivanhoe, the
best-managed mine in the State bar the Kalgurli,
shows no change. The Kalgurli, through the able man-
agement of Moss, has divided £75,000 against £30,000 in
1905, and will show an enormous increase on the year.
Sons of Gwalia pans out £48,750 against £24,375 a year
ago, but is now in a pretty dicky position. Perseverance
has distributed £70,000 against £52,500, but the
future outlook is gloomy, unless the boundary
“ phantom ” lode turns out trumps. Associated
Northern has paid out the same as a year ago,
and so has Associated (by paying nothing). Black Range
has distributed £6750 against £5063, and looks good
enough to make a record on a small scale. Ida H. shows
up well with £BlOO against £5400 a year ago, and prospects
are fairly good. Hainault shows little change, the figures
being £6750 against £6287, owing to more shares having
been issued. South Kalgurli has paid £20,000 against
nil a year ago—improvement is owing to the capable
and energetic management of “Taffie” Morgan. Cum-
berland’s contribution has dropped from £4OOO to £2OOO,
while Hidden Secret’s has gone from £32,400 to nil.
Fremantle Smelter has only panned out £15,000 against
£47,500 a year year ago. Golden Ridge, which, last yeStr,
paid £6287, is now on the rocks, and not likely to pay
for some time to come.
* * * *
Sons of Gwalia, Leonora (W.A.), appears to
have got completely under its ore chute exactly like the
Great Fingall. The thirteenth level has been extended
over 900 ft. in very poor ore, and the face is only worth
about 2dwts. per ton. The fourteenth level is in over
500 ft., and the face shows no values. The present dead
work must be charged to revenue, as no ore is being de-
veloped by it. Under the circumstances, it is pretty
Certain that the current rate of divs. cannot be main-
tained. A lot of prospecting is being done to the south,
and may turn out profitably, but that cannot be relied on.
* * • #
Sons of Gwalia, Leonora (W.A.), shows a very
poor return for May. The grade of ore treated has dropped
from 40s. 6d. to 345. 6d. per ton, with the result that the
yield has fallen from 52040 z. in April to 43780 z., and the
profits from £9540 to £5680. This once for all settles the
question of the maintenance of the Is. 6d. quarterly div.,
which means £97,500 a year. V it.h the extremely poor
outlook in the bottom levels, and this serious decline in
output, the div. will have to be reduced to Is. per quarter,
and possibly even less. It is instructive that, on Dec. 31,
the ore reserves were estimated at 385,500 tons, valued at
40s. 6d. per ton, equivalent to three years’ supply, and in
five months the value has dropped by 6s. per ton. The
fall may be only temporary, but, as the bottom levels are
worthless, the outlook is very poor.

Princess Royal, Norseman (W.A.), has pub
lished its worst return for many a long day, and co. looks
as if it must, be in Queer-street. During May, 686 tons
were treated for 4360 z. bullion worth £l4OO, or about 41s.
per ton. This will show a loss, at the lowest estimate, of
£3OO. Shares in the Princess Royal have of late had a
rather active market, but there is nothing to justify this,
and there must be a collapse sooner or later.
* * * n
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), appears to be
reaching the end of its tether. On January 31 the ore
reserves were given as 13,400 tons worth 1275. per ton,
and since then the returns per ton have been as follows :
January, 695.; February, 665. 3d.; March, 575. 6d. ; April,
635. 9d. ; and May, 665. Bd. per ton. The average works
out at 61s. 7d. per ton, or a shade over half the estimate.
The fact of the matter is that the minimum stoping
width is about 30in., and the value of the reserves is calcu-
lated on 6in. or Bin., or whatever the reef happens to
pinch to, and, when results falsify estimates, management
accuses employees of stealing the gold. The mine is
pretty well played out, in spite of optimistic reports, and
co. will probably give up before many months.
Golden Horseshoe, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), during
1905, sent to the Fremantle smelter 15,700 tons, worth
£194,000 against 24,100 tons in 1904, worth £364,000, or a
decrease in value of nearly half. The average profit
being earned by the co. is about £21,000 a month, and
this can be maintained for the next three years. The ore
reserves on December 31 were given as worth 645., but
results only pan out at 505., which tends to prove that
no allowance is made for mullock broken down in stoping,
and other contingencies. The ore and the mullock in the
Kalgoorlie mines are, in many cases, so exactly alike
that no expert can separate them, and allowance is made
for this in some cases when estimating reserves.
* * * *
Westralia Mt. Morgans appears to be in a very
dicky position. In June last year, Bob Nicolson, of the
Ivanhoe, was commissioned to give a report on the mine
and its reserves, and stated that there were in sight only
34,800 tons of ore, worth 365. per ton. He advised
closing down the treatment plant, and pushing on with
development. Managing director A. E. Morgans pooh-
poohed Bob Nicolson’s pessimism, and cabled London
directors that the mine could work out its own salvation.
Morgans had his way, and ran the mine to a standstill.
The result was that, in March, co. had to reconstruct
with 130,000 preference shares of ss. to provide £32,500
extra working capital. After receiving 200 per cent,
these preference shares are to rank as ordinary. On A.
E. Morgans’ advice the mill was shut down, and the whole
plant overhauled. A start was made in January, and
since then 13,500 tons have been treated for an average of
19s. per ton, which must leave a loss. Morgans is one of
the old-fashioned class of miners ; he does not believe in
constant sampling, and is quite out of place on the W.A.
goldfields. In a very short time co. will probably again
be in trouble.
* * * *
Revenue, Meekathara (W.A.), has just treated
881 b. of stone for a return of 201 oz. It is six months
since the previous parcel of 1701 b. was treated for 2050 z.,
so that it can be easily gauged that the mine is on a very
small scale. All the same, up to date, about 15J tons
have been treated in all for a return of 26650 z. This
would be no good for a Bull co., but is a fortune for a
few working men.

Adelaide to advise the cutting down of the main shaft,
and the erection of an up-to-date mill, but the whole
thing seems a farce.

SPRING ST.
North End, Kalgoorlie, has come to the end of
its tether, and a meeting has been convened for June 30
to inaugurate a reconstruction. The mine was extra-
vagantly managed from the start, and, as a conse-
quence, has not been given a fair go. The chute, or pipe
of ore, is dipping to the south, and very little work has
yet been done on it. Until the mine is equipped with rock
drills to test the hard ground in the sulphide zone no
definite opinion can be expressed of its possibilities.
-ft * • *
Ivanhoe Junction, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), which is
being much boomed by manager Hocking and director
Uren, is the same old lame duck it has been for years.
Hocking advises cutting down the shaft, erecting
an up-to-date treatment plant, and starting crush-
ing on a big scale, and Uren endorses his recom-
mendations. Possibly people will put their good cash
into this “ cat,” as they have so often done before,
especially as Boulder people, who should know better,
have taken up the 15,000 forfeited shares at 3d. apiece.
Manager Hocking asserts that co. has in sight
9000 tons of 15dwt. ore, and untold thousands of tons
of 9dwt. ore, and that he can keep a mill going
at a profit for years. There may be a gamble
in the shares, but Hooking’s statements are much
too sanguine, as the battery has again and again proved.
His alleged ore reserves were there when Johnson was in
charge, and when Tom Hewitson, years ago, said that the
ore would go 10dwt., and tests went less than 3dwt.
* * * #
The Carbine (W.A.), about 45 miles north of
Coolgardie, is being very much talked of at the moment.
It is said to be the greatest gold mine on earth, and
Minister of Mines Gregory has endorsed this statement.
The main shaft is down 400 ft., and, in addition to driving
about 120 ft., a crosscut has been put in 410 ft. east
without finding any wall. The mine has been worked
on and off for the past ten years, and abandoned by
various parties in sheer disgust. The present owners,
Crawford and party, have crushed 0482 tons, taken out
during development for a return of 33430 z. Quite re-
cently the tailings dump has been measured by experts,
who estimate that the tonnage has been rather over than
under estimated, and that not much more than 5000 tons
have been treated. Everything broken out has been
put through the mill, and there is no dump of rejected
mullock, so that the proposition looks genuine. The
owners want £60,000 hard cash for their show, but
nobody seems inclined to jump at the bonanza. On the
strength of this show the adjoining blocks have been
pegged out, and the South block is now being prospected
by a Perth syndicate of £5OOO in £5 scrip, bossed by
Bellingham, M.L.O. If the thing pans out, W.A., which
is now looking decidedly sick, may come again with a
rush.
* • • •
Mining costs have been reduced at
Kalgoorlie, and the effect of the reduction on all the re-
presentative mines has been to increase ore reserves. For
instance, if the South Kalgurli costs can be brought
down to 225., as alleged, sdwt. and over becomes the
stoping limit, and the addition of a large quantity of this
Sdvvt. and 6dwt. orb that will give very little profit will
automatically increase the reserves. Shareholders, when
told that there is a large sudden addition to reserves
because of reduced costs, may be quite sure that reduced
mining costs, at any rate, have been partly brought
about by including lower grade ores in the stoping limit.
Taking a lot of poor ore along with the richer and more
profitable ore is a way some W.A. mines have of cutting
down costs and nominally increasing ore reserves. The
quality of the ore included in reserves is little more than
a guess. Values jn these lodes occur like plums in a
pudding ; assays will not determine if there are sufficient
plums—only stoping and crushing can do that. The
speculative value of lodes like the Perseverance or Lake
View consists in the fact that lenses of rich ore have
occurred and are likely’ to occur again.
* * » *
Oloncurry (N.Q.) is getting ready for the coming
railway. During April 23 mineral leases and 20 mineral
copper claims were applied for, and Warden Cherry ex-
cuses the delay of his report on the grounds of the
extreme rush of business. About 700 people are at pre-
sent making a living in the locality in connection with
Copper shows, and Mount Elliott is endeavoring to get a
smelter to the ground, but is hampered by carriage pro-
blems. There are 4000 acres of mineral freeholds at Clon-
curry—another proof of the necessity for the proposed
Act to shake mineral freeholders out of their wonted
lethargy.

Aerial tramways or conveyors undoubtedly
afford the cheapest means of transport, especially when
they are worked under a system which is entirely
automatic. The, largest manufacturers in the world in
this particular line are Adolf Bleichert and Co., of London,
represented in Australia by W. and J. Lempriere, of Mel-
bourne. Delivering ore automatically from the mine to
the mill over almost any distance at a cost of about one
pennyper ton, is far ahead of all other means of transport.
Automatic systems are in vogue for the cheap discharge
of tailings. Automatic weighing and counting of loaded
cars is also assured under Bleichert’s system, thereby
nothing is left to assumption or imagination.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected,' inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discretion of cast or wrought iron work.*

The enterprising engineering firm It. Hornsby
and Sons, Ltd., Grantham, England, world renowned
manufacturers of the “ Hornsby ” Patent Safety Oil
Engine, the latest adaption of which is their Traction
Engine, have entered the Gas Engine arena and are now
making the Hornsby-Stockport Gas Engine and Suction
Gas Plant. The latter we learn will develop upwards of
10 brake horse power at the cost of Id. per hour. The
minimum cost and attention required should prove a great
inducement to users of motive power to instal this type
of plant. The Australasian addresses of the above firm
are : Sussex-street, Sydney and Spencer-street, Mel-
bourne.*
* * * •
The electric plant of the Federal Palace Hotel,
corner Collins and King Streets, Melbourne, furnishes a
good example of a thoroughly successful electric installa-
tion, in technical as well as in commercial respects. This
plant generates all electric current required for the hotel
lighting ; it consists of a 20 horse-power gas engine belted
to a “Ganz” 12 kilowatts dynamo, a “Ganz” switch-
board containing all the controlling and measuring instru-
ments, and a storage battery with 82 cells. The generating
machinery runs continually during the day time at its
full capacity and highest efficiency ; .the surplus current
being stored into the battery; the latter supplies all
necessary electricity required during the night; the
battery forms also a substantial reserve in case of acci-
dents. Compared with the previous method of working a
saving of £4OO per annum has been effected by this plant.
The contractors for this plant were The Austral Otis
Engineering Company, Ltd., corner Hanah and Kavanagh
Streets, South Melbourne, as sole agents for Ganz and
Company, Budapest, Hungary.* [3.]
* * * *
The introduction into the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small hand rock-drill for
boring “pops” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“ hitches” for timber, and squaring up shafts and levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad. The best machine for this work is the
“ Shaw ” Hand Rock Drill and is manufactured by the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 161bs.
and has only one working part—the hammer—which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. In some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 2Jin. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now Using the Shaw
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne, where
the machine mav be seen working.* [o]

The Zinc Corporation has taken careful advice
in connection with the large plant required at Broken
Hill, and has ordered Babcock and Wilcox water-tube
boilers with superheaters and mechanical stokers. This
order has been followed by the great firm of mining
engineers, Messrs. Bewick, Moreing and Co., placing
orders with the same boiler company, in connection with
the South Blocks, also at the Hill. These instances
further prove the assertion that, wherever economy in
fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. This company stock boilers at “ Haymarket,”
Sydney.*

Dick Seddon’s greatness was not solely due
to his intellectual and physical capacity and force
of character. It was his big hearted sympathy
with human suffering which gave his soul its con-
quering magic. All politicians should quicken the
magnetic power of human kindness by well con-
sidered instalments of D.C.L. whisky ; and so
quicken the march to the goal of human happiness.
A little D.C.L. in a first class human vessel has
beautiful results.*
Westralian barrister Pennefather (one of
John Forrest’s Attorney-Generals) made a loud
complaint, when English-imported Judge Rooth
appeared on the Perth bench last week, against
the passing-over of local barristers for the im-
ported one, and made it reasonably clear to Rooth
that the bar was very sorry to make his acquaint-
ance. Rooth was appointed by Premier Rason.
Pennefather treated the matter mostly as a per-
sonal grievance, because (though once acting as
Judge) he had not got a permanent appointment
himself. Pennefather alleges that Rason first
offered the billet to a colleague (Moss), but there-
after gave no Westralian barrister a chance.
Pennefather’s complaint, and the general slight
given to the Westralian bar, make Rason’s action
appear a little smellful. It looked unpatriotic all
the time.

You may beat the tattoo on the drum
Imperialistic or any other kind of a tom-tom. But you
cannot beat Tattoo Oil for sheep marking. And it needs
no “ drummer ” to convince wool-growers of this import-
ant and well-known fact. Tattoo oil carries conviction
on its merits. Every branded sheep is its advertiser—
and the best of advertisers at that.*
“ Tuna ” writes from an unpronounceable
place in Maoriland :
Par in Bulletin (24/5/’O6) re Maoriland’s latest religious
fanatic calls for a few corrections. Rua Tapu is not a
son of Te Kooti, neither is lie a firebrand ; merely a waster
from the Urewera country, where they grow a prophet
once, in three months. He is a clumsy person who
hasn’t even sense enough to map out a programme that
is reasonably likely to eventuate, but assures his fol-
lowers that, in addition to meeting Edward VII., they
will have discovered to them untold wealth in the shape
of huge diamonds, &c. His adherents are rapidly leaving
him, and from a retinue of 2000 he will pass along the
coast en route to Gisborne with a rabble of probably 50.
His method of curing the sick is original, if not effective.
The patient hands up 9d., 3d. of which Rua places under
his armpit for a few moments. Then he directs the
victim to stir it in some tea and drink the liquid. If no
good result follows, Rua falls backs on the ancient gag—
“ not sufficient faith.” I asked one of his disciples what
would happen to the Prophet if Edward VII. failed to keep
his appointment. “Oh! cut him plurr.v troat, I tink.”
The drawback to a large policeman dealing violently
with Rua is that the natives would then make a martyr
of him, and he would loom up in legend as a real prophet,
of whom the Pakehas were afraid.
“ Ailath ” : Willie-motor Kelly got into
trouble with Pa Deakin, the other day, for asking
frivolous and impertinent questions. “If is was
was,” or something to that effect, chirrupped
little Willie, with an evident satisfaction at having
said something real funny and clever. He was
rebuked much. Willy does certainly come out
with some silly little questions and silly big motor-
cars, though now and then he happens to put
a trace of common-sense into a query, but
he throws it in the midst of some other member’s
eloquence, and Master Willie’s bit of gumption is
sucked up in the vortex of Parliamentary gab and
strife. Poor Willie is never taken for what he
is—the only member who can really walk grace-
fully down the Block.
Robert Bodycomb—it is a remarkable
name—who passed out, recently, at Dromana
(Vic.), aged 80, did a lot of solid work as a Pro-
tectionist during his career. He was a native of
South Wales, an engineer by profession ; he came
to Melbourne in 1852, and put in 35 years making
good Australian machinery with Langlands’
Foundry Co. He was treasurer to Victoria’s first
Protectionist Society, which got through Parlia-
ment a Bill protecting the local machinery in-
dustry with a 25 per cent, import duty.
DEVELIN AND O'DONOVAN, THE
IRISH ENVOYS.
The love affairs of thrifty John O’Brien,
draper’s assistant, were made public recently at
the Northcote (Vic.) Police Court, where Nellie
Rollo, “a daintily-attired young woman of pre-
possessing appearance,” sued him for £4l 10s.,
money paid, money lent, and money received.
She also proceeded against him for the illegal
detention of two rings, valued at £6. Nellie
related that John, whose dazzling smile over the
drapery counter had captured her young affec-
tions, had got from her £5 to pay for a watch, for
which she afterwards received a bill, he having
spent the money otherwise. She further stated
to a benevolent Bench :
I bought, at his request, 15s. worth of shirts for him.
He promised to pay for them, but never did so. I also
gave him £1 for his fare from Melbourne to Castlemaine,
and £llss. to pay a bill, which he never paid. Besides
this, I gave him sums 'amounting to £9, to pay the
expenses of his illness. While I was in Queensland I sent
him £4 10s., to help to pay for an electric belt. He in-
vited me to go to the theatre, but when we got there he
said he had no money, and I gave him £l. He bought
the tickets, but never gave me any change.
And, in addition, it seems, John the Draper wrote
her letters like these
Dear Nell, —I want you to send me 10s. or 12s. 6d. I
hit a young man, and he is going to bring me to court
if I don’t square it....
If you send me down the money I will come up and see
y0u.... I am going to compensate you some day by
taking you as my life partner.
I think you had better come back to Victoria. How is
your purse ? I think full by this time. Mine’s very low
at present.
Have no work at present. Can you spare me a few
pounds ? I intend to make up for it some day by making
you Mrs. O’B.
Had to pawn your ring to pay for my board.... Got
p. note you sent. Wish it was four times as big.
I suppose Annie told you I went to Ballarat, and I
did not have to pawn my ring to go there. I went over
with a young woman as my companion. She paid my
fare, so you see it cost me nothing. I have got your ring
out again, and am going to keep it.
Then Nellie realised that she had lost her John.
He was borrowing money from a new girl—the
minx ! The Bench decided that John (who had
married another woman) should pay Nellie
£41105., with £3 3s. costs, and should give her
back her rings. Presumably, Nellie Rollo is
“off” drapers at present.
Transpires that in New South Wales they
have a system of paying a juryman by results. If
he attends four hours he gets 4s. If the time runs
beyond that he gets luncheon and 3s. extra.
Naturally, therefore, an impecunious juror will
often be inclined to spin his job out. Better, on
the whole, to pay him 7s. and a ham sandwich for
the day, whether short or long.

Thomas Seddon, second son of Premier
Dick, who, it is said, will be asked to stand for
the old lion’s seat in Parliament, is generally
regarded in Maoriland as a chip of the old block.
He is a law student (having just finished an Arts
course at Wellington), and has a fine record both
in study and in athletics. He was a likely (though
finally unsuccessful) candidate for the last Rhodes
scholarship, for which King Richard thought him
ideally fitted. It would be an amazing thing if
Thomas, in politics, proved to be the second of a
dynasty of Seddons. Such things have been. It
took five generations to wear out the statesman-
ship of the Koprilis, and there might be five
successive Seddons in like manner.
It is announced that a “ Japanese gentle-
man ” resident in Victoria has persuaded Premier
Bent and Lands Minister Murray that rice can be
grown there, and they have “ arranged to grant
him an area on the Murray on which to experi-
ment with rice-farming.” Here three points loom
out very conspicuously:—
(1) If the Jap had been an Australian he would have
had to buy his land in the ordinary way, and if that way
is good enough for an Australian it is good enough for a
Jap.
(2) If an Australian made a similar application in
Japan, so far from getting “ a grant" of land he wouldn’t
even be allowed to buy land.
(3) It is a weird piece of foolishness to give land to a
Jap, to experiment in rice-growing, seeing that the
necessary experiments are even now being made by white
men at Mildura without any “ grant.”
The skipper of a little Maori-
land hooker, the topsail schooner
Jessie Nicol, has probably come to the
conclusion by this time that he is a
now Vanderdecken, a hoodooed man,
and a person under a commodious curse.
In February last the little vessel bobbed
away out of Bluff Harbor en route for
the Macquaries—a mere handful of
black, bleak debris in the sea, half-way
between the great Maoriland public debt
and the Antarctic ice-floes. Its business
was to fetch away the summer penguin
fishers before they froze to death, and to
bring away their stock of penguin-oil.
The little topsail schooner hasn’t arrived
at the islands yet, and the penguiners
are still freezing on the Macquaries.
For weeks the south-west gales struck
the vessel, and the whole Southern
Ocean climbed over the weather rail
and poured out over the lee one. The
captain found the Auckland isles, which
he didn’t want, and the Campbell isle 3,
which he had no use for, and other isles
which were quite unsaleable, but he
couldn’t get far enough to windward
to strike the Macquaries. He came
back to the Bluff with a broken bowsprit
and did repairs, and sailed again. The
same S. W. gale blew him off, and he
returned to Port William (Stewart
Island) to land two injured sailors. He
made his third start, and was blown
back. He made his fourth ditto, and
was ditto. When last heard of, he had
a disgusted anchor down at Port
William, and was preparing for a
fifth start. Meanwhile, the penguiners
must be doing a bitter hungry freeze on
the Macquaries, and it seems as if it
would be a fair thing to send a steamer
to their rescue.

and the side-board ? As an alternative he might
stop talking bosh.
A System that Swells Profits.
The “Time Clerk" who used to be such a feature of
the big factory all over the world will soon have gone to
join the megatharium, plesiosaurus, and Queen Anne, and
they of an inquiring mind can see his successor round at
the show-rooms of Fairfax and Roberts, 23 and 25 Hun-
ter-street, Sydney. It is called the Rochester Time
Jobcosting Recorder, and is an amazing development of
mechanical record. Attached to a clock is a printing
apparatus. Each man in the establishment has a card
that records his time at the establishment, and the
machine prints on it the date and the exact moment
of his arrival. When he leaves the building
he rings off, and his card bears the time.
In the matter of piece work men are given “ job cards ”
and they “ ring on ” when they start the job, and “ ring
off ” when they finish it, or lay it aside for another. By
this device the manufacturer can obtain the exact time
that has been taken over any particular piece of work,
and can also compare the value of the various workmen
to the establishment. In the U.S.A., where there are
many thousands of these machines installed, employers
have instituted a system of bonus paying for fast
work, and this amazing Recorder does the discrimi-
nating and picks out the fast worker from the slow.
For all-round utility in the matter of keeping a check on
the going and coming and various operations of hundreds
of men working on various jobs, all of which have to be
checked and recorded, it is perfect in its mechanism, and
there is practically no operation that the Recorder
cannot do better than a clerk, and for an invisible fraction
of the cost, to wit, £4O for a lifetime. Its invention and
production cost $lOO,OOO in tools, machinery, and models,
and it has been developed to a state of absolute perfection.
For the proper conducting of an establishment in which
men are paid by results the thing is indispensable, as by
reference to a man’s working cards it is possible to ascer-
tain by a little observation exactly the time he takes
over' any class of work, and when tendering, or
quoting estimates, that information is of the utmost
value. For establishments in which piece work is not ,
the system, and where all that the management requires- 1
is a record of the time at which the assistants arrive and
leave, there is the Bundy Recorder, of which each em-
ployee has his key. When he comes in he inserts his key
and rings the bell. The clock prints the time on a
travelling paper tape, and the key prints the number, and
the tape, having taken its impression, moves on inex-
orably, and nothing can bring it back. When an em-
ployee goes out to buy his mother-in-law a new hat he
presses a lever as he rings off, and the machine marks the
fact that he is out by affixing a Star against his name. It
is a perfect time recorder. x

A BACK-DOOR TRAGEDY.
WOMAN: “Aren't you the man I gave a lump
of pie to yesterday?”
TATTERED TED: “ No, mum; it was me mate ,
Billy. He eat all of it before I could stop
him."
WOMAN: “ The greedy wretch! I suppose you
come for another slice?"
TATTERED TED: “ No, mum, I come ter say as
'Our Bill win is dying breaf forgive yer, mum."
Bratkins, who would probably have been
none the worse if he had passed the last five or
six drinks, landed home the other Saturday after-
noon with a parcel containing a fine fish. Mrs.
Bratkins was out shopping with the children"
and her temporarily benevolent husband decided
that he would save the poor, tired missus the
trouble of cooking the evening meal for once in a
way. He filled the kettle with water, and
managed to cram the parcel—paper, string and
all into it. This made the kettle overflow and
put out the struggling fire which Bratkins had
just lighted. Undaunted, he procured another
blaze with several weeks’ supply of fire-kindlers
and a few pints of kerosene. Then he gave the
kettle a seasoning of everything in the cruet-
stand, and awaited his wife’s return with an
“alone I did it” bearing. She came, and when
he proudly shewed her his pulpy handiwork she
indiscreet enough to exclaim, “Oh, Jack,
why did you leave it in the paper?” Righte-
ously indignant, and with a hazy recollection of
something he had heard about preparing joints
Bratkins scornfully replied, “So’s to retain the
natchral juices, o’ course. You a woman, and
don’t know that much about cookin’ ! ”

“Kulpara” : “F.Y.T.” (B. 3/5/’O6) says
that in Queensland irrigation with bore water
will not destroy Mitchell grass. Can’t speak
about Queensland, but in South Australia the
bore water kills Mitchell and every other kind of
grass in a few months. The only things that will
grow round a bore are a few reeds fringing the
banks, and perhaps a smattering of bulrushes in
the centre of the stream. Have also tried to grow
vegetables with bore water, but failed every time.
Seeds that were planted -would just come above
ground, then die off; while cabbage and lettuce
plants would grow for a month or so, get leggy
and also perish. The reason in “South Oss,” at
any rate, is that the bore water is so highly
mineralised that it impregnates the soil, which
becomes hard, caked and brittle, and encrusted
with salt. Perhaps if you could find bore water
that was not.mineralised and had the same pro-
perties as rain water, then irrigation might be a
success; but it has not been my luck to strike such
a bore and I have seen a good many. I have
noticed the same trouble with artesian springs.
With the exception of a wiry, spiky kind of couch
grass extending for about twenty yards round the
springs, and the aforesaid smattering of reeds and
bulrushes, there’s not a skerrick of feed within
two miles of the springs. (Almost forgot to men-
tion that dates are the exception. They grow
round bores or springs in luxuriant style—planted
by man, of course.)

“ A.E.P.” does a blob of enthusiasm :
Your correspondent “ Mark ”is right. With the excep-
tion of the stockwhip, and a few bellbirds, and an occa-
sional lowrie, king- parrot, black cockatoo, lyre bird, or

destitute of bird life. A correspondent more recent than
Mark ” says he camped at the foot of Wentworth Falls
(Valley of Waters) and heard rosellas, magpies, king
parrots, &c., &c. But when we hear the lyre bird getting
in a bit of fine orchestral work in the early morning, and
especially when he puts all the tiimmings on, one might
fancy the whole ornithological creation was on show. I
was walking round Wentworth Falls, a few mornings ago
and .heard a concert, with the whole gamut from the wren
to the black cockatoo. Then the solitary performer hap-
pened to spot my dog, and stopped short.
1 camped at the foot of Vera Falls (Valley of Waters)
last Xmas, and at night the millions of fire-flies darting
about among the sombre foliage made a veritable fairy-
land. Add the rushing of the waters, and the sighing of
the winds, and there is the material for some of our
rising poets or musical geniuses to make something for
the world to hear.
By the way, Arnold Mote, now in London, has com-
posed a piece on the Valley of the Waters. Our mountains
and glens are calling for some sweet singer like our own
Kendall, or some word painter in sympathy with the
spirit of the mountains, to make something that will last
till the end of time. The fascination of the mountains
lies, not in their animal or bird life, but in the lights and
j The grim veteran crags that have
battled with the elements since before man was, the
storms and the winds and the gentle mists that shroud
the grandeur and majesty of the place—all the ele-
ments are here waiting for the master’s touch.

“ Flinderino ” : Re curious catches in
rabbit traps (“Elmore,” B. 17/5’06). Some
months back, finding things quiet, I took it into
my head to go trapping. Got a mate, bought a
moke and spring cart, and started off to beyond
JJandenong. Set two dozen traps for the
first night. Result: One sheep, one tomtit, one
water-rat, no rabbit. On the second round I
managed to catch two fingers.
Sydney Morning Herald’s man “ on the
land ” rhapsodises about the well-known squattage
“Tabletop,” a huge station outside Albury, on
the main railway line of the State:—
It produces luxuriant herbage, and the estate presents
a pastoral picture of great beauty and productiveness,
fine tracts of country extending away from the table-
lands as far as the eye can reach, richly timbered hills,
spacious flats, and rolling meadow-like areas which are
covered with herds of magnificent Devon cattle, their red
coats showing up through the trees in the distance,
against a background of misty purple ranges afar off.
And the 47,000 acres which make up this Garden
of Eden are devoted to sheep, cattle, emptiness
and scenery. Of men and women there are none.
The Bulletin begins to believe that the Serpent
was a member of the Upper House with a great
hatred of the graduated land-tax, and that Adam
and Eve were expelled from Eden to prevent
closer settlement.

“Te K.” : The Bulletin (24/5/’O6) has
something to say about the happenings of a back-
blocks race meeting down the West Coast of the
other Island. Recently there was a meeting up
the West Coast of this
(North) Island. It was at
Milkville, or Calfvale, or at
any rate, somewhere about
Cowtown. The course was
a paddock, and the crowd
made a street of itself near
the judge’s box. Young
Separator, getting a bit
anxious about how Farmer
Milkcan’s horse, Cream Test,
was getting on in the race then
eventuating, stepped out to have a
look down the course just as that animal
happened along. When young Separator
came down he hit the ground like a
paunch and slithered a lane for himself,
unparticular-like and sudden, through
the crowd, and eventually brought
up against a big plough horse. After
calling that equine several different
kinds of cow for treading on his waist,
he re-lit his cigarette. He is “ pivot
forward ” of the local football club, and
it is of such material that Maoriland
footballers are made.
“Rypo”: Was at Traralgon (Yic.) the
other day and spent an hour amongst the cattle.
You cannot be at Traralgon an hour without
getting amongst cattle. And one would become
cow-like after a few days there. Those who live
there do ; they moo at each other. After seeing
the cow people in their native stalls, I can quite
understand the intellect of cattle-dealing men
like ex-fractional Premier Allen M’Lean. M’Lean
just loves ox-like folks that vote as their employers
do. It was a dreary day at Traralgon, and the
dreary “ Man-with-the-Hoe ” cast of counten-
ance there made me miserable. I hurried away,
yearning for some strong man to bellow loudly in
the ears of those ruminating citizens, and wake
them up.
“ The ordinary bush J.P., if level-headed,
is better suited for average bush cases than an
acute lawyer,” said the Sergeant. “Do you re-
member Jim Hannell, of Maitland? He was
M.L.A. for Newcastle or somewhere about there
a few years after. Well, when Jim was made a
J.P. everyone laughed. He knew no more law
than his own horse. But his head was in the
right place. I mind his first case. Jim mounted
the binch with the air of a Chief Justice and
listened to both parties with his eyes shut an’ his
mouth open. I must say the swearin’ was hard,
for the women were ould, an’ were figlitin’ over
ducks. The flock ivas all there in the
coort yard, an’ each woman claimed
certain ducks as hers, which the other
denied on oath. It was a divil of a case to
see through, an’ would have bogged the Attorney-
General. But did it puzzle Jim? Not a bit.
He just opened his eyes when the parjury was
over, an’ says he, ‘ Constable, take these ducks to
where the row began—they must be hungry now.
When you get there let ’em go—let ’em all go,’ he
exclaimed, throwin’ out his hands, ‘ an’ then
watch them where they go. Them as goes to this
oul’ woman’s yard is hers, an’ them as goes to that
oul’ woman’s yard is that oul’ woman’s.’ An’ it’s
the truth —the right ducks wint to the right yards,
an’ both the oul’ women admitted it.”

“W.P.8.”: The picture-poem entitled
“ Old Jack ” (B. 3/15/06) is not the least bit of an
exaggeration as some may think. Those who
know sheep dogs will cheerfully allow that the
animals can do almost all but talk. A good
sheep dog has not a lazy bone in his body. Have
known a kelpie to “work” a rooster in his
leisure moments, just to keep his eye in, as it were.
And if commanded to “hold” it, would do so all
day if desired. There is no rushing about a good
dog. If the object under its control makes a
wild break for liberty the canine does not try to
race it, but sort of sneaks round in a semi-circle
until it gets ahead, and then back to the original
spot comes the rooster (or whatever the animal is)
as if he had no will of his own. A good dog can
“ work ” all other animals except the common or
garden variety of goat (of course elephants, fleas,
alligators, and other minute animals are excepted).
A goat doesn’t play the game; As soon as the
dog comes near, Billy or Nanny will just look at
him. If the dog comes too near the goat will
stand on its hind legs and strike with its fore
hoofs. The positions soon become entirely re-
versed, and the dog is driven backwards, the goat
with its eye kept unwaveringly on the enemy all
the time. The dog soon gives it up as a bad job
and turns away in disgust, and then William re-
sumes his dinner.
* * * *
“Silas Snell”: I have just made the
acquaintance of a benignant old Cousin Jack
miner, out of whose kindly eye shines truth like
the lamp on the bowsprit of a tramcar, and he
tells me of an almost incredibly tight fix in which
he once found himself. He was working in a
Creswick mine with a mate named Jim —not the
Jim occasionally mentioned in connection with
Bill; another Jim altogether—when he heard the
ground going overhead. It was treacherous
country, and the drive was a long one, A big
piece of reef started to come away, and the brave
old miner, seeing no other means of averting a
catastrophe, set his shoulders under it, and,
although the junk must have weighed upwards of
tons, kept it from shifting further till Jim put
some fresh timber in. A good idea of the terrible
strain upon my informant may be gathered from
the fact that when the timbers were in and the
ground secured he had to be belted out from
under with a hammer. I must admit that I could
hardly have believed this last item had it not been
told to me myself.
THE BOXING BOOM.
ADMIRING BARACKER : “ Ain't he a Bill Squires!
I call ’im a ’urricane fighter.”
Rabbit-exterminator Dr. Danysz, inter-
viewed in Melbourne, seems to have in some degree
given his case away. Said he : “In Europe, the
microbes I use have existed for centuries, and
epidemics break out among the rabbits frequently.
No precautions are ever taken against them
spreading, and never has there been any infection
known among men, beasts or birds.” Just so !
Admitting that the virus is fatal to bunnies alone,
what is gained by inoculating them with a disease
which has been trying for centuries to kill them
in Europe, but has failed to do so ?
LORD OF THE STABLES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ranging 1 down in the morning chill, by scrub and stony
face,
While snug in tent and stringy-shack the eight-hour
sweaters snore ;
Bull-eyed and stiff at daybreak,.in a reckless, crazv race
High up among the bouldered bush, to tail the mob once
more,
Slow back to craft and harness—crawling back to greet
the sun
With their weeping bluestoned shoulder-sores, their chain
cuts raw and red ;
Here, dodging needled ant-hills when the day has scarce
begun—
Watch me ! —loping in to see my motley mine-teams fed !
To bump them back to camp, and crowd the loafers past
the rails,
And weed them, slops and kickers, till they’re fixed up
tail to bar;
To dole out skimpy rations, bin to box, in careful pails,
Chow-fashjon ; and, with hungry eyes, to watch where,
low and far,
The drifting blue smoke swirls and lifts where Smithy
grills our chops ;
To shove and coax and curse and praise, and, as they
feed, stand by
To paw my aching void, while Staggers, off his tucker,
stops
To flash a vicious pound or two of hoof-iron in my eye.
In the cool of the Gulf morning, in the shade of the
green brush roof,
The long line scuffles and sniffs its grub—a mixed lot,
northern-bred,
With the pauper blood of their nameless sires picked out
in hair and hoof ;
Bare rib and lifted shoulder, flirting ear, and lumbering 1
head,
And cunning eye, they munch and plunge and bite ; a
graceless crowd,
With skinny thigh and cavern mouth—with each uncanny
sign
Of swift and savage schooling to the game of graft, and
ploughed
Between gaunt haunch and buttock with the slow-starva-
vation line.
Still, seven days in seven days, I snatch my crib, and
toil
All day and half the restless night, red-eyed and cursing
hard;
I’ve sold myself to slavery—to burn the midnight oil,
Mooching down with my “hurricane” when the late
teams strike the yard.
And still, a-bunk, from dreams of quick hoofs slashed
across my mouth—
Of foddery breath and ghostly molars meeting in my arm,
I start up, shuddering, to find the stars pale in the south,
And, shouting through the dark, the clamorous five
o'clock alarm !
vie.

“Musselburgh”: Re “Janor’s” query
(8., 24/5/’O6) as to why bark always curls the one
way. I can’t give him any reason except natural
cussedness, and I recall one time when I was the
victim thereof. I was stripping some stringy-
bark near the top of a rather steep ridge. Had
got a long wide strip on my back, and was carry-
ing it with my arms stretched out to keep the
sides open. I stumbled over a root or something,
and instinctively let go my hold of the bark to put
out my hands to save myself. Instantly the bark
curse curled round me, and I was rolling down the
ridge inside that vegetable stove pipe, at the
rate of I don’t know how many miles a minute.
When I was just about dazed with the whirling,
there came an almighty bump, and I thought I
felt myself sliding out of that circular coffin. We
(tfie bark and I) had struck a tree, but before I
could crawl out, the wretched concern had slewed
round and started off again for another spin.
Finally we crashed into a clump of saplings and
scrub near the bottom of the gully. When I
emerged from the inside of that improvised fern
roller and stump crusher, I felt a bitterness to-
wards bark that amounted to hatred. And my
mate laughed. Some men would laugh at an
Early Christian martyr boiling at the stake.

been unable to resist the opportunity afforded by

* » * *
A too faithful dog has sprung into notoriety
at Tarnagulla (Vic.) The owner, having killed a
pig, told the dog to watch it over night. In the
morning, a son of the house approached the car-
case, and the dog discharged his trust by biting
five inches of flesh off him.
“ Jabez ” : Would like to referee in the
white ant mound wrangle. It makes men who
know the bush tired. “Kulfara,” “.Tanor,” and
the others are each right, as far a’s his information
goes; but each seems to know only his own pet
mound. The large mound of “Kulfara’s”
acquaintance has a crust of incombustible material
up to six inches thick, and the inside is composed
of organic matter which burns freely. The small
tapering mound will not burn. I have a small
furnace built of this kind which stands any
ordinary heat, and at my tent door I have a large
one with the inside burnt out, in which I bake the
toothsome damper and the succulent bear.

“ Mallarapoona ” ; Can any of your
“ Abo’s” elucidate a mystery of Australian verna-
cular ? How is it if a person leaves the coast to
go inland, they say he is going cutback, and when
he reaches the very interior he’s right ow£side ?
If the person were on Central Mt. Stuart—the
very in’ards of the thing—he'd be about as far
out as he could get. On the other hand, if he left
Central Mt. Stuart to push out to the coast (if go-
ing inland is going in and not out as they say, then
going out to the coast must be going out and not
in as they call it), he’d be going in —inside; and,
when he reached the coast, he’d be right inside,
Mariners are correct in saying out to sea and in to
land ; but how the deuce can a chap be going out
—o«£baek, when he’s going in—inland all the
time ? What with going out when one’s going in,
and going in when one’s going out, I’m about as
bamboozled as the enraged Chow, who, failing to
comprehend these anomalies, remarked—“ Fuss
time you say insi’, then you say on’si’ —on’si’,
insi’, any blurry si’ too welly clankv.”

“ Curfew ” : The mysterious death of Sam
Yin perplexed the camp. It was well known that
he had nearly a bottle of gold, the result of five
years’ labor on the Dargo. Sam’s presence being
missed for about a week, it was decided to burst
his door open. They found him suspended from
a rafter with a rope round his neck, very dead.
Rumors were current that Sam had been mur-
dered, but at the inquiry the verdict was “found
dead.” The day after the funeral a report went
round that Sam had swallowed his gold to make
him heavy so that he would hang better. “ Ned,”
feeling convinced that he had done so, unearthed
the victim and took his stomach. Then he crept
to the river and started to wash up. He got
nothing. We afterwards learned that a syndicate
of Chinese had hanged Sam Yin and divided the
gold. At least that was the tale told by Ah Yep
in his dying moments. Ah Yep said that the
gash in his stomach had arisen through the rest of
the syndicate turning dog on him.

“Traveller”: A good story comes from
West Queensland —even better than that you told
of the Scotchman and his mouthful of
sovereigns. At Longreach show there was
an opulent prize of half-a-crown (25.6 d.)
for the best sheep dog—t hree dogs to be
entered or no prize. A Scotch grazing
person entered two sheep dogs, and
found to his horror that there was no
third entry, and the eighth part of a
pound was liable to forfeiture. But
at the last moment a homeless town
mongrel —a back-yard marauder with
the mange—drifted past, and the
Scotchman captured it by the hind
leg and entered it, and the situation
was saved.

“Haloli” : Although Queens-
land has been blessed with a good
season and a bountiful supply of
grass, money is so scarce in some out-
bade towns that the great natural
resources are used as legal tender.
The other day two scalpers entered
a liquor-house in Springsure (C.Q.),
ordered two pints, and p issed a dingo
scalp over the bar in payment. Mrs.
Public-house, after making a careful
inspection of the dead dog ears, re-
marked “things is tarybill bad just
now.” Then she handed over as
change three wallaby scalps, and the
scalpers departed with their currency.

WHEN FATHER SNORES IN CHURCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When father snores in church all other sounds
Are stifled by the loud crescendo tones ;
The quiet dead, beneath their verdant mounds
Outside, awake in fear, with rattling bones,
When father snores in church.
When father snores in church it’s like, Oh ! my !
A tropic cyclone out upon the loose ;
llysrericfemales squirm, and sob, and cry,
’Cos with their nerves it plays the very deuce,
When father snores in church.
When father snores in church the flippant smile,
The priest turns pale, the verger frowns reproof;
The nasal thunder rumbles through the aisle,
And rattles up the rafters in the roof,
When father snores in church.
When father snores in church you'd think there was
A lion in the vestry roarin’ hard ;
The organist gets mad as cats because
llis diapason can’t be heard a yard
When father snores in church.
When father snores in church it’s like a band
That’s all bassoon and drum—a horrid vamp
Of tortured tones—then mother takes a hand
And bangs him in the brisket with her gamp,
When father snores in church.
N.S.W.

The President and Vice-President must be native-born.
A Senator must be at least 30 years of age, native-born or
(if foreign-born) a citizen for at least nine years, and a
resident of the State he sits for. A Representative must
be aged at least 25 years, a resident of the State he sits
for, "and must be either native-born or have been
a citizen for at least seven years. Therefore both
A and B are partly wrong, and the bet is a draw'

A COMPLICATED CASE.
Melbourne, Sunday.—A youth charged at the Ballarat Court
on Saturday with lunacy, was said to have religious mania and
to suffer from delusions. He fancied, among other things, that
he was a member of Parliament. The youth was also under
the impression that he had been swallowed by a whale.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Exports by the Runic last week included
M 54,437 crates of rabbits and one atheist.
It is said Henry Parkes kept a political
diary. Well, it was about the only thing he
managed to keep. His creditors got the rest.
By the irony of circumstances, Seddon
being dead and Ward absent, old harmless Hall
Jones becomes Premier of Maoriland. Even in
the wildest visions of Jones’s head upon the bed
of Jones, it seems impossible that Hall Jones ever
dreamt of himself as a possible Premier.
When Dowie was last seen prophesying to
the small scrag end of Zion which still adheres to
him, he was described as looking fat and scared,
also he was carried by two niggers, and he wore a
purple and white turban, a purple robe, and em-
broideries of scarlet, green and gold. No wings.
C. C. Kingston is in Melbourne for the
Federal session, but still in indifferent health.
His friends hold the view that if he could get
.away for a quiet holiday spell on his own, the
great Federalist might recover enough strength
for much useful work.
Charles Edward Gordon, of Sydney, who
.-succeeds to a Scotch property and title, has been
enjoying a N.S.W. Customs House pension for
•over a decade. He was a serious, plodding, slow-
moving official of the old school, and until pen-
sioned off looked as fixed and reliable a figure as
the big front clock overlooking Circular Quay.
This motion was carried last week at a
meeting of the Yic. May Day Committee :
That this committee indignantly protests against the
tyrannical treatment to which the May Day deputation
was subjected by Mr. Bent, and considers it a serious dis-
grace to this State that an unmannerly Bombastes
Furioso should be permitted to assume the double role of
clown and Czar in a free and enlightened community, and
thus convey to outsiders that if Maoriland be “ God’s own
country," Victoria has the “ Devil’s own Premier.”
N.S. W. GOVERNMENT SONG IN THE
HOUR OF TROUBLE.
“ Our Anchor’s Wade.”
N.S. W. Equity Judge Simpson got a severe
shock from a witness, the other day. It was a point
of “ language, ” and the witness said that it was
very, very bad. “In tact,” he amplified, “it was
language your Honor wouldn’t use in a ” well,
“ house of ill-fame ” is the best expression for the
word. The witness didn’t mean to make any
offensive and wholly unjustifiable insinuation, but
the Court nearly died on the spot.
The S.A. Treasurer has had a painful dis-
appointment over the estate of Edward Spicer,
the pioneer squatter and merchant who died in
May, The old gentleman was known to be
immensely wealthy, and visions of another Angas
or Scarfe windfall were entertained. But deceased
was one of the kind who would much rather give
a pound away than waste a sixpence. He lived
on to nearly 90, and in his later years must have
made heavy distributions among his favorite
Wesleyan charities and the members of his large
family, for his estate is sworn at a mere £130,000,
which probably means under £lO,OOO in duty.
Ebenezer Ward, aged 69, who has long
been reckoned a political back number in S.A. is
running in the Ebenezer Ward interest for the
vacancy in the Murray district. So long ago as
1870 he was elected for one of the two old
I electorates which now form the constituency of
Murray. He was in the Assembly 20 years and
afterwards sat in the Council for nine years, and
he was a Minister under Boucaut and again
under Oolton for two years. So that he is
permanently ‘ ‘ honorable.” He is a journalist and
ages ago succeeded the Much-Wandered Smytheas
sub-editor of Adelaide Advertiser. Also he
acted as a member of G. V. Brooke’s company in
'the far-away day's.
