TOMMY : “Shall I! ”
A Victorian parson who plays among the
Anglicans the Dill Macky game has been cited to
appear before the Archbishop of Melbourne con-
cerning his insectarian lecturing. This is appa-
rently the first step in Anglican circles towards
squelching the reverends who stir up the muddy
drain of sectarian politics.
William O’Sullivan, for many years
A.W.TJ. secretary at Charleville (Q.),* dropped
off recently, aged 40. For some time he belonged
toN.S.W. Education Department; then he took
to the political arena to try and educate the
working classes. In 1902 he ran a bad second to
Janny Leahy for Bulloo, but at last general
election he came within a sma’l bag of votes of
ousting the other Leahy (Patsy) at the Warrego.
Amongst the officers in the S.A. militia is
one whose father was a carpenter. Some years
ago he applied for a vacant appointment in the
forces. A superior officer of Hebrew extraction
received the application, and wrote on it, “ I do
not consider this man suitable; his social position
is nothing.” The document then passed on to
another officer, who, reading the comment with
disgust, wrote beneath it, “I consider this man
suitable for the position in every respect, and I
may add that this is not the first time the Jews
have persecuted a carpenter’s son.”
The Sydney morning papers are getting
the public mind used to the idea of providing
W. T. Dick, M.L.A., with a “stiddv job” on
N.S.W. Public Service Board when the retire-
ment of Joe Barling is arranged for. Dick’s poli-
tical career has been rather a disappointment.
He was always going to do things, and after long
waiting his hopes of a portfolio seemed to be
really blossoming when Carruthers formed his
Ministry. But at the 1904 election Dick only re-
tained his seat by 48 votes in a poll of nearly 5000
(in 1901 he had 500 votes to spare), and, for a man
whose seat was so “dicky ” as Dick’s, the billet of
honorary ornament without portfolio seemed good
enough.
Robert Fowler, M.L.C., who was buried
at Waverley on Thursday, was born in George-
street West, Sydney, in 1841. His father, Enoch
Fowler, started a pottery there (near Bay-street),
but in the mid-fifties went to Camperdown, where
the business has been carried on now for half-a-
century. Robert Fowler was Mayor of Sydney,
M.L.A. for Phillip Division, and subsequently
M.L.C. As a member of the community, he was
an upright man; as member of the municipal
council, he was a good road-maker ; as a legis-
lator, he was a nonentity. The great public work
of his life was to bring about the amalgamation of
the municipalities of Cook and Camperdown. The
two together only made 435 acres, and are now
insolvent.
Died recently at Devonport, near Auck-
land (M.L.), Mrs. Wynyard. She was the half-
caste daughter of a Captain Benson, who settled
in the Bay of Islands in the long ago, and took
unto himself land and a number of dusky wives.
Mrs. Wynyard, although descended from one of
the big gun chiefs, never made herself ridiculous
by claiming the title “princess,” as some recent
humbugs have done, especially when visiting
England. In some respects she was a remarkable
woman, and was at one time of her life exposed to
severe derision by scientific men for having
claimed to nave seen two living moas. She was
10 years old, or thereabouts, at the time, and all
the north end of the island was in a state of
excitement over an impending marriage. Among
the live stock to be slaughtered on the occasion
were two moas, which Mrs. Wynyard (then Miss
Benson) saw tethered to trees by thick ropes of
twisted flax. Scientists at that time declared
that the moa antedated the glacial period, and
stigmatised Miss Benson’s statement as utter rot.
She was asked to describe the bird, which at that
time had not been rebuilt. Twenty years
later Von Haast got the first complete skeleton of
a moa, and the reconstructed bird was found to
tally in every detail with the description given by
the half-caste lady. Ever since then Science has
been wearing its considering-cap right down over
its noble brow.
“ H.” writes :
The Rev. A. R. Edgar, the Melbourne Methodist parson
who lately acquired public sympathy owing to Senator
Dawson’s attacks on him, is a large, heavy-going indivi-
dual, who is accustomed to pose as the embodiment of
fearlessness. It is now about 10 years since Edgar was
charged with having arisen and denounced members of
the Vic. Assembly as “liars and profligates.” He was
summoned to the bar of the House to prove his words,
and there he rather died away, and said that he had been
misreporbed. Edgar is a slow and deliberate speaker
who is easy to report, and the scribe who caused the
trouble came forward with a declaration, backed up by
witnesses, that the words attributed to Edgar were
actually used. Edgar said that if they were (which he
didn’t remember), he didn’t intend his remarks to be
taken that way. He departed amid something of a
general smile. For some time his stock was pretty low,
but he has contrived to rehabilitate himself a bit. Still,
he is conspicuously a man with a lost opportunity. If he
had neither backed down nor apologised, the Assembly,
having once taken the matter up, would have found
itself with a large parson on its hands. If;it had gaoled
him it would have been a huge advertisement for Edgar,
and he could have eaten up the whole surplus in board.
There wasn’t enough Victorian surplus to feed a big
parson ten years ago, and it would have broken the
Treasurer’s heart to see it eaten.
According to Adelaide Herald,
S. A. Chief Justice Way is a very busy
man. He is Lieutenant-Governor,
Chief Justice, Chancellor of the Uni-
versity, Chairman of the Public
Library, Museum, and Art Gallery,
one of the Board of the Botanic
Gardens, of the Horticultural Society,
and the Zoological Society. In addi-
tion he is President of the Society of
Arts, Vice-president of the Society for
the Protection of Birds, a sheep-
farmer, a yacht club patron, several
sorts of hospital committeeman, and
patron of various societies. It is un-
kindly suggested that in that long list
is to be found part of the reason why
Mr. Way has been hatching one judg-
ment for a year and eight months.
Vic. Premier Bent grows more
brilliant every week, more airily persi-
flageous and witty. Talking at Chil-
well (a drowsy outpost of Geelong,
Vic.) his replies to interjectors were
as cutting as potatoes. To a small
youthful person who questioned his
moral courage, Tommy the Sparkler
shouted “ Six stone eight could tackle
and beat you ; ” and to an old hairy-
faced man he yelled, “If my father
was like you I’d put him in the Zoo-
logical Gardens.” There’s no silencing
Bent. He’s so ready with his tongue.
Smaller men may boast their flowers of speech,
but the grand old market gardenerraises huge
vegetables of oratory from his mental soil.
“ Dolly de Vere ” was the kittenish fluffy-
sounding name of a witness in a police
court the other day. Pussy complained that she
had been taken down for 10s. by a monster of
iniquity who promised her a billet; and the Court,
after hearing what Tottie had to say, decided that
the man who allegedly took in Flossie should be
committed for trial.
J. C. Tucker, who has passed out at the
age of 90, and F. A. Cooper, who still lingers at
St. Kilda,' Melbourne, were big men in the early
wine and spirit business of Sydney. The Tucker
firm still survives, but the Cooper one has
crumbled into many hands. Tucker disliked
politics, but Cooper sat for a time as member for
Braidwood. They both drank little of the liquor
they vended.
After over 40 years’ service as R.C. priest
in the Western District, Monsignor O’Donovan
leaves Mudgee to reside in Sydney. He has been
38 years in Mudgee alone, his previous station
being Orange. In his day Rev. O’Donovan was a
noted horseman, and took short cuts across his
parish on his big black horse, fences, gullies,
fallen trees, and sundries being matters of indif-
ference to him.
In S.M. Herald of Saturday, Mr. W. L.
Vardy, Sydney solicitor, gives some reminiscences
of his fifty years residence in Australia. During
the half century he has known nine Chief Justices,
and 33 puisne Judges. Of the 42 only 12 remain
on the Bench to-day, Samuel Way being the
senior C.J. Mr. Vardy practised 40 years ago
with George Higinbotham, afterwards Chief
Justice of Victoria, and he served as a full private
in the volunteer corps to which Redmond Barry
belonged. When the 40th Regiment left Mel-
bourne to take part in the Maori war, Vardy and
George Higinbotham did sentry duty at the
Treasury and Government House: Higinbotham
was then a private in the Brighton Corps. Mr.
Vardy has practised in three States—S. A., Vic.,
and N.S.W.
“ Egmont ” on the late Richard Seddon :
How the superincumbent and fraudulent weight of
aristocracy would have kept him down in England, while
young Lord Nozoo would have been trumpeted as a
genius, when not fit to black Dick's boots ! He was in-
finitely more wonderful than Whittington. Coming to
Australia, he took bis schoolmaster father’s advice, to
“go with the crowd.” Seddon, Vogel, Stout! What
Victoria lost. The stamp of men who made Ballarat and
Bendigo were most]} 1 driven out of Australia. I reckon
that from 1879 to 1890 Seddon, in the Maoriland Parlia-
ment, only seemed a troublesome Boanerges, like
Don in Victoria. When Ballance passed out, Stout
reckoned, like the Grand Inquisitor in the “ Gondoliers,”
that there was “ no possible doubt whatever” of what
would happen next—viz., that, though one of the Outs,
he would be summoned as - Premier. But let a man
beware how he loses touch in politics or journalism.
Dick the Digger put Stout’s pug out of joint, and dug
down a foundation that lasted him to the end of his
days. In the later Seddon you came to a man with the
dimensions of Lincoln, the kind to whom one can assign
no hounds. 1 have a colored print of the coronation.
Next to the panjandrum, King Dick decidedly sticks out
most. Seddon and Grey were the only eminent Anglo-
Maorilanders who imbibed and absorbed the Maori
spirit—Seddon much more thoroughly and pleasantly
than Hori, although nothing like so good on the lan-
guage. Now that Elijah has gone where is Elisha?
Nicholas Walpole Raven, the old gentle-
man of the Petersham (Sydney) alleged shooting
case—he was acquitted on the ground that he
bombarded in self-defence—resided in Brisbane
some time ago, and once opposed the Muckle-
wrath Ministry at the polls. This was in ’93.
Raven was not taken seriously by the electors,
and his meetings were a thing of joy and a shriek
for ever. The result at Brisbane North (which
returned two members) was MTlwraith, 1570 •
Kingsbury, 1482; Chas. Lilley, 947 ; T. Glassey,’
766; Raven, 55. Still three candidates struck
worse luck than his ; one dejected person named
Banks scored 15 at Barcoo.
Harry Levien, M.P. (N.S.W.) writes :
Re comment (Bulletin, 31/5/’O6) on a paragraph of my
recollections as “Father of the House,” published in
Sydney Telegraph, I should like to point out that whilst
your critic is perfectly correct in the view he takes of
diaries, at the same time he scarcely appreciates old
John Robertson’s distrust of the particular “political
diary ” kept by his contemporary, Henry Parkes. It was
not the use but the abuse of the diary that provoked the
hostility and aroused the ire of John Robertson. I was
speaking from memory of a debate in N.S.W. Assembly
of 20 years ago, and made only a passing allusion to the
idea in Robertson’s mind that “ the man who kept a diary
was dangerous.” The fact that Parkes kept a diary, and
that he had made statements founded on it in
order to justify his change of attitude towards his old
friend and one-time colleague, nettled Robertson.
The politician who keeps a diary in order that he may
some day use it against a colleague (who does not keep a
diary) is certainly dangerous. If conversations and con-
clusions in confidential conference are to be committed
to paper, then the colleague should confirm the record
and have a copy of it, otherwise he is not safe against
some future use of it. The diary may be unfair and in-
complete, and only the clearest memory may prevail
against it. Again, politicians in friendly intercourse,
just like private friends in confidential speech, do not
expect minutes to be secretly taken—and partially taken
—of the conversation. If they did, then they would he
ou guard, and there would be an end .to trust and con-
fidence. A diary, in the circumstances, is something
that must be counted suspicious, and its use dangerous.
Of course, the diary of a journalist, or of one who intends
to write memoirs, or biographies, or the history of his
time, is not only defensible, but creditable as well. It
was not against such John Robertson railed. lam not
writing this in any captious spirit, for The Bulletin has
for over 20 years treated me with the greatest kindness
and consideration ; hut I do wish to clear a misconception
as to John Robertson’s real attitude towards diaries—a
misconception, I am bound to say, due to the brevity of
the reference to the subject in my recollections.
A Bar story. She was a demure
little lady witness, and the sharp way in which
barrister Gannon cross-examined her seemed to
annoy N.S. W. Chief Justice Darley. For really
the lady didn’t look as if butter would melt in her
mouth. Then the next witness came, and he
testified that the demure little lady had used
“shocking language.” “ I shan’t ask you to sav
what it was ” said Mr. Gannon. “Oh ” broke in
the Chief Justice “I should like to hear what
you call shocking language ! No need to be so
squeamish, Mr. Gannon.” His Honor evidently
had a fixed opinion that the remarks made by
such a demure little lady couldn’t be anythin-?
serious. The witness started to give the language”
which was red and terrible : “She said 1
!!***!! J ——” “ Wait a minute. A little more
slowly,” broke in Mr. Gannon. “His Honor
wants this. Now watch his Honor’s pen.” Hi s
Honor put on his air of severest dignity, as he
didn’t “ take it down.” But it was evident that
in his mind that demure little lady’s name had be-
come Mud-Miss Amelia Jane Mud.
“ W.M.8.” reminisces :
The Dick Seddon I met in Sydney in May, 1906, was
scarcely the same man I knew in Durban in May, 1902.
The intervening four years had whitened his skin, dimmed
his eyes, and left his gigantic frame limp and draggy.
There was still left in him a spark of that fierce fire that
had burnt so long, but it was the will and not the dis-
position that roused him to interest. lie was obviously
worked to almost the finish, and heavily-laden nature
was collapsing under the incessant burden. But in South
Africa the indefatigable Richard was stout and strong as
a locomotive, and a puzzle to the people whose tempera-
ment was personified in “ Waate-een-beetje.” There was
no wait awhile about Seddon. He had arrived with a
solution of the three years’argument of words and of war
with the Boers, and it took more than the press censor
to turn off his riot of rhetoric. He could not understand
why Milner and Kitchener did not retire in his favor
from the peace conference at Veeriniging and allow him
to deal with Botha, De Wet, De la Rey, and Smuts. He
could not understand any other terms than unconditional
surrender, and the fact that there was plenty of fight left
in the Boers never occurred to him. “He was the
most rampant Imperialist in South Africa, and quite a
new and embarrassing problem to the leaders of the
British and a first-class curiosity to the Boers. But,
somehow, he did not get himself disliked by either side.
He was voted honest, but ill-informed. He was soon
rigorously press-censored and, no doubt, much was whis-
pered in his ear. He subsided into silence regarding
military matters and peace problems, but continued to
enthuse over the Empire. At the Cape he was made much
of by the three parties—the Jingoes, the Bondmen and the
Moderates—and the sturdy vitality of the Maoriland
statesman won popularity. He was the lion of Capetown
for a week, and when he was not dining or being hospit-
ably entertained and eulogised, he was scattering cables
to the four quarters of the world about his views.
A REALLY WELL-BALANCED
INTELLECT.
Mr. Trey car (Maoriland Secretary of Labor) is a very
emotional man of pronounced opinions which he cannot
refrain from preaching in his departmental reports.—
Argus.
Fat : “ Gimme a man of no opinions, or else
fat ones, like mine! ”
Panton, P.M., who long ago passed the
statutory age of retirement —he was born in Scot-
land over 75 years ago—is now being assailed by
the Melbourne Labor Leagues, on account of his
treatment of the Melville Island sailors. Brunswick
Laborites urge Panton’s removal on the ground
that his “erratic decisions and manner of treating
witnesses clearly show his incapacity to hold
office.” Panton’s methods in the City Court make
lawyers, witnesses and reporters sit up and gasp.
His blunders in the Melville Island case have
raised a storm that he will find it hard to weather,
and the ancient vessel is in troubled waters.
TRIBUTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One greater than a king
Hath from the world been taken.
A mighty soul takes wing,
A country lies forsaken,
A voice is heard in weeping
And bitter lamentation,
The Isles a vigil keeping
For their grief-stricken nation.
A mighty voice is still,
AVe hear no more in thunder
That resolute “ I will ”
AVhich cleaved the foes asunder.
A stalwart prince of men,
High planets may he tread on ;
A noble citizen—
We loved thee, Richard Seddon.
No struggle and no pain—
Thy very death was royal ;
Unto the people plain
Thou wert for ever loyal.
A wreath of laurel leaves
Thy brow doth deck with glory.
Each heart’s a loom that weaves
Thee in thy country’s story ;
It showeth clear and far
In blaze of triple splendor,
Thy great and steadfast star—
The nation’s strong defender.
The people’s chosen chief,
Strong-purposed and reliant,
Australia bows in grief
Beside the Fallen Giant.

The Genesis of the Organ Man.
OF MUSICAL INSTRUMENTS, it may be said THE ORGAN
is one of THE OLDEST, for in Genesis iv., 21, we read of
JUBAL, a PERSON of that period :
“HE WAS THE FATHER of all such as
HANDLE THE HARP AND ORGAN.”
WHAT THE ORGAN of those EARLY DAYS was like, IT IS
NOT EASY to conjecture, but it may be assumed it WAS
ALTOGETHER DIFFERENT from THE ORGAN of to-day.
MUSICAL SCIENCE was in its infancy. The melodious JEW’S
HARP and the EAR-SPLITTING DRUM were yet to come, and
NEITHER THE SOL FA METHOD NOR THE MUSICAL
GLASSES had been heard of.
IT WAS FOR LONG-AFTER CENTURIES
TO PRODUCE AND PERFECT . . .
THE. jz?
PAC HARD Organ,
and it was for THOUSANDS living in the NINETEENTH and
TWENTIETH CENTURIES to say
“THE PACKARD ORGAN
IS THE BEST.”
What is the VERDICT of Music ? The MUSICAL PROFES-
SION says THE PACKARD ORGAN IS THE BEST, and the
MUSICAL WORLD admits it!
GhQ Pacßard Organ.
is the ORGAN of all others to STAND ROUND when THE
HOURS OF EVENING FALL, and FAMILY VOICES break
forth into SONG AND PRAISE.

Foodless, Shirtless, and Brainless.
In the Westminster Gazette (London) Miss
Frances Campbell has just been describing her
experiences in a visit to a school in a poor quarter
of London. The school contained 600 children.
It provided a midday meal for every one of them,
and had managed, by contract with a bootmaker,
to get them all shod—Government and charity
finding 2s. and the parents Is. for each pair of
boots. Miss Campbell talked wi th the youngsters,
and quotes (as a typical instance) one boy of six
years whose mother was in hospital, whose father
was looking for work, and who, when not attend-
ing school, looked after his three younger brothers
or sisters. Says Miss Campbell :
This child had one meal a day —at the school.
Another case was that of Ned, a boy with the
stamp of country birth on his features.
The head-master stooped and absently thrust a finger
beneath the hoy’s collar, which lay pinned tightly across
his neck. He opened the coat, and showed an expanse of
fair chest. Ned wore no shirt.
Unfed, unclothed children. Look at the result
The head-inaster passed by the first line of desks, and
said, in a loud tone, “ There is too much noise in this
class-room.” Immediately on the following stillness, he
pointed at the boy nearest him, and asked, “ What did I
say when I came into this room ? ”
The boy rose and thought. “ There is—there is— ”He
could remember no more, though the words were spoken
at his elbow.
The head-master stepped into the middle of the class-
room, and repeated the sentence slowly and emphatically ;
then asked what boy could remember it.
Only one boy out of the whole class, numbering about
20, could remember beyond the first three words.
Nature takes vengeance on the foolish nation.
Nothing but bread and meat will suffice to make
brains. When the old man is out of work, his
children starve into useless imbecility. It is of
no use-to provide schools and Universities for a
race whose stomachs are empty. Australia has
thousands of men thrown out of work because of
a futile, make-believe tariff. Keep them out of
work long enough, and their children will become
incompetents whom no tariff will help.
♦
When the British Tory sets out to do things, he
is thorough. In East Dorset, one Churchill, a
fishmonger, is a Liberal, and this is the note he
received from a she-Tory customer :
Ciiurciiill,—l have decided to deal elsewhere for fish,
so you need not call here again ; and I should like your
trifling account sent in. It has got to a serious pitch
now, and Conservatives must hold together and support
each other.—Yours, etc., B. Radclyffe.
“Churchill,” a poor wretch with a sick wife,
wrote a pitiful letter, begging to be allowed to
call with fish, and pointing out how he had never
done Mrs. Radclyffe any harm, and how he
respected her, etc., etc. She replied in a cold
way that she was sorry for him and his wife, but
she couldn’t deal with him. The letter concluded
thus: —
I hear a report that the Conservative employers o
labor in this and other counties are about to form a re-
solution that they will not in future employ either
tradesmen or laborers who do not support the Unionist
cause. I cannot wonder at this, as it is the object of the
Liberals to do away with all the landed gentry and others
in England. —Yours, etc., B. Radclyffe.
Our Australian Tories can borrow several hints
from this episode. First, there is a new way
of writing to fishmongers and other scum like
“Churchill”; secondly, everyone ought to be
sacked who doesn’t vote for Knox, and Bruce
Smith, and Joke Cook, and Mrs. Dobson’s hus-
band.
“Bois” : Is the Corrupt Practices Act in opera-
tion in N.S.W., or does it apply only to Timbuctoo
and Iceland ? Also, does it apply to the civil ser-
vice, or only to tramps and cadgers ? I ask because
things in that line (corrupt practices) seem to be
as brisk as ever. I’m in business, and, when the
Act was passed, I bought a big money-box to put
my prospective savings in. I looked in the box
the other day, but found nothing. Royal Com-
missions have lately been shaking things up.
How would it be to have a Royal Commission to
inquire into how the aforesaid Act is working ?
And for witnesses, call all Government con-
tractors. One contractor of my acquaintance told
me he would never dream of sending a con-
signment of timber to the Government without
going down himself and “making things right.
And,” said he, “ a pound spent properly saves you
hundreds.” I can believe it, because this man, so
far as I can learn, never has anything con-
demned. Others (who “ make things right ”) can
be as long as they like in sending in their goods,
while a poor wretch (who has not the capacity for
“making things right”) has no sooner signed his
contract, and begun to congratulate himself upon
having a “Guv’ment job,” than telegrams showei
upon him, threatening him with all sorts of pains
and penalties if he does not hurry up. Gasping
with terror, and his eyes bulging out of his head
like hat-pegs, he rushes around and does his best,
but the nearer he gets to carrying out the con-
ditions of his contract, the thicker become the
threatening messages, until at last he begs to be
allowed to forfeit his deposit and live at peace.
After he has got free, his job is handed over to
someone who can “make things right,” and the
victim is more than astonished to see how long
the second man lingers over the job, and how com-
fortably he takes things. I know what I’m talk-
ing about. I’ve been there. So let’s have a Royal
Commission.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 19, 1906.
My Dear Arini.—
For a few months—these winter months—Lake
Cowal Station, where Governor Rawson, Miss
Rawson, and son Wyatt have gone on a visit, is
“a place full of sweet trees and color of glad
grass. The glorified waterhole that gives a name
to the station may also have a few feet of wet in
it and support a boat, and the verdure is probably
so long that you could tie it over the horses,
backs. In the summer-time the country is mostly a
dust-pan. The ,T. B. Donkin’s homestead (the
Donkins own the place and host his Ex. and
party) is only a lathe-and-plaster cottage, but
the; proprietors are mentally well-furnished
and their own physical architecture is of
the, best. Private secretaries and aides have
been at Lake Cowal homestead by the
bunch, and enjoyed regular Lindsay Gordon gal-
lops after game. The district teems with Austra-
lian ammals that men pursue with guns and dogs.
And at night the brolgas come out and do their
strange ballet around the lake or in its dry bed
limn, of course, the air is delicate, and the holy
ji • u 1 - S g enerall y unbroken. The homestead
which is enclosed in snake-proof netting, is a 25-
hiile drive from Wyalong, a township which
drowses within a wide, wide belt of mallee
faking it all around, it’s just an ideal place for
tbe young Australian story-writer to stand the
pither tottery legs of her “local color” short
story upon.
Gordon, K.C., who goes hence to marry
Miss Margaret Thomas, ate a congratu-
latory dinner from the Equity Bar previous to
putting on his deck shoes and unlimbering his
deck chairs. He takes
all the wish-you-joys ”of his friends like a cat
lapping cream. Sometimes he tells how he came
f n- Ut rf th f« «T, and dum P until the mid-summer
ot bis life. When I was a quite young man I

toOO a year. But it seemed inadequate when it
and 1 de termmed to defer the event till
£lOOO a year was assured. But that, too, seemed
insufficient, and so did £2OOO per annum when it
was there, and I told myself ‘Just you wait till
it s three. When that sum and that time came
the girl.was not in sight.”
That’s all very well, but the high gods take a
hand, in these matters, and one of the Lightning
JSw f°i? 8 i 0 bveup among the lightning
don t they ?) had already ear-marked a little
Welsh rarebit, away North in the country of coal
and religious revivals, as the K.C.’s mate) and one
day a few months Ago hq-the god-looked at the
leaf.on which Gordon s fate is written, and then

was filling up his nectar-glass, to send a marconi-

Miss- Thomas will be accompanied back to her
liome by Miss Hodges, a lady who met her whilst
both were studying music at some or other
academy. Every other singer and actress of note
has one special woman satellite who either comes
out here with her, or begins to move in her orbit
when she arrives. The satellite is moneyed, and
has an infinite capacity for admiration of the star
and for self-effacement. Melba had one; Miss
*lorelh has another; Miss Palotta has hers.
Miss Brune is also well provided for in the
matter of feminine worship. But getting back to
the marriage of the fascinating footlight lady : It
has been _ said that every woman is an actress
(garden of Eden Adam called her a serpent) so an
acfress is doubly a woman, therefore doubly fasci-
nating. But all this is dust in the mouth to the
swarm of Sydney society girls who must stand and
see their best matches select the imported juiube
to sweeten his road through life. Miss Knight
Molhson took our notable Tin Kelly II (she was
as graceful beyond the footlights as an antelope and
her smile might allure an oyster off his rock so
ttiere isn t much cause for wonder there) Now
comes Miss Thomas, with her big eyes and sweet
smgingfand annexes Gordon. It’s enough to make
the Society girl jump on her best hat, or nav to
go, on the stage. J
Cbi ® f impression of Grace Palotta, off the stage
is of her exuberant health. She should surely
write a book on physical culture or diet, or both
with a title, “ How to be always young and
debonair.’’ Stage life is hard L Twoman’s
health and looks, as a rule. Theatres are neces-
sanly poor in oxygen, and the acting art calls for
long hours. Meals must be often irregular and
tor the actor or actress there is always lurking the
deadly snare of late suppers (a cynic friend of
mine_ keeps a table of mortality, or something
showing the actuarial effect on the actress’ life of
lobster and cold beef at midnight or there-
abouts). Further the “make-up” is injurious
to the skin .unless used with the utmost
precautions. But Miss Palotta-who is at least
over 17—shows to-day the skin of a child, the step
and the fresh vitality of a school-girl. That is off
the stage with no “make-up.”
The secret ? She makes no secret of it. “ Start-
ing the day at the wrong, end—l believe in as
early to bed as the theatre allows of. After a
performance no supper, if I can possibly avoid it
Suppers sometimes can’t be avoided. They are
always a trial. Usually I have a cup of warm
milk, and then I don’t hear 12 strike In
the morning a hot bath, a real' wallow in
very hot water. One needs it at least once
a day to get rad ,of the- dust and the
grease of stage life. Then exercises—Swedish and
Sandow exercises—until I am thoroughly warm
Then a cold plunge. Yes, always, winter and
summer. Breakfast, very light. No, I’m not
faddy as to food. - But not too much. Fruit
grains, perhaps an egg. Rehearsal or study takes
up mosfc.of the morning, but I always insist on
getting in a walk. That is, perhaps, the greatest
thing of all,, m my opinion— to get as much time
in the open air as possible. I always get my walk
m, even if I have to cut short the sleeping time.
Usually, too, X walk to and from the theatre.
V Now lunch, that is a meal. I make the most
°l *** • d° I ea t ? Anything that is nice and
that likes me. For the rest of the day, very little.
If I were to dine, in the sense of taking a big meal,
I would not feel as if I could do my best on the
stage. So tea is a very light affair, taken before
dressing for the theatre. The afternoon is usually
kept busy with calls; and there are books to read
—I am studying your English literature very hard,
hoping to know a little of it some day—and letters
to write; and a thousand and one other things.
One must even go to a dressmaker’s or photo-
grapher’s now and again. But, if I can, I get
some open air among the trees in the afternoon.
“Tea? Yes, I drink tea and I drink coffee,
and I drink wine, and I drink water, and I drink
almost anything, but very moderately, except,
perhaps, milk and water. lam a little greedy
with milk and water. Would you think I was
greedy of milk-and-water? And I don’t worry.
I eeling glad is good, and so I always try to feel
glad.”
The recipe is excellent. But—but, lam afraid
it comes back in the end to a matter of tempera-
ment. All this careless carefulness might fail in
another just for want of “feeling glad.” Still it’s
an interesting Confession of a Healthy Woman.
Little Leila Doubleday is in Sydney, preparing
for her concert at the Y.M.O.A. Hall on Saturday,
the 30th. She proves to be a merry, unspoilt
voungster of twelve and a half, with curly dark
brovyn hair, brown eyes with drooping lids, and a
sensitive mouth. Also she is straight and well-
grown. When playing or listening to music
her face almost grows sad in its seriousness.
Away from music she is just a wide-smiling, jolly
child. At two years of age she sang to an audi-
ence of some hundreds of grown-ups, but that was
at a school. It is as a pianiste and violiniste she
will appear in Sydney. Melbourne thought
enough of her to roll up at her farewell con-
cert to the extent of £450 profit. At ten
years of age she took a scholarship (piano) at
Marshall Hall’s Conservatorium from all-comers,
some of them nearly old enough to be her mother
or her father. The following year she got it
again, and would have gone on catching it if rules
had permitted. Which instrument—piano or
violin—Leila will elect as the chosen one where-
with to play her way to stardom neither she nor
her advisers have yet decided. Some of the tip-
top musicians of Sydney who have heard her play
assert that she has a most accurate ear, alive to
every shade and color of musical expression, and
that the interpretations are keen and original and
possessing nervous force. The last possession
being for a girl, and a child at that, something
more than it sounds.
To-night Mrs. Doubleday and her gifted lamb
are the guests of the management at “The Man
from Mexico.” Miss Palotta, who shows a great
interest in the young musician, has taken that,
the only available chance, to express her glad
wishes. There is some talk of a reception at the
Australia. Miss Marie Narelle has promised to
interest herself, and there is talk of Lady North-
cote doing the same. Also Rivers Allpress has
offered her a lesson a day during her stay here;
so Leila has the right to wear a smile.
Miss Marie Narelle, wearing a tailor-made and
her Tara brooch, got out of the train at Redfern
into the arms of friends, and started what up to
the present has been a perfectly dizzy time. A
tidal wave of welcomers overwhelmed her. But,
like most “arrived” singers, she is very strong!
Imagine her being able to appear twice a day for
the seven months of the St. Louis Ex. ! Premier
Carruthers and a Citizens’ Reception Committee
gave her a comet-like night at the Town Hall and
presented her with an address. On Saturday,
Miss Narelle’s first concert was a Conquest.
Her voice is as fresh as of old, and as
she studied all she could with Steffani in
London she has gained in musicianship. Lady
Northcote, escort*! by Private-Secretaries Share
and Stephens, was an absorbed listener, although
I believe her little feet in their pale-pink satin
shoes must have been damp. It was a pouring
wet night, and the steps were to match. Mister
Spruson, Mister Shipman, and the rest of yez—
where was that red carpet ?
First aid to the Vice-regal party was rendered
by Mr. Southwell, and after the tumult and the
shouting died at the end of the concert, her Ex.
said the equivalent of “I’m glad I came.”
Marie Narelle’s gown of emerald-green chiffon
velvet, with panels of Irish point lace from corsage
to hem, was a splendid robe, but its magnificence
is too middle-aged for Marie. In her hair was a
coruscating half-wreath of green laurel. However,
in dressing and programming, she honored the
land of her people. And even those present who
were not of that land, with loud smiting of palms
showed appreciation.
As for the new tenor, Chester Fentress, the
audience took him to its heart after hearipg him
warble but a few notes, and the subsequent
applause was spontaneous, every clap of it, though
both his opening numbers were sung in French.
Fentress received a floral gilt horseshoe, which he
accepted as one who says, “Take away this
bauble ! ” His voice has a silvery quality, and
his manner gives the impression that he would
only say “ Thank you ” if one told him the house
was on fire.
Miss_ Mary Innes, daughter of the late Justice
Innes, is back in Sydney, after much going l to and
fro on the countenance of the globe. She is a tall
girl with a complexion, and carries her tailor-made
garniture to advantage. It will be remembered that
the young lady’s mother was married recently in
Paris to ex-French Consul Biard d’ Auriet. It
must be a great responsibility off a girl’s mind to
have a mother, who still wears her own hair and a
twenty-one inch corset, comfortably re-settled in
life,
A year or so ago five well-known, nice Sydney
girls got married within the one week. This
month the whole group is mourning the
death of its first-born. Two of the bereaved
young mothers, who were respectively Miss Enid
Whiting and Miss Mildred Coffee, were wedded
on the same day, one to Captain Waters, the other
to Dr. Kelly of North Shore,
A correspondent comments on the fact that the
flag at Government House, Sydney, was a notable
exception to others on the day Mr. Seddon’s
death was announced, in not being half-masted.
But the rule is that the Government House jack
—which is hoisted only when the Governor is in
residence there—may not be half-masted except
on the death of the British Sovereign, or by
special instructions from the British Court. I
am informed, however, that instructions have
been received warranting the half-masting of the
flag to-day (the funeral day) as a mark of respect
for the deceased statesman.
That cabled picture of the body of Dick Seddon
carried ashore in the cold starlight while the
tolling of bells and the firing of guns reverberated
from the hills, and Maori chiefs uttered cries of
grief, is curiously affecting. The hills of the
Town Belt crowd in round Wellington city and
by starlight seem to hang over the crater-harbor.
The wail of the tanyi, the measured boom of
minute guns, and the tolling of bells would all
come back in echoes with quite creepy effect. In
Wellington it either blows a gale or is dead calm,
and the stillness of the small hours of the morn-
ing under the shadow of the everlasting hills,
always strikes the wayfarer from this side, used to
the continuance of city noises in places like Syd-
ney and Melbourne which never quite go to bed.
Captain Wilson, of Sir Harry Rawson’s staff, is
going to take himself and, of course, his lovely
brown eyes and teeth away back to England.
What will Cranbrook do without his manage-
ment ? He is a marvellously skilful director and
organiser, and the Rawsons will certainly feel like
collapsed puff-paste after his departure.
Mrs. Bland Holt schemed in color when she
started to redecorate “Sevenoaks,” the romantic
high-walled-in house with the three oaks, in a
Darlinghurst lane. Outside it is painted baby-
leaf green; so are the garden seats and the garden
roller, the watering-cans and the laughing-jack-
asses. Inside, all is pale amber—otherwise, the
color of the sunlight. The idea is for artistic self-
deception. Missus wants to make believe that
it s always spring. Spring sunshine filtering into
the rooms, spring leafage rustling outside. As
for the lady herself, she’s got the same old spring
in her step, and her semi-barbaric, gypsy-like
jewellery touches the spot, so to speak, in our
retina.
Maoriland Tourist Chief Donne is back from
Melbourne, and even in his dreams confesses to be
still pursuing premises in which to plant the
agency that will take the poor English tourist by
the eye glass and show him how good God has
been—to Maoriland. Although the death of his
great chief has not taken any of the buckram
out of I.E.D. it has affected him so far that he
now declines to be rushed around in the night air
to banquets where twenty-five toasts are honored
in a half hour speech apiece. An ex-pressman
from Auckland, Mr. Montgomery, well-known to
many Australians as the acute and courteous
Auckland rep. of the M.L. Tourist Bureau, has
now been appointed Sydney agent for the
Bureau.

White of Mudgee, sheep-breeder and racehorse

J-aroorn, Queensland. (No prize is offered to the

‘‘The Primate (Saumarez Smith) and five of
his daughters have left Marseilles by the Oruba
for Sydney.” Five of his daughters ! Of course
it’s not as startling as the late G. R. Dibbs’
unfinished story, “when I and ten of my daugh-
ters were crossing George-street to-day,” used to
be, but it s enough. Some of the girl Smithereens
have been left behind, then, by Primate Smith—
the married ones, of course. And the marriage
of his son, the Rev. S.S., to Miss Hanbury has
been postponed on account of the bridegroom-
elect’s fall from a horse. But that’s not as bad as
a fall from grace.
Maoriland’s greatest matrimonial catch passed
through Sydney from Europe last week (or may-
u tt S here) cn route to one of his homes in
the Hawkes Bay district. He is six-feet-two, a
great oarsman, wears his chest half a foot in front
of him, and rakes in an income of some £20,000 a
year. His flocks are white on many a hill. Also
he s getting into the forties. Isn’t it Pinero who
asserts that the magnetic age in man is the bunch
of years between forty-three and fifty-five—that
any nice bloque within that group of seasons can
sing successfully, even without much voice: “ I
fear no foe of twenty-five in shining armor drest;
he couldn’t cut me out.”
For the first time, so far as I know, a law has
been passed against the wearing of trailing skirts
in the streets. The penalty for each offence is a
fane of thirty shillings. A town in Hanover is the
courageous place that has started to cut the human
female tail.
Miss Roland Watts Phillips is doing a little
writing these mushroom-growing days in the
Evening News. She drives a light and pleasant
pen and is very philosophical though naturally a
little bitter over the collapse of the Stephenson
company and the twenty-one weeks’ salary owing
to her. There isn’t a cleverer or more original
actress m her line than “ Roley,” and she deserves
better luck than she’s had the last few years.
Mr. Knibbs, of the picturesque white hair and
the wonderful conversational powers, had the
usual good-bye meal given him at the A.B.C.
before going to Melbourne to frolic with the
Commonwealth statistics. Over 200 whiskered
or moustached or clean-shaven Somebodies ate in
his honor. Mrs. Knibbs had appendicitis some
few years ago, and has not been about much
There is a daughter “out,” and some
Knibbs boys.
Artist Dattilo-Rubbo, who leaves Australia bv
and-bye for a visit to his native Vatican land
tells how he came to be cast up on our shores jH ( >
and a merry troop of art students, studying m
Milan, used to go down to the quay at luncheon
hour to see new faces coming off the ships. On
one occasion they saw a beautiful girl pacing the
deck of a liner. She was so lovely that half of
them fell to sketching her, and ail fell in love
with her. Bribing an interpreter with a bottle of
wine, the man discovered for them that the ladv
was an Australian. Till then, the artist
had thought Australians were black. A few
years later Dattilo-Rubbo went down to
the quay to leave for a foreign land. He
did not care an olive which land. “ Where does
that vessel go to ? ” “To the Transvaal.” “Ah
Ido not like the name. Where does that one
go ? ” “To Australia.” He liked that name and
he remembered the lady. So he came here—in
’97. It is only lately, after nine years, and speak-
ing the language capitally, that Datillo-Rubbo
learnt that the expression “for good” is a collo-
quialism. When asked “if he were going for
good,” he would say “Yes,” meaning that he
expected to benefit by the trip. Not until tear-
sodden letters from former and intending students
began to rea,ch him, asking him not to go for good
did he see light.
In the current fashionable novels you get very
mbblesome crumbs of description of the menu
sandwiched between the brilliant conversation of
the diners. In “The Transgression of Andrew
Vane, ’ one of the characters (at a dinner) is
“ deeply engaged in dressing little morsels of
smelts in overcoats of sauce tartare, assisting them
carefully with his knife to scramble aboard his
fork, and having braced them there firmly with
cubes of creamed potatoes, conveying the whole
arrangement to his month, where he instantly
secured it from escape by popping in a piece of
bread upon its heels.”
A quiet wedding took place at “ Fintona,” the
residence of Mr. A. G. Thompson, solicitor,
Bathurst, when that gentleman’s eldest daughter,
Lucy Ethel, was wedded to Mr. R. M. Drum-
mond, chief Government Surveyor, New Guinea.
The Thompsons are “ old folk ” in Bathurst. The
grandfather of the bride was postmaster in the
City of the Plains over G 5 years ago, and on his
retirement his son, W. G. Thompson, got the
billet just 40 years ago, and holds it still. The
son entered the post office service in 1863. Father
and son have, therefore, obliterated the postage
stamp in Bathurst for something like three gene-
rations.
According to the Freeman’s Journal Mrs.
Catherine Byrne, the mother of Mrs, Sydney
Burdekin, died at A.ttuhga, in the Tamworth
district, at the end of last month, aged 86. The
Byrne family are old identities in the dairy
business.
Pianist Godfrey Smith, who is cabled as having
done something wonderful in London in the way
of teaching a piano to take a joke (as some Yankee
humorist has said), is a nephew of De Rego, the
musical man of the Telegraph. He was a pupil
of the late Sidney Moss.

court. The lawyer : “So prisoner persuaded you

This item comes from Ballarat:—
The subtlety of the municipal personage reached a
gorgeous stage in Ballarat on Friday. The people who
take charge ot the Common wanted trees planted, and as
labor costs money, it was decided to have recourse to
philanthropy. So it was ordained that the State school
kids should have an Arbor Day, at which local snobs
could pose as the children’s friends, and get the planting
done for next to nothing. Three hundred little boys
of requisite toughness were accordingly marched
off on a three miles tramp at 9 a.m. The
Common is the bleakest spot outside the Antarctic, and
in the teeth of an icy wind the wretched little children
were set to work with pick and-shovel. There were 130
holes to be dug. Under the eye of “teacher” (whose
teeth chattered in his head), the ironstone Was carefully
extracted, and the soil broken up and put back. The
kids had to bring their own dinners, but partook of some
sloppy tea generously provided for them. A number of
august persons arrived during th» afternoon, in time to
attitudinise in a sheltered / >ee. The Orphan
Asylum band, which declined Ary its instruments
five miles, was brought at in a dray to make
music. Alderman Somebody something with a spade,
and declared the first tree “ and truly planted.” The
kiddies, with blistered ha’ put in the other 129 trees.
Labor ended, they were g /a roll and a saveloy apiece,
and were talked to at great .ength, and then they hoofed
it three miles back to town.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, June 19, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The big attraction of the Orpheus Society con-
cert was the first public appearance of Madame
Agnes Janson since her return. The special num-
f°r the contralto’s starring was
‘ The Crusaders’ Cantata,” in which the wicked
Armida has to woo, and temporarily win, the holy
Rinaldo from the strict and religious path, in a
series of sirenesque appeals of a mezzo-soprano
range. Madame Janson’s appearance didn’t
evoke very roof-splitting applause. Grateful
memories of her “Carmen” performances didn’t
influence the Orpheus congregation to wear out
its gloves in welcome. Yet the former star has
improved with time. She looks happier and
brighter, and her smile in singing moments is a
sweetly-inspiring thing. The voice is not as fine,
but the Janson art, with its dramatic taste and
fervor, made every number a treat.
Miss Joyce Brown was billed for the Orpheus
concert last week as the winner of the Austral
Scholarship, in Bendigo. On the charge-list she
was down for Papini’s “Danse Hongrois”—one
of the trickiest of fiddle show pieces. Joyce
turned out to be a small squat budget of early
childhood, with a rosy moon-face and a pair of
fatted calves. She swung her bow up valiantly,
and attacked the strings like any old platform
warrior. Joyce must have brandished the bow in
her lullaby and cot days instead of the usual coral
rattle. She was glib, sure and, on the whole,
correct; but her art is still in bib and socks, and
her gift of expression in the earlier teething stage.
Joyce is really too young and green to stage at a
grown-up concert, but as a bold wielder of fiddle
and bow, and an intrepid performer, she’s a glad
curio.
The concert head is a sadly neglected affair.
During a dull show, when someone is horribly
wailing “Out on the Rocks,” or some perjurous
pianist is strumming a Schubert study as if a
dozen harpies were sooling her on to do her worst,
the concert head comes in for review. And it’s a
sad prospect. In some cases it looks leaked and
weeded in a rubbish pile. In other cases the thin
wisps have ghastly partings where the upholster-
ing patches of horsehair show gruesomely. Two
hairpins hold together a reckless topknot, or a
few dozen skewers and tortoise-shell clasps stab and
clutch the coiffure in ludicrous profusion. People
who devote their souls to music seem to forget to
worry about their back hair; and the woman who
neglects her back hair'cannot compensate for the
artistic outrage by any amount of soulfulness in
her eyes.
New frivolities are filling these violet days.
The whole of Toorak turns out to try a frozen
waltz at the Glaciarium. Miss Byron Moore is so
far the nearest approach to the fetching skateress
of the Christmas-card type. She is sunny-haired,
cream-complexioned, snug of figure, and chic.
Also she loses her prunes and prism look of pro-
priety on the ice. Most others lose their balance,
and a few their urbanity. A repetition of im-
promptu falls has an irritating effect. One thing
about these frozen frolics : the adopted com-
plexion can’t withstand the early chill and sub-
sequent steam. I saw one charmingly-applied
color-scheme (where they had been much holding
of art up to the mirror) melt out of the face-scape.
It was pathetic. First, the siren glow—the rose-
bloom—deepened, then it spread, and finally it
trickled in rainbow streaks off the countenance.
A fast color is needed—something guaranteed to
withstand the chill and steam of the iced whiz.
One’s complexion really needs to be chained on at
the Glaciarium.
The Talbottle Exes., with the curly aide,
looked upon the skating multitude last Tuesday,
and were duly impressed at the expert with the
intemperate name doing the swallow-on-the-wing
turn on the ice, and the Holy City Miss (a young
relative of electrician Reid) waltzing like a joyous
skiff in a big sea. She gets that peculiar pitch on
as she whirrs. It makes the sensitive eye quite
sea-drunk.
The Homoeopathic Hospital hop proved a dull,
drab affair. The local Worships and the bulk of
the municipal Fathers foregathered, and supplied
an official Lancers, but they failed to give a fillip
to things. Some fledglings—a quartet of them—
fluttered into a special square, and clung to their
bouquets fearsomely. Miss Winifred Fraser,
after Little Marying and breaking up a whole
stage picture by the mere mention of “stomach,”
rushed into the polka scene, and had her pro-
gramme all marked out for her immediate whirl-
ing. Miss Win. wore milky-white chiffons, with
pretty blue-and-pink furbishings. This spruce
little comedienne is the one extensive social
flutterer of the Flemming-Brough co.
The Alliance Francaise wakes from its long
sleep, and promises a gay hop. Th e date is fixed
for July 12, and the cards are already in brisk
circulation.
Idle fashionables and the frou-frou sex generally
are trooping to the Tea and Song matinees at
Menzies and the Winter Gardens neighboring the
Chicago bun resort. Miss Topsy Conn, who isn’t
at all as giddy as she sounds, arranges the musical
bill-of-fare at the Subterranean Gardens, and
supplies some honeyed melodies herself. You’d
never dream from tbe serious and soulful strains
that a Topsy was singing. Topsy as a girl-label
is in this case misleading.
The craze for rag-time and coon wiggle-waggle
ditties is getting stronger. They creep into quite
solemn programmes, and no tea seems to be
quaffed (is a temperance brew quaffed ?) without
their aid.
The Austral Salon is not dead but forgotten of
late. An announcement to the effect that the
Talbottle Dame will shortly descend upon tbe
Salon fora blessing or a bun serves to remind tbe
public that the Salon still survives.
Te Rangi Pai (Mrs. Howie) comes along here
on a musical visit for rainy July. Probably it’s
another deep design of Commissioner T. E. Donne
to keep up the top C. of the Maoriland Hymn of
G-lory. The tourist hustler is night, noon and
morning searching round for new schemes to
advertise and glorify the hot-spring Paradise to
the world at large. Before quitting this Bent
city Mr. Donne made a special promise to a host
of intending exhibitioners and down-south gad-
ders that the Isles of the Blest would not go off,
blow up, fizzle, or pass out of the map without a
month s warning. There seems to be a Govern-
ment guarantee that tourists will always receive
due notice to quit when an eruption is imminent.
Mr. Donne has a yarn that sizes up simply the
divorce faith of the Yank. One of the Irresistibles
ot JNew York suddenly announced to her friends
she was about to be married. Somebody en-
quired if the stranger were a good sort of fellow.
“Guess he’ll have to be,” replied the Juno, “or
ill get a divorce so quick I reckon he’ll feel
giddy. He d have to be ! She didn’t know
what lie was, but pretty sure guessed what he’d
have to be. Docile, you bet.
Indian “chudders” are the latest fad. Chudder
isn t a cheese or a cocktail or a cakewalk. It’s a
shawl intended to keep out the theatre draughts
and Jook becoming. The other night Miss Eileen
Whiting covered her plump shoulders with a
chudder—a flaming red chudder, too. It serpen-
tined round neck and arms very like a tongue of
hre.
Ihe fierce light that beats upon a honeymoon
couple in a stylish hotel blazed upon a particular
pair of doves last week. The fact was that their
coo was so loud and glaring in the corridors, at
the dinner-table, and in other public places that
even the discreetest waiters were moved to a grin.
One of the staidest of them fell from grace and
was caught in the act of a smile. He pleaded he
couldn’t help it—that he wasn’t well and a little
upset him. The lift-boy, for an open guffaw, was
expelled instanter. Why not expel the doves ?
knot-tying of Mr. Charles Lempriere and
Miss Dora Mitchell, sister of Melba, is booked for
damp and cold-nosed July. Dora is the youngest
chick of David s clan, and is likely to start away
in double harness with a substantial gold bit in
her mouth. She’s a Titian-haired, slender Miss
with a rather shy air and none of sister Nellie’s
aplomb or dominance. Mrs. Harry Box—the
second blossom of the prosperous family tree—is
more of the bossy Melba pattern. The Lemprieres
are all smart and sporty.
The Wiedermann-Pinschofs, the other night,
celebrated their eldest girl’s coming of age. Every
guest now has that event marked indelibly on th 6
memory. In years ahead when the Miss in
question is still looking 21, there will be those in
the land who will proclaim her age as 27 and state
how they know she is this and not 17 or just Sweet
and Twenty. It’s not fair to a girl. This sex
should have no birthdays, or should have them at
such irregular intervals that they won’t convey any
information.
The Malcolm M'Eacharns are being publicly
blessed. Of the municipal gush there is no end.
The other day, there being nothing doing pro-
bably and the City Fathers feeling the need of a
biscuit and a speech, the thought of the
M‘Eaeharn occurred, prompting excuse for both.
So they gathered and spoke. Then some silver-
ware was unveiled and presented to the Maes;
and someone voiced the hope that it would be as
an heirloom in the great and noble familv.
Before the chrysanthemums of Goathlands were
put back into crimping-pins for the To Let
season, and the chairs restored to their pinnies,
the M'Eacharns invited some festive hundreds.
The small Mikado of Melbourne set Saturday
apart for this daylight wassail, and accomplished
one clever deal in getting the rival Arch-
bishops to the scene. It’s a long time
since the two competitive prelates took a
joint turn at a shivoo of the kind, but
the Mikado has a way with him and a munificence
that melts Christian rivalry out of the holiest
hearts. Guests were surprised to find Australian
cutlery and china ; somehow they expected to be
required to use chop-sticks. Another concession
to old times was the drum-banging of the Scottish
Regiment Band. The M’Eacharn clan started
for the Cold Country on Tuesday.
Pasteboards are out in the name of the Wil-
loughby and Ward Co. “to welcome Miss Grace
Palotta on her return to Melbourne. ” The Cream
are keenly interested, and are going to gather
thickly at the Princess’ on the 26tli. Grace is
such a Toorak pet that even a sewing-bee or
scone-class in her honor would draw the frilly
legions.
The State Parliament has a curtain-raising act
on Wednesday next (June 27th) with the usual
spree to follow. It is the Wrixons’ party, and
will be good, and will put the Federal House of
inhospitality to the blush. They don’t spree visi-
tors at the opening of the Federal House. It’s
political good sense, of course—there’s no earthly
reason why Parliament should be opened with
fizz and tea. But the society woman finds in
the fact another of the evils of Federation.
There was a Gordon anniversary bill on last
Thursday, when the Australian Literature Society
(a high-sounding label this for a very small potato)
endeavored to help the Victor Daley fund
by talk of another deceased bard. Mrs.
A. H. Macdonald did this particular talking.
Mr. E. J. Brady also bestowed some rele-
vant remarks on the listeners. The one fresh
feature of the Gordon talk was the introduction
of the bard’s political interests. Mrs. Macdonald
dug up the political past to the amazement of
some fervent Gordonites who had no notion that
the devil-may-care rhymer had suffered politicitis
at any time of his life. Yet it didn’t spoil his
Muse. Mrs. Macdonald failed to mention that.
There’s a streak of fiery genius in one strummer
of the ivories hereabouts. The youth bears the
apt name of Spry—H. E. Spry. And he is. He
makes music of a warm, ardent, knowing kind,
and in a Liszt Concerto at the Town Hall the
other night, dissipated the chill on the big sneezy
audience by his glowing performance.
Senator Keating’s brisk young wife is likely to
be the foremost galleryite this Federal session.
Already the astute young person has a first-class
and familiar knowledge of politics and the
people’s wants. She speaks with a kindling
eye of Acts to be passed, and work to be
done. She has clear-cut views of men and
measures, and speaks out with all the confidence
of the innocent, the young and the earnest. The
curly Senator should find such a wife a spur and
stimulus. And the curly member needs both.
It’s a bad thing to have the lowliest place at a
wedding-table, for according to a recent example,
the bad brand of wine travels down to the poor-
relation end of the board, and the unwary is
straightway poisoned. A ’groom in one such case
learnt that the girl’s dad, who was giving the
spree (according to a merciful tradition which
spares the ’groom in this matter), had ordered
two brands of wine—the good to circulate at the
upper and more important section, of the feasting-
board and the bad to fizz treacherously at the
other.
The satchel slung over the shoulder is the latest
adjunct to the cold-weather toilette. It’s a Yank
notion—sporty but reasonable. The small cash
and hanky and miscellany bag that up to this had
been carried in the hand and left in the tram, in
the cafe and other places of call, after the beauti-
fully lax and absent-minded manner of woman, was
a miserable source of trouble and loss. But it’s
not every She who can sportfully sling a satchel
over her shoulder and stand the gape of the vulgar
little boy or the grin of the man in the street.
One has to have a good amount of dash and
splendor to vindicate this fashion.
The infant aide raises a still small voice to
plead against his condemnation as a child. He’s
twenty-seven and his curls are a family matter.
He didn’t want them and would gladly shave
them off if he were allowed. He’s sorry. Tooralc
should be gracious and forgive him his curls. It
seems that there’s an irritating reproach of
innocence in curls. All the world’s villains are
lank-haired. There was never a curly monster in
any dark chapter of history. There are curly
rogues—males with the feminine gifts of prevarica-
tion and subterfuge and with the curly character.
But the true brute is straight-haired. He even
finds it difficult to go bald.
A wanderer in the Big Smoke has seen Carrie
Moore in “The Dairy Maids,” and expresses the
change in Carrie thusly—“ Such a treat is our old
friend romping through a star part in this frivolous
show. She’s overflowing with vitality and high
spirits. The jaded Londoner blinks on her in
wonderment. You couldn’t measure the joy of
such splendid vitality till you arrived among the
blase, lackadaisical folks astir in this
hub. Then you d throw up your hat as a tribute
to our Carrie. Besides, she’s plump and coy and
pretty these days. ”
The Stuart Balmains hied away last week by
the Sydney express for Uncle Sam’s Land. A
host of Toorak pals waved them off, and presented
the violet of farewell. How is it a violet remains
the best symbol of grief ? The forget-me-not is
too giddy for a funeral wreath. The violet does
as a long-drawn sigh, a melancholic tear, or a
Platonic kiss. A chrysanthemum expresses no
tender sentiment. Its slothful sagginess or its
severe crimp makes all emotion impossible. A
rose stands supremely for love. Adolphus tenders
it to Araminta. What about the cactus? A
bunch of cactus might suit the Woman-Leaguer,
or stand as the badge of the cat-enthusiast, the
dog-fancier, or the “ole clo’” dealeress. In
flower language the cactus must be the swear
word.
A creamy committee is toiling to make St.
Vincent’s Hospital hop a glaring success. The
medical fraternity has been roped in by the gross,
and elaborate fancy sets are being promoted to
catch the right crowd. Debutantes are promised
in swarms.
An Adelaide correspondent writes: —
Adelaide’s Mayor, Theodore Bruce, is something of an
inventor, and the alleged mayoral ball that eventuated in
the Holy City t’other day, proved it by taking the form
of a grandparents’ bread ai.d butter dance. Age limit, or
rather youth limit, of guests is said to have fluctuated
somewhere between 40 and 100, and the whole affair was
a correspondingly ancient and feeble spree. In
ex-Mayor Cohen’s hilarious time the annual municipal
carousal was an event at which 50 or 60 buds took the
opportunity of spreading their petals. Mayor Bruce,
however, apparently thinks the budding habit ought to
be suppressed, for he carefully omitted every suspected
bud from his invitation list. Buds, in the mean-
time, got in first by spreading their petals at the
“’Varsity” hop, a somewhat important function that
achieved itself some weeks ahead of the municipal depres-
sion. Mayor Cohen used to import a few Governor-
Generals and other potentates to add to the eflect of his
balls, but Mayor Bruce does not look so high. He was
humbly content with the State viee-regals and the Lord
and Lady Mayor of Melbourne for decoration purposes.
News by cable of the death of Miss Helen Lloyd was a
considerable shock to Adelaide. The girl’een was
the step-daughter of philanthropist C. H. Goode, and
set ofif only a few months ago on a jaunt round the globe
with her people. In London she was taken ill of periton-
itis, and died in a few days, aged only 21. Pathetic part
of it was that she was to have been married this month to
Bev. A. N. Marshall, a popular parson of the City of
Churches.
Yours Affectionately,
TRYPHENA.

plexions ; or, what is infinitely worse, cover a rough, coarse
skin with injurious paint and powder—a subterfuge
apparent to all but the most vulgar 1 In times gone-by
such foolish methods were excusable; but science has
made such strides in recent years that ladies troubled
with wrinkles, pimples, freckles or any facial blemish
need no longer worry. They have now a most reliable
and dainty cure in Patti’s Beauty Cream, which has added
three years of the highest Australasian reputation to a
quarter of a century of the most distinguished European
appreciation as the only perfect skin food and complexion
beautifier. It is found on the toilet tables of every lady
of refinement, because it is absolutely pure, harmless,
and a perfectly natural and never-failing remedy for all
complexion troubles, no matter how bad they may be. It
instantly clears the skin of all pimples, rash, &c., caused
by injurious concoctions and imitations against which
ladies are cautioned. All chemists and stores, price 3s. fid.

Tins is how a postal telegraph operator
announced the news of the San. Francisco catas-
trophe over the wires before he left his instru-
ment and ran into the street:—“Earthquake hit
us at dawn to-day. They are carting the dead
rrom the fallen buildings, fires are all over the
tovvn. no water, no brigade. I’m off, as this
building is shaking from top to bottom, and it’s
me for the simple life,”

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Bananaland Government is making
Sro vision on the next Estimates for a Tourist
,ureau. The winter traffic to the Cairns resorts
is increasing considerably. There is no more
attractive sea and landscape scenery in the world
than that along the coast of North Queensland.
The Tory paper’s epitaph as written lately
in a score of leading articles
George Heid has formulated what he calls
the “seven great wants of Australia, ’ but they
boil down to little more than a demand that we
should employ black men on our mail boats' and
blacklegs in our factories — Arthur Griffith at
South Botany.
TOUTING FOR RURAL MEMBERS.
Conn fanner who can spare time to attend Parliament
should stand as member for Barwon. He need not be an
orator or even a fluent speaker.— Arbus.
“ Not a scholard? No lamin'? Why, you'll he
another George Reid, I can see it in your face."
The United States, as the only effective
remedy for the Chicago meat horrors, proposes
State inspection and grading. That’s what the
Australian Commerce Act, just passed, intends
to enforce ; and the Commerce Act, according to
the daily press, will utterly ruin Australia’s
export trade.
The N.S.W. State Government pursues a
policy of masterly inactivity over filling the posi-
tion of State Statistician. The acting man is Hall,
and while he gives no excuse why he shouldn’t
be made permanent, Carruthers is not game to
give him the position lest Woolly Rutledge should
pour new kerosene on the Flames of Smithfield.
So the policy of let-it-slide is followed in the hope
that Hall may die, or grow old, or turn Buddhist.
J. 0. Watson will stand for the new South
Sydney seat. .His old Bland seat (really in the
fitness of things J. C. Watson should always have
represented Bland ; the adjective fits him better
than any other notable figure in politics) is
divided up between the constituencies now repre-
sented by Lyne, Tom Brown and Conroy.
The most banal idea in the matter
of the redistribution of Federal electorates was
the suggestion of Page, M.H.R., that the musical
name of Maranoa (Q.) should be changed to
“ Artesia,” because of the artesian bores there.
It is a wonder he didn’t suggest calling the elec-
torate in which Rockhampton stands “Tinned
Meatia ” for similar workaday reasons.
George Reid had been going strong the
other day about the alleged discouragement
of immigrants by the Australian Government
when he was floored by Kennedy, M.H.R., who
produced Reid’s own Act of 11 years ago, prohibit-
ing aliens—even desirable white immigrants—
from taking up State lands in N.S.W. Then the
House gave George the metallic laugh.
G, H. Reid shortly embarks on a tour of
Queensland. He should undo some of the mis-
chief that the Labor extremists have been re-
sponsible for and rally many votes to the Federal
Labor party. Last time he toured Queensland,
Reid delivered a staggering blow to anti-Sosli.
The Tories expected a great champion, and found
a man whom they reckoned had escaped from a
second-class music-hall.
It is expected that Homburg, son of
the Jenkins Attorney General who recently
made himself a judge, will win the Murray seat
in the S.A. Assembly vacated by the death of
Squatter Duncan. The candidate likely to be
nearest to him is the Labor nominee, George
Monks. There are 2000 German voters in the
district, and Homburg is of German parentage
and speaks the lager beer language, which counts
for much when appealing to a clannish Frankfurt
sausage constituency.
When Joe Cook and J. W. McOay talked
at CaStlemaine (Vic.), the statements hurled at
the pair Were so scathing and so uproarious that
Joe was moved to tell the audience that he had
never, in all his travels throughout the Common-
wealth, met with so an exhibition of
personal insult. It is something to have suc-
ceeded in insulting Joe Cook more than anyone
else in Australia, for nearly everybody has tried
his hand at it some time or other.
The Tasmanian Auditor-General has been
expressing his opinion about a small but inter-
esting point. On October 28, 1904, Lieutenant-
Governor and Chief Justice John Dodds gave up
the tiller, and Full-Blown Governor Strickland
took it. So careful were these two men that each
collected a full day’s pay for the 28th. The
Auditor-General reports that he has been legally
advised that Chief Justice Dodds had acted
wrongly in collecting the money, but that all his
efforts to recover the money from Dodds had
failed.
A recent Parliamentary report explains
how the Chow deserter from the Rand (S’Africa)
mines diverted himself during the period between
June 1, 1905, and February 28, 1906. In the
Transvaal town police area the roving Chow was
principal ip 87 cases of outrages upon whites, most
of them in connection with housebreaking. He
committed 20 outrages upon natives, and 204 of
him were arrested. In addition there were 29
cases of outrage outside the town police area, and
181 of the deserters were jugged. Not a bad nine
months’ record for what Bruce Smith calls the
‘ ‘ law-abiding colored workmen.”
James Gibb, the Foreign-trader who got
in on a split vote for Flinders (Vic.), scoring 4916
votes out of 13,604 polled, has announced at
Warragul that he won’t contest the seat again.
Gibb is a farmers’ representative, but he speaks
on no subject whatever ; he is the champion silent
man of the House. He sits behind Reid, but
doesn’t much approve of him. He doesn’t approve
of anything in particular, but the farmers like-
him because he is a good agriculturist. By
grievous mismanagement the Protectionist vote
was split among three at Flinders last election,:
L. L. Smith being the tail of the procession.
Sydney Evening News has been, this
long time, only an infrequent worshipper of the
battered fetish Freetrade, and whenever it did
pray it resembled some of the youths at suburban
Bethels—most of its time went in ogling pre-
sentable parties in the neighborhood. But in its
leader of June 16 it abandoned its aged jade,
Freetrade, and threw itself at the feet of Pro-
tection. Admitting the sound and rapid advance
of Germany’s shipping industry, it explains the
cause thus :
The secret of the whole business may, however, be
broadly expressed in the words “State help.” As we
know, 'individualism was and is often unable to develop
new industries when powerful and wealthy foreign com-
petition is in the field.
Shade of Sam Bennett, who was wont to call
Freetrade “an eternal and indestructible prin-
ciple !” Shade of Alfred, who, though he set a
bad example in spelling, yet warned the junior
reporters to “ watch closely the policy of the
paper” !
The Victorian Political Labor League has
now bumped against the trouble it was certain to
strike sooner or later—that there may arise certain
circumstances and conditions that no hard and
fast rules will serve to meet. The Labor caucus
lias unanimously backed up Watson in his refusal
to break the promise given to certain Democratic
Protectionists not to oppose them at the next
elections, and has officially notified the League
that it absolutely refuses to break that promise at
its bidding. What will happen if the League
branches prove obstreperous, Heaven only knows.
The branches choose their candidates, and the
Central Executive—not the Parliamentary Labor
party—endorses them, so the existing Labor
members may be disqualified like so many
cricketers. But probably there are enough honest
men in the branches to ensure that Labor—as a
party—shall not be guilty of an unparalleled piece
of treachery, a political breach of faith that would
cover the party with infamy for years to come.
It rests with the branches now, for it is to the
discredit of a few of the Central Executive, who
want to gain Parliamentary distinction at any
cost, that this proposed infamy must be placed.
A FIGURE OF SPEECH.
MR. REID: “No matter what I promised, Deakin, I must offer a stout resist-
ance because I’m built that way.”
London Times, in reference to the working
of the German increases of tariff
Coincidently there has been an influx of workless
miners from Austria, and of crowds of starving Russian
refugees ; but so healthy is the state of the labor market
that, according to the reports of the public employment
bureaux throughout Germany, there were, last March,
only an average of 1009 applications for every 1000
vacancies. The mining, iron, clothing, building, and
farming industries are particularly mentioned as afford-
ing abundant employment.
The country that can show an average of less than
101 applicants for every 100 billets is a place where
lack of employment has been reduced to a mini-
mum.
Three Tory candidates and one Labor man
came forward for dead J. F. Levien’s seat of Bar-
won in Vic. Assembly. They were Gurr, once
famous as the baldest man in Parliament, and an
inept Minister of Education when Peacock was
an inept Premier ; Levien, a son of the crafty old
politician; and Farrar, who' stood down to let
Levien, senior, win the seat at the last election.
The Labor man was Trades Hall Sutch. Premier
Bent went to Geelong to arbitrate, and
Farrar was chosen. Local feeling was mostly
in favor of Levien, chiefly out of regard for the
old man who pulled so many hidden wires to
oblige his constituents. Levien swallowed his
mortification, but his barrackers used language
about Bent. The election, of course, resulted in
the return of Farrar, who is a nonentity and
would eat grass out of the hand.
An exhibition, organised by London
Daily News, was opened on May 2 in Queen’s
Hall, London. It was unique in its way, being
an exhibition of the sweated industries of Free-
trade England. There were 22 stalls, each de-
voted to an industry and occupied by two
workers, and each article, when completed, was
ticketed with the price paid to the worker and
the time occupied in making. Very few panned
out 2d an hour, and from that deductions had to
be made for material. Among the worst of the
“sweated” occupations were the making of
“ sassiety ladies ” fal-lals and the folding of
Bibles and prayer-books. Into the saddening
spectacle an element of humor was introduced by
the exhibition being opened by a royal princess,
with a large following of upper-crust women and
rich clerical nobs.
Sydney has had an experience similar to
that of Ballarat (24/5/’O6), in regard to the unre-
liability of Italian marble. The lamp pedestals
in front of the G.P.O. are cracking and crumbling
on a large scale. If the Australian marble
couldn’t do better than that, Australians wouldn’t
take the trouble to quarry it.
Premier Price (of S.A.) and his colleagues
are said to be resolved to ask for a dissolution of
the Assembly this session if the Council refuses
to pass the Bill for the extension of its own fran-
chise. It is hard to see, however, what they will
gain by it. They can certainly force a section of
the Councillors to go to their constituents also,
but it is hard to see what they will gain by that
either. The Assembly electors (there are 188,000
of them) will send Price and his party back as
strong as ever. The Council electors (52,884 pro-
perty-holders), being asked if they want to share
their privileges with others, will answer “No”
by sending back the same old Councillors. Then
each House will say that its constituents endorse
its action, and things will be where they were.
The remedy then will be in a Bill to abolish the
Upper House and a stoppage of supplies—a
national strike—if the Council doesn’t pass it. A
refusal to vote supplies, which would mean the
temporary stoppage of railways and the disband-
ment of the police and civil service, would make
the propertied classes climb down within a week.
The refusal of members of the Australian
House of Reps, to listen to those long, dreary
speeches about nothing in particular which are
called the “debate on the Address-in-Reply ” is
one of the most cheerful of recent happenings.
It indicates a dislike to mere words that promises
well for work. And the velocity with which the
work of distributing the States into new elec-
torates was rushed through keeps up the promise.
A frequent lie of the Sydney Freetrade press was
that the Deakin Government would evade the
redistribution in order to do another outrage on
Ma State by depriving it of a member. When
the re-distribution was made the first business of
the session, the only thing that could be said was
that Deakin had had the worst intentions, but
public opinion had driven him to do the right
thing, or that he was “dictated to by the Labor
party.”

It is stated that the Labor caucus in S.A.
wants Representatives Batchelor, Boynton and
Hutchinson to stand for the Senate against Baker,
Playford and Symon in December next. They
would then run weaker men for the present Labor
seats with good hope of success. All the same,
the suggestion is pretty rough on the sitting
members (Batchelor, Poynton and Hutchison
aforesaid), who would probably be returned uno P'
posed for their old districts, while they would
have to put up a great and gory fight at the Senate
election.
Premier Seddon, of Maoriland, was a
stupendous toiler in his life time, and when he
died the Australian House of Reps., with a huge
lot of work ahead of it, could think of no better
way of honoring his memory than by wasting a
day and loafing. A logical Premier would have
said : “ Mr. Speaker, out of regard for the great
dead we will do as he would have done had he
been alive—have an extra-long sitting and try to
do two days’ work in one. All his life he was
trying to do two days’ work in one, and he would
think it a poor compliment if anyone loafed to
show respect for his memory. He was the sort of
man who would like people to break stones in his
memory, or to pound hot iron with sledge-
hammer for the repose of his soul. Mr. Speaker,
out of regard for the statesman who has passed
away, I beg to move that this House works like
Sheol with the lid on.”

To a Sydney City Council request that it
should be given control of the city theatres—at
present lack of effective control causes serious
danger to the public—Premier Carruthers re-
torted, with a characteristic ha-ha, or hee-haw,
and suggested that if the City Council wanted
new responsibilities it could take over the Pyr-
mont bridge. Of course the Council should have
the bridge as well as the theatres ; but it’s more
important that it should control the places of
amusement. Carruthers’ sneerful offer of a con-
trol which would cost a good deal, firing in no
revenue, and effect practically no reform, is a poor
sort of answer.
A CHEAP AND NASTY NOTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Arguing in favor of the free importation of foreign rot,
Senator GoUld said “they had no right to increase the
cost of living,"— Debate, on Address-in-Reply, Australian
