Fetch along the potted poodle
Canned as sheep!
Let Us eat the whole caboodle
At a sweep!
We should not be over hasty
In demanding what is tasty,
Everything goes in the pasty
If it’s cheap!
Eat the offal from the Yankee
Rubbish-heap!
Drink imported doctored swankey—
Drink it deep !
Wear imported tweeds —all cotton !
As to whether it is rotten
That’s a fact to he forgotten
If it’s cheap!
That’s the drie arid drily question—
IS it cheap ?
Never ask, as a suggestion,
Will it keep V
To achieve an estimation
For a doubtful importation
That’s the one consideration—
.ls it cheap ?
As to quality, you’d better
Keep asleep!
That’s the notion, to the letter !
Pretty steep !
That’s the side that perfect joy’s on,
Feed your little girls and boys on
Any kind of potted poison—
If it’s cheap.
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
HICHAED SEDDON,
aged 61.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Whispered that at least some of the ex-Players
are getting scared, and inclined to regard the Melbourne
Cricket Club as a sinking ship. Members of the N.S.W.
Association are said to have been approached with the
question whether abandonment of the Melbourne Club
will lead to the lifting of the disqualification. The loyalty
of the ex-Players to the Melbourne Club has not been in-
creased by the evident readiness of the Club to throw
them to the wolves when it suits its purpose to do so.
Also, the M.C.O. makes the ex-Players understand very
plainly that they are mere mercenaries, hired to do
certain work—and not allies. So they have no part in the
negotiations.
« « • *
After all, the Sydney Cricket Ground Trustees
proved a very tractable crowd of animals when Brother
Trickett took off his coat and started in to break things.
The fact was, that up to the date of the row with the
M. and the ex-Players, the Trust had been run by a
very small clique, and straight-going sportsmen like “ Old
Bill Lyne,” busy with what are, after all, more important
matters, had taken no part in the concern. The result
was disastrous. Consider the unholy mess things have
got into at the Cricket Ground. No wonder the Trust is
fighting tooth and nail to get out an English team. It
has hardly anything on view to encourage people to
become members of the ground. What is the
use of a Cricket Ground ticket at the present
time? The big Rugby matches are played at the
Sports Ground ; cycling is likely to collapse through
internecine warfare, and at least half of it will be carried
on at the Sports Ground next season for a certainty.
And now even the lawn tennis club is leaving the place,
so all the ticket-holders for next season are likely to get
is one first-grade cricket match every week, and a couple
of inter-State fixtures thrown in. The Trust may have
kept the ground well, but it has certainly done nothing
else, and the place that ought to be the site of the most
important sporting event of the week is a desolation, in
which a few score of people watch the Y.M.O.A. team
being licked by, a crowd of boys who haven’t the stamina
to play Rugby’foo'tball.
The cricketing position, as The Bulletin goes
to press, is very hopeful. The Melbourne Club has offered
to release the ex-players if the N.S.W. Association, will
join in with the club and other associations to bring a
team from England, and fight out the matter of a
Board of Control later on. And the N.S.W. Associa-
tion’s reply is a freezing remark that the Mel-
bourne Club is a Victorian institution, and the
N. Association can only treat with Victorian
cricketers through the Victorian Association. Of course,
the fact is that the M.C.O. is on its knees. Its
schemes have collapsed, and now, supposing it could get
the Marylebone Club drunk and a team started for
Australia, it could not be sure of getting even the Mel-
bourne Cricket Ground, of which it has quite lost con-
trol ; while it knows that it has no hope of Sydney
Ground, in spite of the well-known sympathy of the late
managing clique. As a matter of fact, the M.O.C. and
the Sydney Ground Trustees are about the only people
who want an English XI out here this season. The
Melbourne crowd wants it so as to. demonstrate
that it was only its overwhelming desire to get an
XI out that drove it to such contemptible tactics.
But for the accidental discovery of the fact that the club
had secured the services of the ex-Players, that part of
the business would have been kept a dark secret
till the XL was actually on its way, and then in
the interests of international courtesy the N.S.W.
Association would probably have had to back down.
Too many English XL’s are bad for cricket ; , they
serveto foster the spirit of professionalism,andoneinevery
five years is quite enough. Just at present, Vic. cricket is
not in a sufficiently flourishing state to make an
English XI. desirable; arid ihere in N.S.W. we have
to find another XI., for, no matter what happens, Noble
andco. will not be played. So, under the circumstances,
the Australian Board of Control should just sit Light and
decline to recognise the Melbourne Club as being alive at
all. If it doesn’t belong to the Victorian Association it is
dead, and no decent Board of Control has any right to be
fooling about with corpses. If it does belong to the Vic-
torian body, then the Association’s Executive Committee
will speak for it. In the meantime, no English XI. is
wanted here.
■*- * * •*
Eastern Suburbs and Glebe are at the top of the
Sydney Metropolitan Rugby competition with 11 points
each, and South Sydney and Newtown are formidable,
with 10 to their respective credit. Sydney and Univer-
sity are bracketed on the nine point mark, and after that
the list becomes uninteresting till Manly is reached, once
more at the bottom of the class. On Saturday the good
manners of the previous week had disappeared, and oppo-
nents addressed one another as “cows.” The lialmain
men played up splendidly against the first-class team that
Eastern Suburbs put into the field, and the first spell was
all theirs. Eastern Suburbs had to force frequently to
save the situation, and the first half ended with Balmain
leading by three to nil. In the second spell E.S. made
great efforts, but was repeatedly blocked. The Bal-
mainers punted beautifully, and followed fast. But the
inevitable opening came along, and like a flash E.S.
scored a try, which Messenger Eastern
Suburbs doesn’t miss opportunities easily. After that
Balmainers had to force, but came again, full of grit, and
raged on the Suburbs line. However, they failed to break
through, and were beaten by-two points in a game in
which most of the horiors were with the losers.
• '" *
Altogether it was a day of interesting matches.
University strove hard to get in .its passing charges
against Newtown, but the Newtown men came round the
scrum so fast that in spite of the strong wind behind,
them, the Uni. backs could do nothing, and were beaten
by nine to nil. St. George forwards put up a wonderful
game against North Sydney, whose back division played
equally well. On the other hand, St. George back division
might as well have been away playing skittles for all the
good it was, and North Sydney forward division seemed
to be present only to be trodden on. Under these circum-
stances, North Sydney was lucky to be beaten by only six
to nil. The surprise o'f the day was the stubborn' stand
made by the despised Manly team against South Sydney,
last year’s' premiers. Over and over again in the first
spell the Villagers had South Sydney struggling on the
line, and the premiers’ famous dribbling rushes were
stopped in great style. In the scrum Manly climbed all
over the premiers, but it was the backs of the South who
broke through the Villagers at the slightest opportunity,
and scored six to nil in the first spell. In the second half
Manly showed it is developing a back machine of its own,
and there were some pretty dribbling rushes, followed by
passing charges that were worth watching. One of them
went right through the premiers and scored. The gam*
eventually ended in a victory for South Sydney by 14 to
three, but the S.S. team had all the luck, and it looks as
though, in another six weeks’ time, things might easily be
all the other way. The Villagers are improving wonder-
fully.
The Glebe-Western Suburbs was a fearsome
struggle. Nobody thought W.S. had a hope, and against
the heavy, formidable Glebe men they were expected to
curl up like bacon in the pan. However, they declined
to curl at all, and pulled down the red forwards who
came rushing on to their line with a grim persistency.
It was a great proceeding. Fast as a Glebe man got his
feet against the leather a Western man sprang in, and
frequently both teams rolled together on the swampy
ground. Then right out of a most muddy scrimmage
somebody passed, and there was a scramble, a roar, and
a wild rush carried the ball right down to the Glebe 25,
where the red team piled its component parts on top of
it, and the mass writhed and swayed till a Glebe
forward kicked the ball out of touch. A Glebe
man secured it and streaked for the Western line,
and just as he reached it was dragged down. As he was
swung off his feet he tried to pass, but the Western
three-quarter sprang in, and intercepting, went past
the whole red team till the full-back scragged him. He
passed beautifully, but the taker was overtaken, and
thrown out of touch. More wild scrambling, and a hard
kick, which Glebe marked and scored from. This gave
the butcher-men fresh energy, and they whooped into
the Western 25, where they met as stern a defence as one
could wish to see. Man after man was pulled down by
the lighter team, and the line was repeatedly cleared
only to be swarmed on again and again, till at last the
Glebe got through, but failed to add the points. Then
Glebe having temporarily exhausted itself, Westerns
goc a rush on, and carried it clean over the
line. The shot at goal failed. The second spell, opened
with a series of charges, by both sides, all of which were
stopped till the Glebes worked down the touch-line and
scored three more. Getting possession, they tried
the same game again, but the Westerns replied
with a passing charge, only to lose the ball.
Getting possession again they hurled themselves
on the Glebe line, and after a wild scramble, in
which the leather flew backw'arks and forwards across
the field, a Westerner dashed through and scored. The
game ended in a victory for Glebe by nine to eight. It
was a great match.
* * * «
The British Association competition at Sydney
is headed by Pyrmont with the considerable lead of four
competition points, the club’s score being 14. Balmain,
Rozelle and Glebe are in the second position with ten
each, and after that Ultimo, Granville, Sydney and South
Sydney have six, four, two and nil respectively. That
sign of the duck is an impressive figure. In the Aus-
tralian competition, Newtown and Redfern are abreast
with 14 competition points, Sydney is in second place
with 12, and Balmain has 10. After that the figures are
uninteresting. The Y.M.C.A. and Paddington are at the
bottom of the list with two points each. Last year and
the year before Y.M.O.A. had a high position. What has
gone wrong with the good young men ?. Early piety,
presumably.
* * * *
The odor arising from the different, football
grounds round Melbourne gets even more powerful than
the voice of the barracker. Last Saturday the undefeated
Carlton team met Essendon, previously beaten by
“South” and “Saints.” The public expected a good
game, but the betting market in the morning told a dif-
ferent tale—2 to 1 on Essendon, with a goal and
sometimes two thrown in. The “ same old ” played
a fast, strenuous game, but as for Carlton—its
play is best indicated by the result : four goals 15
behinds to Essendon’s 10 goals 14 behinds, which, as
Euclid used to remark when the Athenians played stiff to
the Spartans, is absurd. The incompetence, or worse, of
two Carlton players was so glaring as to fix everybody’s
attention. A subject of comment was Essendon’s play-
ing of Thurgood. ; That club’s committee signified in
1902 that it wbuld never select the champion
goal-kicker again, arid, after standing down a season,
he went West. Returning last year he applied to
play for Collingwood, but the League refused a permit.
During last week the Club’s president stated in the press
that T. was not wanted—yet on Saturday he played,
and showed up brilliantly ; while the fool crowd, forget-
ting how it hooted him but four years back, cheered him
uproariously. The Fitzroy v. South argument was a mere
tussle, without actual bloodshed,.’Roy ultimately winning
by 62 points to 40. Collingwood had been upside-
down during the week. During its last few matches it
had been noticed that some of the players declined to
pass the ball to certain others, while one brilliant
“marker” was'afflicted with painfully slippery fingers.
On the Thursday evening, at the training-room, recrimi-
nations resulted in several healthy scraps, and when the
team filed out to play Melbourne it was found that four
of the leading men had been left out—for the present, at
any rate. As ’Wood beat Melbourne by 90 points to 46, the
result seems to have justified the committee’s action. In
the Association game West Melbourne still holds top place,
with only a draw to'its discredit, while Williariistovyn,
North, Footscray and Richmond try to keep within
coo-ee. On Friday afternoon, the youngsters of the C.E.
Grammar School settled the Wesley College infants by 55
- points to 44, on the East Melbourne:ground.~ Remember-
ing last year’s scuffle, the authorities had carefully
separated, the sheep from the-goats, as it were;
but the two forces collided ..in Flinders-street,
on the way home, and a pleasing little scuffle ensued.
One infant lost two teeth—which was the more regret-
table as the child was going to a dance that night, j It
was a notable scrimmage. Seeing the trains stopped,
Proputty evidently thought Socialism had arrived, arid
telephoned for the police.
* * * *
There is a frightful howl from the “Australian”
footballers over the selection of the team that is to play
Queensland. Boiled down; it is a cheerful indictment,
and'the selection committee is charged with favoritism,
incompetence, arson, bigamy, and prevarication. If
N.S.W. is beaten on Saturday, something horrible will
certainly happen to that selection crowd. On the other
ha.nd,’if N.S.W. wins, something will also certainly
happen to it—it will get drunk with delight at having
. defeated its detractors. The Rugby enthusiast makes
fairly nasty remarks about his selectors, but he is a meek
and mild sort of animal compared to the Australian-rules
camel.
* * * *
“Corinthian ” : I don’t think there is any occa-
sion for Australia to dust its hair and howl over its share
of the honors in the Olympic games (as suggested in 8.,
14/6/’66). Australia sent four competitors where some of
the other nations mentioned sent four dozen, or more,
arid it scored a point or two after all. With a fair show
it would have done very much better. The winner of the
Marathon (26 miles) was in Greece training for two
months. G. Blake, the Australian, was there two weeks ;
he was not allowed the use of the Ophir’sdeck for train-
ing going over; was not permitted to train in
the Stadium when he got to Athens; was
afforded no privileges at all. Yet after doing his
preparatory runs on soft ground, he was out
of sight of all competitors 'at 16 miles. So
far ahead was he, in fact, that the Canadian who won
afterwards admitted that he had given, up all hope, and
was running only for second place, when Blake’s legs
cramped as a result of his defective training. The Aus-
tralian sprint runner, Barker, was deliberately outed in
his favorite distance, and the Australian swimmer, who
has since knocked holes in the Olympic records, hadn’t a
chance of a decent swim after striking' Athens. Blake’s
chance in the five-mile was destroyed by the patriotic
Greek who stole his running shoes.. This runner has
beaten the Olympic time easily. The sports vvere managed
about as smartly as those at a butcher’s picnic, and many
of the American points are due to the Yanks imperishable
powers of bluff, while the Australians lost points through
having no manager to bluff for them.
The Bulletin office is inundated with corres-
pondence telling of the awful mismanagement at Athens.
In many of the contests the conditions were not
published, and were only explained by judges to the
competitors before the contests began, and where there
were several judges they often started wrangling as to
how the contest should be decided. In the swimming
contests this wrangle started after the competitors were
in the water, which was icy cold, and some of the men had
to retire and seek medical aid. What the Australians
complained most about was the lack of training facilities.
They were not admitted to the Stadium till a week before
the Contests. All the trainers and the only bit of ground
suitable for training operations had been secured by a
Yankee millionaire for the exclusive use of the Amurikan
contingent, which is the true Amurikan idea of “ sport.’
Even then the effect of eating the Beef Trust’s sausages
seems to have been too deadly, for, considering their
numbers, the Yankees did not do so very well. The track
at the Stadium was supposed to be a cinder one, but if
the accounts received here are correct, it was like racing
over the top of an ash-pit. The result was that most of
the contests degenerated into mere tests of brute
strength, and the times were all unspeakably bad.
* * * *
An American girl impersonated Annette
Kellerman for a time at the Olympia Games at Athens,
the other day. However, a crowd of Australians came
upon her and detected the fraud, and the supposed
Annette made a disappearance.
* * * *
Saturday afternoon saw another Sydney betting
club raided—the States Sporting Club this time. But it
was only a moderate bag totalling 26, and the loot is
understood to have been under £5. Now, when is Pre-
mier Joe going to swoop down on the big bookmakers’
clubs ?—or hasn’t he the pluck to do it ? Consider the
money there would be in it the night the card is called
on Sydney Cup 1
* * * *
Lacrosse players around Sydney are now nicely
hardened up, and the stick work is in some cases well
nigh perfect, as far as appearances go. Mosman and
Marrickville met at Petersham. This was one of the
games in which the stick work was not elegant nor
finished ; and the final score was Mosman eight, Marrick-
ville one. Balmain had two teams in the field, one of
which defeated Burwood, after a hard struggle, by nine
to seven, while the other was donkey-licked by North
Sydney—eight to one.
Naturally the game of the day was the tussle
between Balmain A team and Burwood, and the Balmain
captain, securing from the face-off, fed his homes, but
they failed to turn the pass to any account. The pre-
sence of one named Newman was a tower of strength to
the Balmainers. He was as full of tricks as a Chicago
meat operator, and played in a style that was
really brilliant. He drew the first blood, and
the other, side responding gamely, the first quar-
ter ended 'two all. The second quarter both sides
settled down to play the game as it ought to
be played, and then came one of the finest displays of
stick-work ever seen in Sydney. The equality of the
teams was amazing, and they scored goal and goal about
regularly. Second quarter ended four all. The third
quarter found the Balmainers playing much the better
game. The Burwood men seemed to suddenly tire or sro
off or something, and the Balmainers scored three goals
by three successive rushes. After that, the luck varied
for a while, and the game ended in a victory for Balmain
by nine to seven.
• « • *
Last week Edward'Tipper and Charles Connors,
the conductors of Melbourne’s chief two-up school, were
each fined £2O, with £5 ss. costs. This is the school held
in a big two-storeyed building in Market-lane, between
Bourke and Little Bourke. The constable who proved
the case slipped past the guardians of the Cumberland
Athletic Club, as the precious school is called, over a
score of times in four months. The fines were inflicted
on Thursday, but on Saturday evening the school was
running full swing as usual. One small fine in four
months won’t break a prosperous two-up academy.
• * * «
N.S. Wales bowlers, having wrestled with the
Liquor Act, have struck further trouble. Investigation
has proved the suspicion that some of the bowlers have
been on the straight instead of on the bias, which means
in bowls the opposite to what the expression would
mean in other walks of life. Every bowl played with is
supposed to be weighted on one side, and in consequence
to roll it straight means that the bowl will curve out
of the direct line. It is this “bias” which makes
bowls a game, and to play with “straight’’balls would
, reduce the pastime to the level of marbles. Yet in their
j lust after trophies some of the bowling cult have des-
i cothled to subterfuge and deceit. The mischief is that
j ho adequate system of easily testing the. bowls has been
\ introduced, and in their wrath the bowling community—
i mostly fat and anti-Socialistio—threatens to turn for
refuge to the security of a Government stamp on its im-
plements of warfare.
r * * » *
The entry for the veterans’ sculling match on
the Parramatta River has been cut down to nine—the
other applicants being refused as not sufficiently qualified
for such an event. But the accepted are real “ old ’uns.”
: Beach, Kemp, M‘Lean, Clifford, Green, Laycock, Rush,
Pearce, and Reynolds, all champions in their time.
* » * *
Larry Corbett’s story in N.S.W. Bankruptcy
Court is no encouragement for the young man to go to
the bike for a living. His professional career lasted
JO years and his total winnings were under £4OO. And
Corbett as a rider is reckoned superior to the average.
The one bright spot in his cycling career w'as when he got
£6 a week for fancy riding in a vaudeville entertainment.
If an ambitious youth turns to the bicycle for a living let
him learn to ride the implement wrong way up.
The interesting item in Sydney hurling, on
Saturday, was the City-Redfern match. In the first
round City defeated Redfern, and it was very confident
that it would repeat the licking. Having won the toss
and decided to play downhill, it seemed likely that it
would pile up the points so fast while it was fresh as to
get an invincible lead. But from the puck-off Redfern
secured, and carried the ball up the incline with a rush,
and scored promptly. Thunders of applause. Redfern
had splendid combination, and by a series of deliberate,
well-sustained charges scored again and again, till at
half-tame it was leading by 13 to 2. With the change of
ends City played frantically, but those systenused
charges by the Redferners—who seemed to be capable of
making the ball go where they liked, and kept it dancing
along their front—were irresistible, and they piled up
points till the whistle blew and left them winners by -3
to three. ,
• • * '
The Bulletin is beginning to have doubts
about the docility of the motor-car. It seems a fractious
brute that is liable to play the buckjumper or take the
bit between its teeth and fly off the handle, or do other
weird things, without the slightest provocation. Writer
went out in a motor-car last Sunday, and only the fact
that he had not been over the road before prevented hin-
travelling at somewhere about 40 miles an hour. As it
was, he was blowing along at somewhere about 15, which
is about the same rate as a Sydney electric tram. There
was a curve in the road. The driver turned the steering
wheel—or rather he tried to. It stuck, and next
•moment’ the machine had left the road, plunged
down the bank, banged into the fence, and ejected
everybody as the whale ejected Jonah. The part)
picked each other up, and then went back
to the beastly machine which was (standing on its head,
its engine snorting at the task of working in that
unnatural position, and the wheels racing for all they
were worth. Driver shouted “Whoa!” and “Heave
to!” “Belay there,” and “Avast!” But the wheels
whirred on, and then the boss, who had only bought the
machine two days before, swore fiercely, and turned off
the supply of petrol, which immediately oozed through
somewhere and caught fire. Then there was more
language, and the boss beat out the flames with his top
coat, and eventually the machine was got right side up,
pulled round by brute force, its nose pointed for home,
and the engine started. It went home quite quietly, and
the wheel worked beautifully. There was nothing wrong
with the machine when it refused to come round. It was
just a strike, with no time for arbitration.
••■ * *
Athletic trainer Sammy Goodman, who died in
Auckland (M.L.) t’other day, was a top-notch walker in
his time. Over two miles, he had no equal in M.L., and
a long list of matches resulted mostly in successes for
him.
• » * •
Dan Creedon’s last fight has a pathetic little
story attached to it. Dan’s mother kept a pub., but
things went down of late, and she had to sell out at a
ridiculously low figure. For some time she had been hard-
up-r-very hard-up—and Dari’s heart smote him. He re-
solved that it was up to him to find enough money to set
the “ old woman ” up again, and Dan knows only oneway
of earning money. So the old fellow went into training
and got on that match with Bill Smith in the hope that
he might pull it off and fixup the “old woman.” Dan
was all out of condition, and it is awful work taking two
stone off when you’re close on 40 and feel like 50.
But the thought of the “ old woman ” kept him at it, and
Dan lived a pure, righteous, and sober life for
a couple of months. He went into the ring full
of the hope that, having won the worst fight—against
himself—and got rid of that two stone of flesh, fortune
would not desert him against Smith. He knew that one
lucky jolt of the old sort would win the fight and the
“old woman ” the purse. But Smith knew it too, and
his long, brown left never gave the “old woman” a
chance. When poor old Dan threw in the towel a whole
mountain of hope tumbled with it.
* * * *
Further particulars to hand concerning the big
Dunlop Warrnambool-Melbourne road race. reveal the
..fact that among the prizes are no less than seven bicycles
of various makes, a cheque for £3O, and a £5 ss. gold
medal from the Dunlop Co., three pther cheques for two
and three guineas, and a bronze >rind enamel medal for
every rider who gets over the 109 miles in 10J hours. The
Dunlop Rubber Co. will, on application, post a card of
conditions, which contains a wealth of information on the
subject, to any cyclist free of cost.

Kemarkable Testimony.
A Newcastle Lady’s Experience, Twice Given
up by the Doctors, and Twice Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills Prove Successful.
Mrs. Elizabeth Lightfoot, of Lambton Heights,
Newcastle, N.S.W., relates a story which is
often told by women ; of suffering for years from
habitual constipation, dreadful headaches, general
debility, and subsequently from a bad leg. How
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills restored her to
health, and healed the running sores when
doctors’ efforts failed, is best told in her letter
which bears her signature, attested to by a Justice
of the Peace. “For over four years,’’ she states,
“I was in bad health and suffered dreadfully
from constipation, severe headaches, and general

kinds of medicine, and
my doctor prescribed
for many months. He
could practically do
nothing for me, and

take an opening medi-
cine regularly. I tried
many pills and reme-
dies,” but I failed to

regained my strength and activity, and was free
from headaches and dizziness, and my system be-
came regular in every way. This all happened
about four years ago. Some time since, having
discontinued your pills altogether, I had a break-
ing out on my leg, which extended from the ankle
to the knee. I used various ointments, but finding
they had no deterring effect I consulted the
doctors, who treated me for some months, but
they failed to do me any good, and advised me
to lay up or go to the hospital for treatment.
It was only as a last resource that I thought of

to you, and you may use it in any way you wish.
—Mrs. Elizabeth Lightfoot.” Subscribed and
declared at Lambton, N.S.W., this third day of
June, one thousand nine hundred and five, before

independent section of the community.*

SYDNEY
The jubilee of Responsible Government
in New South Wales found four of the officials of
the Parliament of 1556 still alive. Since then one
of the quartette, James Hugh Palmer, has
passed over at 89 years of age. Palmer came
to Sydney in 1841, and joined the Parliamentary
staff in 1843, the year when the first election took
place. He learned shorthand from the original
Gurney, and was counted the father of steno-
graphy in Australia, jig, retired on a pension
of £321 Bs. 6d. after a service of 41 years. The
three remaining officials Are still in harness, two
in Sydney and one in Brisbane.
The cable brings news of the death in the
Cold Country of Dr. James Stewart, who pitched
his hospital tent on 'Black Hill Flat, Ballarat, in
1853, and reaped rich fees at a time when nuggets
were more plentiful than coins. He was a fortu-
nate mining investor, and was one of the heaviest
shareholders in the* famous New North Clunes j
mine when it was paying monthly dividends of
£-1 per share. It kept this up for several years,,
and after Dr. Stewart had shaken all but the gold !
dust of Australia from off his feet and re-pitched
his domestic tent in London, his income from this
mine alone is put down at £40,000 a year. He
was a, member of the first Ballarat municipal
council, but for more than 30 years he was an ab-
sentee who knew Australia only as the magnificent I
source of his wealth, : and the place that was too
foolish to make him pay an absentee-tax. I
Captain Crone died the other day at
Wairarapa (M.L.), aged 74. Many years ago he
commanded a schooner trading between Australia
and Maoriland, and brought over the first batch of
diggers to Otago. He was a nephew of the chief
Police Commissioner of Denmark, and a cousin to
Boer General Cronje. Luckily the latter relation-
ship escaped notice a few years ago at the time of
Maoriland’s wild-beast Jingo fury, else the old
man would probably have been thrown over-
board.
That weary cold-tea bird, Albert Bruntnell,
has at last found rest for his travelled feet. He
has been appointed general secretary of the New
South Wales Alliance. Albert, who was once of
the Salvation Army, and has all the ear-marks of
a Good Man, ran in the Carruthers interest a bad
second to tlie Owe’Sullivan at Belmore last elec-
tion —the one at which Holy Bunker (no —Brun-
ker) passed out, likewise W. N. Willis, Haynes,
Sleath, Dick Meagher and so many other cele-
brities.

Winter Trips to North Queensland
FOR the months of May, June, July, and August,
tourists or those seeking an equable climate and mag-
nificent scenery can be thoroughly recommended to
take the trip to Cairns, North Queensland. The scenery on
the way there and round about Cairns stands unrivalled. The
famous Cairns-Herberton Railway, a marvel of engineering
skill, runs from Cairns over the ranges past the celebrated
falls to Chiilagoe, with its wonderful copper mines and lime-
stone caves. The Cairns-Mulgrave Tramway Line also
takes you through some typical Northern scenery. Cairns
is an ideal resort for the invalid, for the tourist, and pleasure-
seeker. Australia has not any winter trip to compare with
it. The round trip from Melbourne to Cairns and back can
be done in 27 days, Sydney to Cairns and back in 20 days,
Brisbane to Cairns and back in 14 days.

Among the effects of the late Macdonald
Patterson, Queensland and Commonwealth
Parliamentarian, sold by auction the other day,
was a line specified as “45 gallons of whisky.”
Small wonder the quaint old man had many
callers. In his house the bottle could hardly
run dry.


LEMON WHISKY.
WHAT IS IT ?
subject of this article, for several important reasons, should be given preference over
I ordinary whisky. It is less intoxicating, yet more satisfying, and is therefore destined to fill
JL a great wane—a beverage suited to persons of temperate habits, yet not teetotallers—a
stimulant which strikes the happy medium between ardent spirits and the mawkish soft drink.
Lemon Whisky supplies this want ; it is at once a stimulant, a luxury, a food, and a medicine. A
happy combination of all the qualities which are essential to an ideal beverage.
The ingredients used in this preparation, although simple and familiar to everybody, possess most
important medicinal qualities. It is not a mysterious chemical compound ; it is simply a scientific
preparation of ripe lemons, pure whisky, and specially refined cane sugar. It is guaranteed to be
absolutely pure, containing no adulterants whatever.
We invite our readers to carefully note what medical authorities have to say about the ingredients
contained in this unique beverage ; the authorities quoted are the best known and the highest in the
world ; their opinions cannot be questioned.
LEMON JUICE.
Dr. Robert Farquliarson, late lecturer on Materia Medica at St. Mary’s Hospital Medical School,
etc., in his work, “ A Guide to Therapeutics,” p.p. 208-209, says:—
“ Lemon juice has some important therapeutical properties unexplained by any action it possesses
over the healthy organism.
“ There is no doubt that persons afflicted with chronic rheumatic pains may often derive benefit
from taking a tablespoonful of this agreeable remedy two or three times a day with their meals.
“ The occurrence of scurvy on board ship is now looked upon as a clear indication that the regu-
lar administration of lime or lemon juice has been neglected, and that the crew has been attacked by
a painful and dangerous disease, the absolute preventability of which experience has amply confirmed.”
The same authority points out “ that citric acid, from which most of the so-called lemon prepara-
tions are compounded, is in no degree antiscorbutic.”
Ic will be interesting to here mention that, by an Act of the British Parliament, every ship
sailing from a British port must provide at least loz. per day of lemon or lime juice for every member
of the crew.
In the “Companion to the British Pharmacopoeia,” 1899 edition, p.p. 400, we find: —
“Lemon Juice —medicinal properties—refrigerant; when diluted, a particularly useful bever-
age in the prevention and treatment of scurvy ; relieves thirst in febrile and inflammatory affec-
tions. In acute rheumatism, to 1 pint daily.”
Dr. Heinman, speaking on this subject said: —“You cannot take too much lemon juice, the
natural salts of which act as a solvent on uric acid in the blood, the cause of so many painful
maladies.”
WHISKY.
We will now say something concerning the whisky contained in this preparation.
Dr. Robert Farquharson, while deprecating its excessive use, states: —“A moderate quantity
of alcohol stimulates the mental faculties, and is often beneficial to weakly persons by giving tone
to the digestive organs and aiding the due assimilation of food. It is of great service to dwellers in
large towns, and others whose mode of life involves much mental strain.”
In the “Companion to the British Pharmacopoeia,” 1899 edition, we read:—“Alcohol.—
Medical Properties : Internally a powerful diffusable stimulant, especially cardiac; mildly anti-
pyretic, diuretic, diaphoretic ; used in states of acute disease characterised by excessive debility,
as in typhoid, acute pneumonia, and influenza, in insomnia of old people; as an aid to digestion,
more especially in the aged and feeble, and those exhausted by overwork.”
Dr. Robert Hutchinson, M.D., Edin., M.R.C.P., author of “The Principles of Food and
Dietetics,” says, pp. 324 : —“ Alcohol must be regarded as a digestive stimulant ” ; pp. 323 : “Diges-
tion is not merely a chemical process ; the movements of the stomach walls play a large part in it
also, and here the action of alcohol, in dietetic doses at least, is entirely favorable, for it has a won-
derful power of increasing the activity of the stomach peristalsis. Alcohol not merely increases the
Stomach movements ;it promotes very jjowerfully the secretion of gastric juice.” pp. 324 : “ Alcohol
in dietetic doses, 30 to 40 grammes (about two tablespoonfuls), taken before or during meals, actually
shortens the time required for the digestion of a meal by half an hour. The net influence, then, of
moderate doses of alcohol upon stomach digestion, even in health, is favorable rather than the
reverse, while in some conditions of disease its power of exciting the movements of the walls of the
stomach, and promoting the flow of gastric j uice renders it a valuable aid to the digestion of solid
food. The importance of these conclusions regarding the influence of alcohol upon digestion can
hardly be over-rated, for they explain much of the benefit often derived from the moderate use of
alcohol even in health, and still more in cases of disease.” „
SUGAR.
To complete our description of Lemon Whisky, which contains a considerable proportion of sugar,
it will now be necessary to inquire what are the effects of sugar on the human system. In doing so,
we cannot do better than again refer to Dr. Robert Hutchinson’s work, treating of the nutritive value
of sugar. On pp. 271 we find it stated that “refined sugar is to be regarded as a practically pure
carbohydrate; that being so, its food value must be high, for every gramme of it will yield 4T
calories of energy.”
“Itis as a muscle food that sugar is of special importance. We have already learnt (pp. 38) that
carbohydrates are probably the chief source of muscular energy, and sugars, on account of the ease
and rapidity of their absorption, are better calculated to perform this function than any other
member of the carbohydrate group,” Dr. Hutchinson’s treatise on sugar is too lengthy to be fully
reproduced in the space at our command ; we will therefore condense some of his more important
statements, which are as follows i-
“To Swiss guides and Arctic explorers, the value of saccharine has long been familiar.”
“Sugar is given to blood horses in order for sustain them in the trials to which they are sub-
jected.”
“Sugar has a notable effect in lessening muscular fatigue.”
“Yaughn Harley found that if he took 17ioz. of sugar per diem, his power of doing work
was increased 61 to 76 per cent.” .
“Sugar is a specially valuable food for persons who have to perform a single muscular effort,
especially if they are obliged to do so in a state of exhaustion.”
“ Extensive experiments have been made in the German Army, and it was found that the men
who were given ten lumps of sugar per day in addition to ordinary rations, marched better and
suffered less from hunger and thirst than their fellows not so supplied.. As a result of the experi-
ments, the surgeon in charge recommended that the sugar ration be increased to 60 grammes per
day.”
“ Certain rowing clubs in Holland found sugar to be a valuable food in training. The rowers
who used it always won, on account of their superior powers of endurance, and it seemed to counteract
the bad effect of an exclusively meat diet, so that the men did not become ‘stale.’”
We do not think it necessary for our purpose to quote any further from the overwhelming mass
of evidence at our command to prove to the satisfaction of our readers the great value of the ingre- j
dients used in the manufacture of Lemon Whisky. We have quoted sufficiently to show that it is
entitled to first place in the category of alcoholic stimulants, and that it, is beyond doubt over-
whelmingly superior to all other liquors.
It pleases the palate, aids digestion, cleanses the blood, quickens the circulation, creates energy,
and strengthens the powers of endurance.
These are not our own bald, unsupported assertions; they are the teachings of our greatest
medical authorities, beyond whom there are none to refer to.
After a careful computation of the component parts, the quantity it would be advisable to take,
according to expressed medical opinions, would be 10 to 20oz. (half a pint to a pint) per day.
IT MAY BE TAKEN :
Plain, in a wine-glass, as yod would take a liqueur. Diluted with cold water to taste. As a
summer drink. A wineglassful mixed With cold soda-water. A wineglassful mixed with a large glas|
of cold water, with your meals. „ For a winter drink, mix with hot water. It is doubtful whether
there is any better cure for chills and colds than Lemon Whisky, if taken hot and strong on going to
bed. For a sore throat or a troublesome cough, a teaspoonful undiluted at frequent intervals will
give great relief. .

“ Algol ” : Was at an official dinner once
with the Education Department people. One
boss was dining so well that the bones on his
plate formed quite a mound. A subordinate
sitting next to him noticed this, and, leaning over
to him, gently said, “Put your bones on my
plate, sir.” One of the uses of a departmental
subordinate—to provide a plate for the boss’s
bones.”

“ F.” writes :
Apropos vandals (B. 7/6/’O6). Many years ago, the
solitary policeman in Fitzroy Gardens (Melb.) at daylight
found many of the statuettes injured, with arms and kgs
broken off, and eyes gouged out. The wrecks were traced
to two well-connected men, who had been out on a howl-
ing tear. One was associate to a Supreme Court Judge,
and son of a very high law functionary. The other lofty
drunk was a clerk in the County Court, and brother of a
former Minister of the O'own in Victoria. The Judge
violently sacked his associate, and his father, the high
legal functionary, bought the misguided youth a slop out-
fit, took him to Sandridge, placed him on board a ship
going to ’Frisco, put a £5-note in the captain’s hand to be
given to the staggered immigrant on arrival at the Golden
Gate, gave him his paternal curse to go on with, and left
him. The civil servant had influence enough to keep his
billet on payment of damages, and he became a very sober
Minister himself in later years.

The Advantages of Kutnow’s Powder.
SIR JAMES CRICHTON BROWNE said that a prolongation of
life is not desirable unless that life be vigorous and happy.
Many of the quick-killing diseases that involve but brief
disability and suffering, like typhus and typhoid fever, are decreasing,
while slowly degenerative diseases, involving protracted invalidism
and much misery, like Bright’s disease, diabetes, and cirrhosis of the
liver, are mounting up. Society has always had to pay for every
mistake it makes, except when the habit of hygienic correction is
cultivated. PROFESSOR LAWSON TAIT, M.D. recognised the
importance of removing the debris of undigested food the morning
after the banquet, and not only prescribed Kutnow’s Powder in his
hospital and private practice, but used it personally for years with
unqualified success. The secret of health is often found in the
elimination of uric acid, and this is easily achieved by the use of
Kutnow’s Powder. It cleanses the stomach, rouses the liver, flushes
the kidneys, and clears the bowels. By this simple means the errors
of excess in diet and drink are corrected, and the poisonous debris
removed from the system.

At last the Vic. Education Department
discards the 30-years-old “ second reader,” which
lingered in use for years after the other “Royal
Readers ” had dropped into the grave. The new
book will be printed and bound in Australia, and
one-fourth of its articles will deal with Australian
subjects. So much is the good spirit growing that
even the Universities propose to discuss in confer-
ence the question of the local supply of books,
and the possibility of establishing an Australian
University press. At present most text books in
use come from the Oxford University press, or
the Pitt press at Cambridge.
‘ ‘ Snell ” : The testimony of the man in
the game : “I play football, I have been a bit of
a pro in the bike-pushing business, and I’ve
fought for purses, and all I’ve got to say is that
these men who’re howling about the brutality of
boxing are mugs. They don’t know; that’s what’s
the'matter with them, but they’re not content to
be quiet about it. Riding bikes hurts a man worse
than boxing; so does football, especially if your
luck’s bad. It’s not. a matter of mere surface
damage—that’s nothing, it passes in a day ; it’s
getting all out that pulls on a man, and you’ll see
cyclists and footballers carried’off utterly done-up
any time you care to look for it. If these croakers
are for abolishing all sports that call out the
last bit of go a lad’s got in him-—all right. That’ll
obliterate long-distance running and every game
that’s worth a tin tag. But they only yelp against
boxing ’cause they haven’t the sense to see that it
comes to pretty well the same thing whether a
man’s finished by giving and taking blows, and
stopping or avoiding, or by pushing a bike, or I
running ten miles across country. In a boxing |
match more care is taken of the competitors than
in any other game, but a little blood goes a long
way in a scrap, and that’s what frightens the
softies. I’ve seen a lad come out of a fight look-
ing like a Red Indian, blood from head to heel,
and then clean up to a small cut on the eyebrow
that didn’t trouble him more than a mosquito
bite.”

MORE LETTERS FROM WOfIEN.
From Mrs. Elizabeth Sweet, 135 Gouger-street, Adelaide, S.A., 2nd December, 1904.
“ It affords me great pleasure to testify as to the great and lasting benefit I derived from taking
Warner’s Safe Cure, after suffering for many years from constitutional breakdown and nervous
prostration, brought on by mental worry and family troubles. I have been the mother of fourteen
children and had lost my husband, so was compelled to support my family by my own unaided efforts.
This seemed a burden greater than I could bear, but I struggled on until I became so weak and ill
that I could scarcely drag myself through my duties. Then my hands and feet began to swell, and I
could not walk without a stick to support me. Finally the time came when I had to take to mv bed.
A doctor was called in who gave but little hope of my recovery. A friend who came to see me ad vised
me to take a course of Warner’s Safe Cure. She seemed to have such great faith in the medicine that
I began to hope that it would benefit me, and therefore commenced to take it. In a few weeks 1
could hardly realise that I was the same woman. I was able to get up and walk about without the
aid of any support. My strength returned, and I felt that I had been granted a new lease of life.
After taking seven bottles of Warner’s Safe Cure I became assured that I was quite well, and once
more able to battle with life for my family who were dependent upon me.”
From Mrs. Claria E. Horne, Wentworth-street, Plattsburg, Newcastle, N.S.W., 16th March, 1905.
“ For a long time I suffered almost daily from nervousness, giddiness and headaches which were
almost unbearable, accompanied by great lassitude and mental depression, brought on, no doubt, by
the influence of the law of nature applying to women of middle age. Fortunately, I was recommended
to take Warner’s Safe Cure, and was astonished at the great relief I obtained. Continuing to take
the medicine I was soon free from any distressing ailments, and able to go about my work as well as
ever. I have recommended Warner’s Safe Cure to many women, who have taken it with the same
beneficial result.”
From Mrs. Annie Harrison, 54 Campbell-street, Balmain, Sydney, N.S. W., Bth March, 1905.
“ I have suffered as many other women have from the effects of rearing a family of children, com-
plicated by disorders of the liver, constipation, headaches and depression of spirits. Quite frequently,
whilst working about the house, I would have to sit down and rest until faintness passed. About a
year ago I suffered almost torture from pain in my back and head, scarcely knowing a moment’s peace.
A friend, knowing my condition, recommended a trial of Warner’s Safe Cure. The advice was
adopted, and, after taking a few bottles of the medicine, a most favorable change took place. Con-
tinuing the treatment I continuously improved in health, until now all my previous troubles have left
me and I am in good health.”
From Mrs. J. N. Thomas, 84 Oroya-street, Boulder City, W.A., 17th October, 1904.
“ Ten years ago I was taken ill and consulted a leading doctor in Adelaide. I described to him
my symptoms, an intense pain in my right side, accompanied by a swelling or feeling of tightness, the
Eain being so severe that I could not lie on that side nor get my proper rest and sleep, also that I had
egun to lose flesh rapidly. The doctor diagnosed the case as being one of hydatids or of tumor of the
liver, and said that in either case I should have to undergo an operation. He strongly advised me to
go to the hospital and be further examined. I went to the hospital, and the doctor there confirmed
what the other doctor had said in respect to an operation being the only means by which I could be
cured. I did not like the idea of an operation, and promised to think about it, and let them know my
decision. On leaving the hospital I met a friend and told her what the doctors had said. She advised
me not to have an operation performed, but to go home and take a course of Warner’s Safe Cure, as
she was sure it would cure me. She inspired me with such confidence that I resolved to take her
advice, and procured a few bottles of the medicine. I was, however, so weak when I returned home
that I had to go to bed, where I remained for a week, but during that time I took Warner’s Safe
Cure at regular intervals. In a short time I began to feel easier and better, and by the time that I had
taken six bottles of the medicine the pain had all gone. I could eat and sleep and enjoy my rest and
food, which I had not been able to do for many months previous. Since that time I have been in good
bodily health, and able to attend to my household duties.”

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Janor”: “Old Camp’s” salting episode (B.
16/3/’O6) brings to mind an old-time Victorian saline
transaction, the success of which also depended on the
correct forecast by thesalter of the sampler’s procedure.
The operator was a hatter. The mine consisted of a shaft
down 20ft. on a 2ft. reef, worth about 4dwt. to the ton.
Buyer was the local butcher —a man quite unscrupulous
as to the getting of mining information. Thus it hap-
pened that one evening, after working hours, the man of
meat climbed, as he thought, unobserved, the spur which
held the hatter’s show. But the prospector was delayed
later than usual, and, busy at a surface job, saw the
visitor. He hastily picked up two small calico bags used
for bringing prospects up the shaft, and placed therein,
equally divided, the golden contents (some few dwts.) of
“ Kitty.” The salted bags were thrown carelessly by the
shaft head, and the hatter secreted himself in a hollow
woolly butt. Up panted the butcher, glanced quickly
around, grabbed a pick and the readied-up bags, and dis-
appeared down the shaft. After live minutes’belting
with the pick he re-appeared with two samples—one in
each bag. These he carefully panned off and got a fine
prospect. Replacing everything as found, the sneak
sampler departed. Later, the miner called into the shop
for a bit of steak, and the butcher inquired casually how
the reef was looking, and the price of a half-share, The
hatter took £2oo—and the early coach. There was no
crushing broken from that vein, though, strange to say,
17 years afterwards a prospector, overtaken by a snow-
storm sheltered under the remains of a shed close by the
old shaft, and, idly prodding the ground with his pick,
discovered the cap of a parallel vein which crushed up to
7oz. per ton. Thus was the smiter smit.

Complaints are rife that Australian butchers
are going in largely for Yankee methods of weighing, the
chief point about the U.S. method being that it is just a
violent rebounding of the meat off the scale, and the
movement is too rapid for the average dull-eyed buyer.
But long before the Yankee scales were introduced there
were butchers whose rapidity made even a common pair
of scales deceptive. There was one in particular, in
George-street, Sydney, who stands in history as
an unblushing fake. He posed as a friend of the
poor, and on Saturday nights sold legs of mutton
much below current prices. One night it was “ 6Jlb.
legs for 25.” The legs were not more than
51b. each, but for a long time there was not one quick-
eyed enough to dispute. At last an old man said, “ See
here, I think there is a mistake. This is not 6£lb. Please
weigh it, and weigh it slow.” There was excitement in
the shop, but the meat man rose to the occasion. He
seized the leg as if to weigh it, and slung it in among the
other legs, and yelled to the attendant, “Give this man
back his money ; my weights and scales are stamped.”
He successfully dashed the meat into the scales for many
years after that, and died in the odor of sanctity, leaving
much cash.

“Mirumbeck”: The jewellery and watch-
tinkering trades are not what they were in the silver era,
before the time-payment and tanglefoot watch curses
came to the land. The eternal chase after cheapness has
much to answer for. Especially is it responsible for
the gold-filled and rolled-gold rubbish with which the
States are now swamped. The watch-maker, who spent
years at his profession, and took a pride in his work,
begins to find his occupation gone, for the nickel-cased
rubbish people wear nowadays isn’t repaired. When your
watch goes wrong you aim it at the dog and buy
another. There is a class of people, thank Providence,
who still believe in the real article, and, when gold gew-
gaws are out of the question, they take silver rather
than “Brummy,” so I expect silver to come in again
shortly. For 30s. a woman, with taste, can deck herself
out for a Government House “hop” in temporary
“ Brummy.” The average society butterfly is a puzzle
nowadays, for it is hard to tell—even the expert is often
astray—where the real jewellery begins and the paste ends.
Parisian paste gems, especially in the white grades, look
better than the real twinklers, in their light up-to-date
settings, as against the heavier, safer claw-clingings of
our grandma’s days. But the fashions change mystically.
Twenty veais ago, my benchwork consisted in melting
down the casings of cameo brooches and reducing the
despised long granny muff-chains to the natty fobs, of
one-tenth the length and one-quarter the weight. Yet,
to the dismay of Fashion’s vandals, the long muff-chain
is more worn than ever to-day, while cameos, though a
trifle smaller than of yore, are creeping into fashion

“Philo’ : Purchasing a pair of boots, recently,
was shown some marked “ Made in U.S. A.” I felt pretty
sure they were a local article. “I know something
about this line,” I said, “and this isn’t an American
boot.” I thought he’d look upset, but he didn’t. “No,”
he said, calmly. “ Then why is it marked ‘ Made in
U.S. A.’?” “Oh, that means the United States of Aus-
tralia.” I subsided.
o * * *
“ Starr ” : I have lately been going about among
some newly-married friends, old friends, neighbors, and
strangers, and have consequently eaten many meals at
their tables. The bridal lots had plenty of expensive
electro plate, but nary a scrap of solid silver. Neighbors
and others had shabby electro ; whilst the old-timers
could set out some fine old silver, which they had
brought out 40 and 50 years ago. I know many pioneer
women, and each possesses a good silver teapot, cruet, or
spoons—they are great on spoons—which came out in
the old sailing ships about the time of Columbus. Where
does all the Broken Hill silver get to that none is avail-
able for Australian use ? Electro costs almost as much
now as real silver —and it won’t stand the same
cleaning. Surelv we might be allowed to have one fac-
tory in Australia for the making of pure silverware,
after good models, instead of having to buy imported
rubbish of hideous patterns.
THE MORTGAGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I see my houses standing in a row along the street,
And my gardens growing plants, and flowers and trees,
I hear my song-birds singing in their cages clean and neat,
And I scent my perfumes floating on the breeze.
And even little children playing school beside the fence
I look upon with kind paternal mind,
For they’re all beneath my mortgage, and a few short
summers hence
They will bear the yoke their fathers leave behind.
I bought that piece of land for something like a thousand
pounds,
And let it lie until the city spread,
And when I found the people chafing in their narrow
bounds,
I offered them suburban homes instead.
I would sell the land for so much on long terms to suit
each taste,
And would build, to suit their fancies, any style—
Very simple the agreements that these people sign in
haste,
But they pan me out a handsome yearly pile.
Some are with me from the start, but many died and
passed away,
Other tenants taking over their arrears,
But not one of all the living or the dead could ever pay
Off the obligations growing with the years.
There’s that house up near the corner, where the blinds
are trimmed with beads,
I built that to please a tenant who is dead ;
Well, last week his son got married, and has signed the
same old deeds,
And is proud to take the mortgage on his head.
Putting everything together, I can say X love to see
My old people as I move about the street;
I love each beaded window-blind, and shrub, and flov.er,
and tree,
And the song-birds in the cages clean and neat.
And on a sunny morning, as I slowly pass along,
My heart is full of thankfulness and praise,
And when, as sometimes happens, joyous kids around me
throng,
I pat their heads and wish them length of days.
N.W.S.
OBY.

„ Do ”’ fc you 80 too far ih saying (6. 10/5/’O6) that Cook, a
ranker ' 'could never have risen higher than lieutenant
had he lived and stayed in the navy 50 years ” ? Benbow
in the same centiirj', Christopher Ming's in the preceding 1
onej who both crept in through the hawse-hole, rose to
be Admirals, and there are many other instances of men of
similar origin attaining higher rank than Cook held. Smol-
lett knew the service well, and assuredly his Commodore
iJIm 111011 never climbed into it by the cabin-window.
While men of “ birth ” always had the lion’s share of all
billets under Government, their exclusive monopoly
of the big prizes in the army and navy does not seem to
have begun before the early years of the last century.
Gobbett, the son of a peasant, became regimental ser-
geant-major of the 54th Foot before he was 21, and on
leaving at 24 was offered a commission if he would remain.
Writing from St. John's, New Brunswick, about ISOS, he
says (I quote from memory) that of 17 commissioned offi-
cers there, 12 had risen from the ranks. Apropos of Cook,
I saw many years ago in South Shields an ancient brig
which the old salts of the port swore was the identical
craft the navigator served his apprenticeship in. Being
reporter on the local paper, I was sent to hunt up the
facts, but the only evidence to be found in support of the
legend was that the story had been wandering loosely
around for 40 or 50 years, and had been passed down
from one old salt to another. The ancient ruin was still
in the coal trade—the screw-steamer had not then quite
killed off the old sailing collier—and if any of the tim.
bers that Boy Cook walked on were left in her they must
have been at least 130 years old.

A TWELVE TEARS’ SUFFERER CURED.
TNDIGESTION comprises many ailments, has many varied symptoms. Pain after food,
relieved slightly when wind is rifted; a feeling of sickness if awakened suddenly;
headache; a bad taste in the mouth; the recurrence of food; heartburn; sleeplessness—
all these, or any of them, maybe present. They all mean that the digestive
organs are out of order, and need a little help to get them equal to their
work. Bile Beans give that help and tone up the digestive organs so that
they can do their work properly.
“ About nineteen years ago,” says Mr. Henry Speer, a Blacksmith, of
Lynch-street, Young, N.S.W., “ I first felt the symptoms of Indigestion.
The pains I experienced were terrible, and would completely double me up.
I consulted doctors. They all agreed it was an aggravated form of Indiges-
tion, and one told me that I would not live four hours unless I got ease from
the pain. Another doctor advised operating, but this I would not agree to.
All the doctors could do for me was to administer morphia and
opium to deaden the pain. I was under the doctors’ hands for many
years, attending them regularly until my patience was exhausted.
About seven years ago, hearing that Bile Beans had cured many
cases of Indigestion, I decided to give them a fair trial. After
having taken them regularly for a month or six weeks. I noticed a
great improvement in my conditon. Thus encouraged, I persevered
with the Beans, and was effectively and thoroughly cured. It is
now five years since I had an attack of Indigestion, which I
think can be taken as a guarantee that the cure is permanent. Where life was once a
burden it is now a pleasure, and altogether I feel a different man. I have now been a
resident of Young for seven years, and am consequently well-known.”

Said that the English have given up the build-
ing of speed racing yachts. Britain is no longer the
richest nation on earth, and only' multi-millionaires can
stand the strain of building racing yachts. The very
excellence attained by the builders brought about the
ruin of the business. The racing yacht that is to lick
creation is quite unseaworthy, good only for racing in
sheltered waters, and uncomfortable and uninhabitable.
It was fotind that its extreme life was a little more than
two years, and British yachtsmen have given up the
struggle, and left the business to members of the
Chicago Beef Trust. Meanwhile, they have gone in
for the eminently safe and comfortable craft in which
a 1 j a . n . oa j cruise round the Mediterranean with his wife
and friends in good solid comfort and safety—the class of
boat that has a life of somewhere about 40 years. Some
?> , e i llos * ; comfortable and seaworthy craft of the
ltoyai Yacht Squadron, and the Royal Thames Yacht
ioor> a i Yacht Racing Association, date back
to 18J0 and thereabouts. In fact one, the Arrow, built by
Joseph Weld, which is still competing in ocean races,
" as built as far back as 3822 —but it is said that absolutely

noat Has been replaced several times over. The first

Jaime (built at Lambeth to the design of Charles 11., and
which the Merry Monarch also steered) and a Dutch boat,
for a wager of £lOO. The giddy king won with compara-
. tive ease. Ihe Dutch aft envardsgot level by bombarding

. ,A lth ?% rh m ost people seem to have forgotten
all about it, there is to be a race for the sculling cham-
pionship of the world next month. Stanbury is putting
in good, solid training on the Parramatta, while Towns is

championship, and row a lighter man than ho did in the
former race. This time Towns is not going to make the
mistake of under-rating his opponent.

. J-h® Auckland City team, which proposed to
wsit Sydney with a view to showing our Rugbyites how
to kick the inflated sphere, is understood to have a proper
appreciation of its own worth. It includes four of the
famous All Black team that raged through Britain, and
the fact is understood to have prompted the Auck-
landers to make cash demands which have taken away the
breath of the N.S.W. governing body. If the terms can
be arranged the team will play here on July 14,18, and
, . 13 not known how much the Aucklanders are
asking, but the Cornstalk governing body seems
pretty determined that unless the request is reduced very
considerably there will be no tour. Even in their ain
countree the Aucklanders are regarded as grab-alls.
* * * *
~ ■^ l ® recent progress of the All Black team
through Britain has brought about a great burst of
football literature, and the magazines have been making
special mention of William Webb Ellis, the Rugby bov
who broke through the then rules of the game and ran
towards the opposite goal with the ball in his hands.
In those days (it was 1823), when one caught the ball on
the fly he was allowed to run backwards as far as he liked
with a view to taking a good kick. Ellis didn’t—he ran
forward ; and his breach of the rules, which probably
had nothing to do with the change in the game, has been
recorded aa the invention or discovery of Rugby. Which
is all bosh and tripe. As a matter of fact, the game of
football was brought to Britain by the Romans, who got
it from the Greeks, and both those peoples used to carry
the sphere in the hand or under the arm. Then for
centuries the game was played, or fought, much as
bouth Sydney and Glebe played it on a recent Saturday.
That is to say, the object of the game was to
get the ball between the enemy’s goal-posts by any means
whatever, either by carrying it, kicking it, throwing it,
or punching the other fellow on the nose, or kicking him
in the stomach. Shrove Tuesday was high festival day
of footballers, when both sexes came out and scrapped
over the leather for all they were worth. It was the
game of the people, and the aristocracy spoke of it with
sniffs on their way to the cricket pitch. Edward 11.,
Edward 111., Richard 11., all prohibited the game on
account of it interfering with the practice of archery,
so Lord Roberts had good precedent for his objec-
tions. Janies I. prohibited it because it was more
disastrous to the lives of his subjects than war, and he
got nothing out of it. The rules of modern football date
back to the year 1863, when the Football Association and
Rugby Football Association drafted regulations for the
conduct of the game that differ but slightly, and only in
detail, from those at present in force. Previous to that
there were sometimes as many as 60 men a-side, and the
game resembled a battle.
» * * *
One section of Melbourne’s citizens is fairly
honest, at any rate. At the big Carlton-South Melbourne
football match, on June 4, the turnstiles proved inade-
quate to accommodate the rush, and a few climbed the
barb-wire-topped picket-fence, while many more slipped
through a gap where the fence had given way. Yet a
club committeeman who happened along was called by
the crowd to come and collect their “ sprats,” and,
standing in one spot, gathered in £5 18s. 6d.
• • * «
The Bulletin has been watching Australian
football closely, and has come to the conclusion that it is
about twice as dangerous as Rugby, and several times
more so than British Association. Also, it seems that the
men gfet as many minor knocks and bruises as they do in
Rugby, while the danger of a really bad accident is much
greater. If the players indulged in the malicious rough-
ness that has been prevalent in Rugby circles the last few
weeks (Saturday last excepted) the opportunities for mis-
chief would be infinitely greater. A dozen men springing
wildly into the air to score a “ glorious high mark ” make
a situation that is full of unpleasant possibilities, even if
there is no desire on the part of any one of the dozen to
do the other fellow harm. Theoretically Australian foot-
ball is a better game than Rugby, but practically it will
always be more dangerous.
# # * *
One of the numerous betting club proseoutions
in Melbourne came to what looks like an end recently,
when the Full Court upheld Judge Johnston’s decision up-
holding Magistrate Dwyer’s conviction of two Cohens, two
Myers, and a Solomons for running what was known as
the Metropolitan Club, in the Eastern Arcade. Cohen
and Co. said this was a company duly registered under
the Companies Act, '9O, formed for the customary purpose
of “improving the breed of horses, etc.,” but Mr. Dwyer
held that the tote sheets, telephone, bench, and the sub-
sequent billiard-table, which comprised the company’s
assets, no more improved the noble prad than the plain
brown penny the two-up school tosses, and fined Myers
and co. £2O apiece all round. As three Judges have
agreed that Dwyer was right, and as the telephone was
removed next day, there would appear to be an end of
Solomons and Co.’s Metropolitan Club. But it is not
well to be too sure. Solomon is mostly a sticker.
* * * *
Citizen John Wren has fairly taken pugilism
under his wing. He sits close to one of the corners at all
fights nowadays, and the expected hero of that parti-
cular disturbance, when he enters the ring, always leans
through the ropes and shakes hands with John. The
referee bows low to him. The official announcer an-
nounces in his direction. John’s glory is likely to be
visibly increased by his interest in the game (said interest
figures out at something over 50 per cent.). It is pro-
posed indeed that a new set of rules should be framed for
John’s pets to fight under—Marquis of Oollingwood rules
they will probably be called. Murphy, after his fight
with Squires, alleged that these rules were already in
existence, and that the big fight in the Exhibition was
conducted under them. If that is so, they are a good,
hustling set of rules and ought to keep Philadelphia Jack
O’Brien warm for a brief space.
* * * *
It was understood that the real reason for Jim
Jeffries’ retirement from the boxing championship of the
world was that rheumatism of the hands caused his blows
to hurt himself more than the other fellow. Apparently
Jeffries is recovering, at least temporarily, as he recently
appeared in the roped ring at an entertainment at Los
Angeles in aid of the victims of the recent ’Frisco ’quake.
* * • •
Is there any other academy in the world which
possesses such a record for producing first-class pugs as
St. Francis’Church school, Elizabeth-street, Melbourne?
St. Francis taught the young idea how to stousli to such
effect that it numbers on its past rolls the names of Dan
Creedon, Tut Ryan, Tom Tracey, Artie Tully, Micko
Walsh, Jack Willis, and Jack M’Gowan, besides a host
of dimmer lights with local fame. In Melbourne it is
only necessary for a man to say he used to go to St.
Francis’ for everybody about to grow suddenly civil.
* ■* * *
“ A. Zelwood ” : The elusive Ted Nelson, who
came within a shade of winning the middle-weight cham-
pionship of Australia, was mining a few years ago at
North Lyell. In the hut where he camped, his spare
time was mostly occupied in sparring before a looking-
glass. These “ acts of reflection ” afforded much amuse-
ment to his mates. While admitting his quickness, one
and all thought him as harmless as a sunbeam, till, just
to take him down a peg, they induced him to have a spar
with a pro. then showing at Lyell. To everybody’s sur-
prise, Nelson (name, by the way, is Grogan) got all over
his man. After this, Nelson came out of his shell some.
Every Sunday afternoon he would stroll down to where
they head ’em, looking for a fight. Soon lie would single
out a big fellow with a good deal to say, and in the soft
voioe that always provokes wrath, ask him to moderate
his language and not profane the holy Sabbath. Of
course a fight was the result. One round would Nelson
spend looking for the other’s weak points, and, having
located them, the second round generally found the big
fellow dead to the world. Since then but his fights
can speak for themselves.
* * * «
“S.S.”: Bulletin paragraphers trying to ex-
plain the knock-out sensation seem to me to be allowing
their imagination free play. The knock-out is simpiy a
drop into unconsciousness, and whether you take the
brain jar from the jaw-bone, or get it more directly on
the temple, or abaft the ear, the sensation, or absence of
sensation, is identical. You know no feelings till you are
no longer “out,” and the recovery is an awakening
accompanied by such sensations as the wallop would have
set up had you stayed in instead of passing out. It very
often happens that the outed man doesn’t know he has
been outed, and wants to take up the thread of the battle
where he left off. When he is informed that his opponent
is now a mile off with the butt end of the “ gate ” in his
Gladstone bag, the astonishment of the resuscitated
young pug is quite touching. All those fine dramatic
sensations The Bulletin par.-builders tell of must
happen to the more elderly pugs who go out as a matter
of choice.
* • « •
“S.” : Dan Creedon versus Bill Smith, at Mel-
bourne Cvclorama, strikingly illustrated the suddenness
with which the athlete goes to bits after reaching years
of discretion and a little over. The Dan Creedon who
put up a long, worrying battle with champion Billy
M’Oall in Sydney 18 months or so back, and the Daniel
who was beaten all to bits by Bill Smith in Melbourne
were two very different men, and yet Creedon claims
only 38 years. Smith, who is not in the same street with
M‘Call as a pug., simply ate Creedon up, and when Dan
recognised the hopelessness of his effort to get back
again into the game, and requested his seconds to float
the washing, it signified permanent retirement. Cree-
don’s failure was the failure of an old man to whom
training is no good, and out of whom all the sap had
evaporated.

A muscular young parson down Richmond
(Vic.) way is reckoned to be the Rev. Bill Squires of the
sin-shifting profession. He was prominent in a recent
boxing tournament run by the athletic club connected
with his church. After slogging out most of the Bible
class and other good young men (including a prominent
League footballer), he met the Christian Endeavor secre-
tary in the final bout, but, after a bitter fight, threw up
the sponge in the tenth round.

The Five Miles Novice was run by the Mel-
bourne Harriers’ Cluo at Oakleigh on Saturday week.
An entry of 120 is a record for this cross-country event,
and equals several Ten-Mile State Championship Helds.
W. V. Aitken (Coburg Harriers) arrived first by some 60
yards, covering the distance in dOmin. 204*5sec.— a fair run
seeing there was a head wind for half the distance* The
race, however, is run under sealed handicap conditions,
and resulted in a win for A. D. Mackie, Carlton, with a
time allowance of lmin. 30sec. George Blake ana H. A.
Wheatlev, the recent Olympians, both finished ahead in the
Five Miles Novice during preceding .yearp. Amateur
athletics are seemingly on the up grade around Mel-
bourne, for there are some 300 runners in paper-chases
weekly. # #
The Dunlop Motor Reliability Test is, as anti-
cipated, to be held this year over Victorian roads. The
first day's run will be from Melbourne to Hamilton. The
second day they will return to Melbourne by another
route. The third day they run out to Tallarook and back.
Fourth day will find them journeying to Staweil, and on
tiie fifth, like the general in the poem, they will go back
again. The sixth and last day will be occupied whizzing
out to Marysville and back. As usual, all precautions
will be taken to guard against accident by indicating
dangerous turns and hills, and observers will report
drivers who race through towns. No less than five
motorists have a leg in for the Dunlop Cup, and as some
of them are in a position to win it outright this season,
there is sure to be a great struggle. A motor cycle test
will be held over the same roads simultaneously if a suffi-
cient entry is forthcoming. Besides the Dunlop Cup,
there are medals and other valuable trophies on the prize
list.

“ Oracle In issue 24/5/’OG The Bulletin lias
an article on the motor-car business in England and
France. It is not as well known as it might bo that cars
are made in Australia ; that is, about one in every 250
cars on the road is of Australian make. Here at
Armadale (Melb.) we have a man who has been making
reliable steamers for, as near as I can remember, the last
ten years. The “Tarrant” petrol car, of wholly Aus-
tralian construction, is acknowledged by experts to be as
good as any petrol car at present in the country, “ but,”
I was told resignedly by one connected with the firm,
“ the public don’t think so.” The makers get a living by
selling French and English cars. Give us a 60 horse-
power tariff, and Australia will be able to make
all the cars it wants for Its own use, anyway, and
perhaps a few over.
* * » *
The Trumper tour did Queensland cricket
some good. It has shattered the common belief that
Brisbane cricket (which is very poor cricket) is Queens-
land cricket. The country teams have made a better
showing than the metropolis. One country team, Rock-
hampton, made 175, and put Trumper and Go. out in the
first innings for 168. But in the second innings Truniper’s
men made an exhibition of Rockhampton. Another team,
North Queensland, gave the visitors quite a fright by
nearly beating them, and Hopkins and Redgrave were
forced to stonewall in order to make a drawn game.
* * * *
“ Phlipp ” : Visited several country shows this
year, and one of the principal items on the programme
was what is called a tandem race. This is an innovation
which, although a few years old in the country, has not
yet travelled to Sydney. It is a riding and driving race
combined. The horse is driven by means of long reins,
and the driver is on another horse behind. It calls for
good horsemanship, and there is the necessary element of
danger. The foremost horse must be kept travelling at
exactly the same rate of speed as the other ; if it gets too
much pace on it is liable to drag the driver from his
saddle. Also, the leader is always liable to swerve round,
which, again, would make things unpleasant. The pace
some of the riders get up is remarkable, and their con-
tinual shouting to the leaders inspires the required en-
thusiasm and thrill. When introduced into the Sydney
Show this item will easily outrival the water and high
jump for interest.
