The Hunters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THEY had stowed the clattering tops’ls,
And sent the top gear down,
For the sea rolled green and sickly,
With foam at each roller’s crown,
When faintly down to the south’ard,
Where the clouds hung red and low,
The cry of the night-waves echoed,
And a wind began to blow.
It bore down on the schooner,
And buried her white lee-rail,
But she spun to her heavy rudder,
And flew from the angry gale ;
While, nearer, a fearful wolf-cry,
The van of the Hunters came—
Thundering waves of the darkness
Their tossing crests aflame—
Leaping high in their clamor,
Bellowing as they raced.
And one green galloping Hunter
The best of the pack outpaced.
The little ship was their quarry.
As the speedy leverets do,
She prayed that her heels would save her
From the jaws of the ravenous crew.
So over the tumbling water
She staggered and leaped and swung,
Tossing her prow to the heavens
When they would have seized and clung—
Swerving over to starboard,
Shuddering over to port,
To distance the cruel Hunters
Who harried her for their sport.
Then sudden the great green Hunter
Was racing with gleaming fangs
Under the vessel’s counter,
Where the smooth stern overhangs—
Racing with reckless violence,
Panting with grim intent,
But she fled with the speed of terror,
And the Hunter fell back, spent.
While all the other pursuers,
Thinking to see her fall,
Lifted their heads in the darkness
In one long hunting call.
One leaped with a howl on her transom
And snapped her rail away ;
Another sprang at her life-boat,
And tossed it over in play.
Then the angry, green-lipped Hunter,
Shouting to stand aside,
Tore in her solid bulwarks
A great hole, gaping wide,
Sending her forward faster
Yet following faster too
Where the blaze of high Pencarrow
Brighter and brighter grew.
The stars looked down through the storm-wrack
And cried to the rushing wind,
“ Blow hard, blow hard ! The harbor
To the little ships is kind.
Drive her so that the Hunters
Are left in her wake out-paced.”
And the swift wind howled in its hurry
And the little schooner raced.
But the mighty Hunter summoned
His eager pack to his aid
And they raced along with the schooner,
Just raced —and she was afraid.
Until, when the blazing beacon
Threw light on the seas and spars,
The great green Hunter lifted
His head to the trembling stars.
And howled ; and the smallest Hunter
Howled too in the lust for blood.
They raged till the decks of the schooner
Were all afoam and aflood.
Round and about they tossed her,
Snapping her tangled gear.
They tugged at her heavy rudder
Till scarcely her crew could steer.
The stars laughed now, for the harbor
Was close ahead o’er the bar ;
Then wept, for the big green Hunter
Dragged over her side a spar.
But the waves that stand at the entrance —
Guards of the inner seas—
Charged down on the fierce pursuers
That sprang at the schooner’s trees;
The pack called loud defiance —
“ The quarry is ours,” they cried,
And led by the galloping Hunter
They thrashed at the schooner’s side.
Then the Watchmen fell upon them,
While the vessel flew beyond
In where the quiet waters
Lay like a glassy pond.
The chase fell back from the Watchmen,
Fighting in foam and spray—
All save the great green Hunter,
Who thrust them out of his way;
Blown and scarred with battle,
He leaped through their serried ranks;
But the Watchmen bore him under
As he charged at the schooner’s flanks.
As they stow the clattering tops’ls
And send the topgear down,
When the seas roll green and glassy,
The shouted orders drown
In a long-d/r awn, terrible wolf-cry
And the boldest there is dumb,
When, the galloping waves of the darkness,
The harrying Hunters come.
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.
The Motor Boat of Black
and Red.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS entering the American bar in my
usual impetuous manner when the swing
, . _ , . . .
door suddenly new outwards before the
rush of someone who was coming out even more
emphatically than I was going in, and I was
knocked into the middle of the pavement.
Shaping curses with both lips, I was struggling
to my feet when a big burly man in a knicker-
bocker suit offered a helping hand, and a faintly-
familiar voice said :
“Gosh, old man, I believe I upset your apple
cart. Let’s go in and have a wet.”
There was no mistaking him. The big, clean-
shaven face, merry blue eyes and bronzed com-
plexion were all those of Squatter Howard, now
just past 40, showing signs of a paunch, but youth-
ful as ever in spirit. He didn’t know me from a
crow. I spoke severely.
“ I see you are as bad as ever. First you knock
a man into the middle of next week, cause him to
wreck his immortal soul with language, and then
proceed to demoralise him with drink. But as my
inside machinery does feel strained, a little lubri-
cation might possibly make it work easier.”
The big burly man gripped me by the shoulder,
held me off at arm’s length, and stared at me. A
look of recognition spread over his countenance,
and with a wild yell he lugged me into the bar.
“ Sixteen champagne cocktails, Tom. Well,
well; just fancy meeting you in this place of bad
beer and worse whisky. Well, I’m damned!—
this is real joy. Hurry up with those cocktails,
you mongrel ! ”
We spent most of the afternoon talking over
old times and the intervening years, and when it
suddenly chimed seven we realised that the after-
noon had gone. It was no good going back to
work, so I wandered off with the newly-recovered
squatter to have dinner in a French caf&
It was a nice dinner, but the table wines demo-
ralised us, and Howard began to talk of some of
the wilder deeds of the past. We were just drain-
ing the liqueur glasses when he remarked that
he had that morning taken delivery of a new
motor boat, a regular beauty.
“We’ll take her out for a spin presently, ” I
said.
“Anoble idea. Gosh, we’ll have some fun!
We’ll make the police boat chase us—give ’em a
deuce of a run—and I’ll lay 10 to 4 they don’t
catch us. ”
“Done!” I yelled, and we sprang up and
rushed out of the cafe at such a speed that it was
only when we slowed down to board a cab that
the waiters managed to lay hold of us.
“What the Sheol do you want, anyhow?”
snorted the indignant squatter as a big greasy
man in a white uniform grabbed him by the arm
and hung on, a mass of dead weight, like a prize
pork carcase.
It was the head waiter, and he placed both
hands over his heart, which was thumping loudly,
and strove to keep it in its own territory.
“ Zee beel, zare—you have forgot ze beel oh,
Lord ! ”
“Good heavens !” gasped the squatter, hunt-
ing in his pockets for the money. “ Very good
thing you caught us, as we might not have come
back.’
The waiter had by this time got control of his
heart, but had forgotten his assumed nationality
in the struggle. He remarked, in flowing Eng-
lish :
“No, they don’t very often come back when
they leave like you did and owe a bill as long as
my leg. My oath, no ! ”
The squatter eyed him suspiciously as he handed
over the money.
“ That man is a fraud,” he said, very decisively,
as he took his seat in the cab, and we started off
to where the boat was moored.
And she was a beauty. Thirty-six feet long.
Whale-back bow which sloped up to the roof.
Side screens which worked on the Venetian blind
principle and were weather-proof. Seats all
covered with red leather. The steering wheel
was of the large variety, on a long shaft which
sloped up to a nicely-upholstered swivel chair
that gave the steersman an uninterrupted view in
every direction. A four-cylinder 40 h.p. gasoline
engine supplied the motive power, and the craft
was painted pearl grey and turquoise blue below
the waterline.
In two minutes we had the engine running, and
were moving out of Rushcutters towards Watson’s
Bay, looking for the police launch. Like the land
variety, the water police were not about when we
wanted to give them a friendly trial of speed. So
we zig-zagged across the fairway, disregarded the
rule of the road, and broke all the speed regula-
tions of the Harbor Trust.
The police launch came with a rush just as we
rounded the corner of an island. Black smoke
was pouring from its funnel, and we saw that the
object was to crowd us up on the shore. Two
men armed with boat-hooks were standing ready
to hook us. We turned on full speed, and the
Aerolite went across the bows of the police launch
with a bound. We hailed the clean-shaven man
on the bridge as “ Whiskers,” and swung round
for the Pile-light.
The moon suddenly climbed over the hills at
the back of Rose Bay. As we went leaping
through the water, we saw the police boat’s big
black smoke cloud spread across the water, saw
its lights zigzag over towards Middle Harbor, and
knew that the force had lost us in the moonlit
haze.
We rushed on, under the frowning North
Head and slowing down as we glided into a
slight swell, burst suddenly into a moonlit
ocean. Behind us the shore line rose like
a silhouette in Indian ink. Across gleaming
leagues the moon made a track of glory to where
an opalescent archipelago floated partly in the
sky and partly in the sea. Like Winken and
Blinken and Nod we turned our boat into that
golden pathway and reeled along with a shower
of gems falling on our whaleback bow. On, and
on, and on, we fled towards the opalescent archi-
pelago. The charm and witchery of it fell upon
us, and we spoke no word, but sat swaying with
the motion of the boat till I saw among the
islands one larger than the rest. On its beach of
seed pearls lovely girls stood holding glorious
mixed drinks with plenty of soda and ice, under
palms which waved by electricity.
* * * *
A splash in my face awakened me. I sat up
with a curse and said I wanted hot tea and not
cold water. I was answered with a grunt, and
opening my eyes, thought I must be dead and
gone to -. A dull gray sky, the stern of a
boat, and beyond, a rolling ocean of gray-green
water. I tried to think it out, and saw a big
burly man lying under the seat to the left. Then
I remembered.
“ Here, wake up ! This is beyond a joke,” I
gasped, trying to rise to my feet. But just then
the ocean dropped from beneath the boat, and I
fell of a heap, As we struck the floor of the
ocean I hauled myself up. The rebound helped
me, and when I stood upright we were on the top
of an oceanic buckjumper, and I stared round
and found that we were quite out of sight of
land.
Howard got on his knees. His tongue was

light She lingered a moment, murmured some-
thing about eternal gratitude, and then vanished
in the gloom. We got out of the cove and streaked
away for Port Macquarie, where we left the
launch in charge of a varlet while we drove round
to the French cafe for something substantial in
the shape of a supper.
* * * *
On Saturday nights a pompous man dines at that
French cafe in all the glory of a frock coat. The
sheen of his tall hat falls on the dainty head-gear
of a dark-eyed woman who sits beside him, laugh-
ing and talking. The sound of her voice brings with
it the echo of angry waves, and the gurgling noise
that rose from the water as the motor boat of
black and red sank into the depths ; and somehow,
in spite of all her gaiety, I think her heart is
drifting in the Tasman Sea with the body of her
lost lover.
C. A. JEFFBIKS.
Mother-love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A month last night my baby died,
At first I only sat and cried,
Till Parson came to me and said,
“You must not think your baby dead.
“ Think of him up in Heaven,” said he,
“ Where God and all the angels be.
Think of him in Christ’s arms,” lie said,
“ Poor mother, and be comforted.
So, as I work through the long day,
My thoughts go flying far away,
I follow till I almost see
The way my baby went from me.
Up through the clear warm air he goes,
So round and pink without his clothes,
And when he reaches Pleaven’s gate
They do not make my baby wait.
For all the angels walking near
Cry out, “ Oh, see the lovely dear !
Arid swing the broad leaves wide, and run
To carry in my little one.
Set in a ring of merry talk—
He is too little yet to walk—
They bear him, smiling, small and bold,
Up the steep street of shining gold.
To where, above the dazzling height,
Set on a throne so great and white,
God and His dear Son always rest,
With crowds of children round i.hem prest.
God holds all little children dear,
He loves to draw them close and near.
But my wee boy, so sweet and small,
Is drawn the closest of them all.
For Christ holds out a pierced hand;
My baby kicks, and tries to stand,
Stretches to reach Him, snuggles warm,
Deep in the hollow of His arm.
And there he rests and goes to sleep,
So cosy, I forget to weep, . .
Watching each warm and dimpled limb,
And all the sweet content of him.
But when he wakes —ah, Christ, be kind .
He turns and seeks, and cannot find,
Pressing his little downy head
Just where the Saviour’s side is red.
His lips a-trembling I can see—
And then the heart-break catches me—
His little trembling lip—for oh
My boy does want his mother so !
QUEENSLAND.
A.W.

manufacture all of the articles (except the
watch) in our own works, and they carry but
one profit#
The watches are purchased from the
manufacturer in very large numbers for cash.
You get the share that usually goes to the
middlemen. That is your gain.
If you bought these goods at any store in
Australia you would probably have to pay
pretty well double the price for them.
